[[null|]][[null|]]To a gentleman and great champion, Arthur Ashe, who fought for human dignity and the worth of the individual[[null|]]



# [[null|]][[null|]]Introduction 引言

# Winning Ugly in the 21st Century: The More Things Change, the More We Stay the Same

>21世纪的“丑胜”：变化越多，我们越不变

Let’s test your tennis memory with this question: Who were the #1 players in the world when Winning Ugly was published in 1992? If you said Monica Seles and Jim Courier, you aced it. Plenty has changed since then, but plenty has stayed the same. I think that’s why so many tennis players still come up and tell me how Winning Ugly helps their game today, many years after it was introduced to an unsuspecting tennis world.

>让我们用一个问题来测试一下你的网球记忆：《赢得难看》（Winning Ugly）于1992年出版时，当时世界排名第一的选手是谁？如果你回答是莫妮卡·塞莱斯（Monica Seles）和吉姆·柯尔（Jim Courier），那你答对了。从那时起，很多事情已经发生了变化，但也有很多东西始终未变。我想这就是为什么这么多年过去后，仍然有很多网球选手会来找我，告诉我这本书至今仍然对他们打球有帮助，尤其是在这本书最初问世时，当时网球界对它还一无所知的情况下。

Nothing brings a warm smile to my face and does my heart more good than when a club player or pro walks up to me at a tournament with a copy of Winning Ugly for me to sign. Great! To me, that’s the ultimate compliment. And that’s why I wouldn’t change a word in the original edition; it still will have a powerful effect on your game. That’s my promise to you.

>没有什么事情能比一位业余球手或职业选手在比赛中走过来，递给我一本《以丑为胜》让我签名时，更让我感到开心和满足了。太棒了！对我来说，这便是最高级的赞美。因此，我不会对原版的任何一句话做改动；它依旧会对你的球技产生深远的影响。这是我对你做出的承诺。

I would, however, add some ideas and that’s what this brand new and comprehensive introduction is for, to bring Winning Ugly up to speed in the twenty-first century.

>不过，我还要补充一些想法，而这本全新而全面的引言正是为此而作，旨在将《赢得很丑》更新到二十一世纪。

In my opinion, the three biggest changes in tennis since Winning Ugly was written are technology, court surfaces, and athleticism. (Maybe I should add a fourth: instant replay—the shot spot. It’s great for the game and terrific for the fans because it satisfies our curiosity with conclusive evidence on those close calls.)

>在我看来，自《赢得难看》一书问世以来，网球运动的三大变化是技术、球场表面和运动员的身体素质。（也许我应该再加上第四点：即时回放——球落点。这对比赛和球迷都非常有益，因为它用确凿的证据满足了我们对关键判罚的好奇心。）

1) Technology: Those high-tech poly strings and light rackets have transformed shots by allowing pros and lots of club players to hit [[null|]]so much harder and create gigantic spin and angles that let them throw grenades for ground strokes and bombs for serves. (Rafael Nadal’s ball spins up to 5,000 revolutions per minute—more than twice as fast as Pete Sampras or Andre Agassi in their prime.) The ball is hit so hard with so much spin now that it’s practically deformed as it rockets over the net toward an opponent.

>1) 技术：这些高科技的聚酯弦和轻量球拍已经改变了击球方式，使得职业选手和大量业余球员能够以[[null|]] 更大的力量击球，创造出巨大的旋转和角度，让他们能够像投掷手榴弹一样打出平击球，像发射炸弹一样发出发球。 （拉斐尔·纳达尔的球旋转速度可达每分钟5000转——比皮特·桑普拉斯或安德烈·阿加西巅峰时期快两倍以上。）如今球被击打的力量和旋转如此之大，以至于球在飞越球网奔向对手的过程中几乎已经变形。

2) Court surfaces: the surfaces of courts worldwide are more alike than ever before—grass is slower; clay is faster; hard courts produce more bounce. All of this makes for much better points—more interesting for fans to watch, and more fun for television analysts like me to talk about.

>2）球场表面：全球范围内的球场表面越来越相似了——草地球速较慢；红土球速较快；硬地则产生更多的反弹。这一切让比赛更加精彩——对观众来说更具观赏性，也让我这样的电视解说员有更多有趣的点可以谈论。

3) Finally, (and for me, the biggest change), the athleticism and resulting movement and muscularity of twenty-first-century tour players are more epic than ever.

>3）最后（对我而言，这是最大的改变），现代巡回赛选手的体能和由此产生的动作与肌肉线条比以往任何时候都更加震撼人心。

>[!note]- 译者注：
 此处“epic”意为“史诗般的”或“令人震撼的”，用“震撼人心”表达其壮丽与震撼之感。

“Movement” means great footwork coupled with quick speed, balance, dexterity, and flexibility both on offense and defense for as long as it takes to finish the job. It comes from advancements in conditioning and strength, among other things. Supreme fitness is a given today if you want to keep up and a top-end fitness trainer is as prized on tour as a good coach. When you combine that with the new technology you have today’s super tennis played by super players.

>“移动”意味着出色的步法配合快速的速度、平衡、灵巧和灵活性，无论是在进攻还是防守中，都要持续到完成任务为止。这种能力来源于体能和力量训练等方面的进步。如今，顶级的体能是保持竞争力的必要条件，而一流的体能教练在巡回赛中也如同优秀的教练一样备受青睐。当你将这一切与当今的新技术相结合，就造就了如今由顶尖球员演绎的超级网球。

For example, Novak Djokovic—the Djoker—can run down five corners back and forth—boom, boom, boom, boom, boom—and then take control of the point, hit a winner, and finish up by doing the splits. He, Rafa, and others can do this for four hours. They have the amazing ability of the 20-Second Recovery. They make it look easy and it absolutely amazes me.

>例如，诺瓦克·德约科维奇——人们称他为“德约”——可以来回跑动五次角落，砰！砰！砰！砰！砰！然后掌控局面，打出制胜球，最后还来个劈叉动作结束。他、拉斐尔（纳达尔）以及其他顶尖选手可以连续做四个小时这样的动作。他们拥有令人惊叹的“20秒恢复能力”。他们让这一切看起来轻而易举，这让我感到无比震撼。

I’m seeing the kind of points played now that just weren’t around twenty years ago. It’s like watching a video game (or as I’ve called in on ESPN2, “watching Wii tennis”) and it has produced players I call “all-arounders” who can play great from anywhere on the court. The resulting tennis is unbelievable to watch and has led to a new golden age for the sport.

>我现在看到的比赛节奏和打法，是二十年前完全看不到的。就像是在看一款视频游戏（或者像我曾在ESPN2上形容的那样，“看 Wii 网球”），这种打法造就了我称之为“全能型选手”的球员，他们可以在球场的任何位置都打出精彩表现。这种网球观赏性极强，让人叹为观止，也标志着这项运动迎来了一个新的黄金时代。

Where will the game be twenty years from now? Two hundred [[null|]]mile-per-hour serves? It can happen. And even more amazing is that players will be returning those serves.

>二十年后，这场比赛会变成什么样？两百英里每小时的发球？这并非不可能。更令人惊讶的是，球员们还将成功回击这些发球。

The face of twenty-first century tennis so far is Roger Federer. Fed seized #1 worldwide in 2004 and held it until 2008, when Nadal grabbed it for a year, then Fed again, then Nadal again—and then the Djoker took over. But Fed’s mark—seventeen Slams (so far), the most ever in men’s tennis—is the biggest. He personally raised the bar of performance so high that he forced others to follow if they wanted to compete. Rafa and the Djoker did. Others will.

>21世纪网球的面孔至今仍是罗杰·费德勒。费德勒在2004年夺得世界第一，并一直保持到2008年，随后纳达尔夺走头衔一年，之后费德勒再次登顶，纳达尔再度超越，接着是德约科维奇接手。但费德勒的成就——至今共获得17个大满贯（目前），这是男子网球史上最多的——才是最耀眼的。他个人将表现的标杆抬得如此之高，以至于其他人若想竞争，就必须随之而上。纳达尔和德约科维奇做到了，其他人也将如此。

On the men’s side, I’ve never seen a stronger top four than Fed, Rafa, the Djoker, and Andy Murray. I doubt we’ll see a stronger #1, #2, #3, and #4 for a long time—maybe ever.

>在男子组方面，我从未见过比德约科维奇、纳达尔、穆雷和费德勒这四位更强大的前四名选手。我怀疑在很长一段时间——也许永远——都不会再出现如此强劲的排名前三的选手了。

For the women, Serena is it, although Venus is pretty amazing too. Serena and Venus are two of the best things that happened to women’s tennis in the twenty-first century because they shook things up every which way. (Nevertheless, Steffi Graf in her prime would challenge either of the Williams sisters because she was so physical and had those powerful groundies, including her legendary slice backhand—an executioner’s shot. Very tough mentally too, just like Venus and Serena.)

>对女子网球来说，塞雷娜（Serena）是无可争议的王者，尽管维纳斯（Venus）也相当了不起。塞雷娜和维纳斯是二十一世纪女子网球最伟大的两位人物，因为她们从各个方面彻底改变了这项运动。

>[!note]- 译者注：
此处“shook things up every which way”意为“从各个方面彻底改变”，根据上下文意译。 (不过，巅峰时期的施特菲·格拉夫（Steffi Graf）仍然能与威廉姆斯姐妹中的任何一位抗衡，因为她身体素质出色，正手击球力量强大，尤其是她那传奇般的切削反手——这记球如同刽子手般致命。在心理层面同样极具挑战性，就像维纳斯和塞雷娜一样。)

Old-time tennis fans like to say the 1980s produced the greatest cast of characters and performers in tennis—McEnroe, Connors, Becker, Evert, Navratilova, Lendl, Borg, and others. But today’s lineup is more exciting because of the big changes I mentioned and the athletes who are incorporating those changes into their game.

>老一辈的网球迷们总爱说，上世纪80年代是网球史上角色最鲜明、表现最精彩的年代——麦肯罗、康纳斯、贝克尔、埃弗特、纳芙拉蒂洛娃、伦德尔、博格，等等。但如今的球员阵容更加令人激动，这不仅是因为我之前提到的那些巨大变化，还因为现在的运动员们正将这些变化融入到自己的比赛中。

>[!note]- 译者注：
此处“cast of characters”意为“角色群像”或“人物群像”，指当时网坛上个性鲜明、风格各异的球员们。

The more things change for the pros, however, the more they seem to stay the same for club players—and these days you could almost call me a club player.

>然而，对职业选手来说，变化越多，他们似乎越回到原来的状态——如今我几乎可以称自己为一名业余选手。

>[!note]- 译者注：
此处“club player”既可译为“业余选手”，也可理解为“俱乐部选手”，根据上下文语境，选择更贴近日常表达的“业余选手”以增强可读性。

While Winning Ugly was being written Steve Jamison kept reminding me that my focus was exclusively on what club players can learn from the pros when it came to the mental part of tennis. That basic truth hasn’t changed: You can improve your tennis game fastest and the most if you improve the way you think; if you work as hard on your mind as you do on your strokes.

>在《赢得很丑》这本书写作过程中，史蒂夫·贾米森一直提醒我，我的关注点始终只集中在俱乐部球员可以从职业选手身上学到的网球心理层面的内容上。这个基本事实至今未变：如果你想要最快、最有效地提升自己的网球水平，那就必须提升你的思维方式；你要像打磨球技一样努力地打磨自己的心理素质。

However, the new cast of superstars dominating the tennis scene in [[null|]]the twenty-first century provide additional mental-management ideas to add to your arsenal of weapons. Their approach to the brain-game will help you beat players who are beating you—who may have better strokes but lack better thinking.

>然而，二十一世纪崭露头角的这些超级明星们，为你的心理战术武器库提供了更多值得借鉴的思路。他们对待“脑力博弈”的方式，将帮助你战胜那些击败你的选手——他们或许拥有更出色的击球技术，但缺乏更出色的心理策略。

>[!note]- 译者注：
此处“brain-game”意指“心理博弈”或“心智较量”，在网球比赛中强调心理层面的对抗。

First up: Roger Federer. Have you noticed that during matches at the French Open, Fed’s socks don’t get dirty—on clay! And the guy barely breaks a sweat. How is this possible? Especially on the red dirt—it blows me away every time. Everybody else gets covered in dirt and sweats plenty there. Why not Federer? Well, in part it’s because he’s just so perfect; his movement is absolutely flawless, elegant, effortless, and efficient. And he has the ability to dictate points with as good a forehand as tennis has ever produced. (Maybe Nadal is the only one at that level.)

>首先登场的是罗杰·费德勒。你有没有注意到，在法网比赛中，费德勒的袜子几乎不会沾上泥巴——是在红土场上！而且他几乎连汗都很少。这是怎么做到的？特别是在红土场，这每次都让我感到不可思议。其他人都会被泥土和汗水弄得满身大汗，但费德勒却不是这样。部分原因在于他实在太完美了，他的移动完全无懈可击，优雅、轻松、高效。他拥有网球史上最出色的正手击球能力，能够掌控局面。（也许只有纳达尔能与他相提并论。）

But here’s what we can learn from Fed: Although he doesn’t have the biggest serve on tour, he does have the biggest “hold serve” out there; over 90 percent of the time Roger Federer holds serve without having the huge serve. How? In part it’s because he can hit his spots with unbelievable accuracy and consistency. Some players can hit a dime; Fed can hit a dot. A big, powerful serve is great, but believe me, a returner can figure out how to deal with your power a lot easier than dealing with your improved placement. In fact, here’s a little tip: If your opponent is consistently hitting winners with a big serve, the solution is simple: Just take one step back from where you had been standing to receive the serve. You’ll like the results.

>但我们可以从费德勒身上学到一些东西：虽然他的发球并不是巡回赛中最大的，但他却拥有最出色的“保发”能力；超过90%的时间里，费德勒都能成功保发，而并不依赖那颗大威力的发球。这是为什么呢？部分原因在于他能够以令人难以置信的准确性和稳定性击中指定位置。有些球员能击出“分币”般的发球，而费德勒则能击出“点”般的精准发球。强大的发球固然很好，但相信我，对手要应对你的力量会比应对你更精准的落点要容易得多。事实上，这里有个小技巧：如果你的对手总是用大威力发球打出制胜分，那么解决办法很简单：只需后退一步站位接发球。你一定会喜欢结果的。

The lesson? Work on holding your serve and to do that practice placement first and foremost; move your serve around a little bit. You don’t have to be Roger Federer to get some results by moving your serve around. Keep your opponent guessing. And remember, if you’re going to work on one thing, practice your serve! It may be the most important shot in this game.

>教训是什么？要专注于保住自己的发球局，而要做到这一点，首先要练习发球落点。你可以稍微变换一下发球的位置。你不需要像罗杰·费德勒那样才能通过变换发球位置取得效果。让对手难以捉摸。记住，如果你要专注提升某一项技术，那就练习你的发球吧！这可能是这项运动中最重要的一拍。

I think Rafa’s comfort zone is when he feels that he’s not good enough and has to get better and better to stay competitive. So he’s always tinkering with some part of his already fantastic game, never satisfied and always looking to improve. Improve, improve, improve! He doesn’t like to have somebody tell him he’s the greatest because he absolutely does not want to buy into that way of thinking.

>我认为纳达尔的舒适区是当他觉得自己还不够好，必须不断变得更好，才能保持竞争力的时候。因此，他总是对自身已经非常出色的技术进行一些调整，从不满足，一直在寻求进步。进步、进步、进步！他不喜欢有人告诉他他是最伟大的，因为他完全不想接受这种思维方式。

Rafa wants to feel like he’s not the best, that he has to get better and better, and so he’s always working on some part of his great game. When [[null|]]I talk to him at tournaments he’s always fiddling with something—the grip, toss, or something else. The passion you see in Rafa on the court in the fifth set of the finals of a Grand Slam is the same passion he brings off the court to improving his level of execution—to improving his already terrific game. Rafa Nadal gives his game a million percent during matches, during practice, probably during breakfast too. He’s total in his commitment.

>拉斐尔·纳达尔希望感觉自己不是最好的，他必须不断变得更好、更强，因此他总是不断地打磨自己伟大的网球技艺。每当我在比赛中见到他，他总是手忙脚乱地调整着什么——握拍、抛球，或者别的什么。你在大满贯决赛第五盘看到的拉斐尔在球场上的激情，和他离场后不断追求提升自己技术水平、精益求精的执着是一样的。拉斐尔·纳达尔在比赛中、训练中，甚至可能在早餐时都在全力以赴，投入百分之百的精力。他的投入是百分之百的专注与奉献。

The lesson? Never quit trying to get better, to raise the mechanics and the thinking with your own game. If one of the greatest players in the history of tennis can feel like his game is never good enough, so can you. And so can I. Here’s my own example.

>教训是什么呢？永远不要停止努力变得更好，不断提升自己的技术和思维方式。如果你连网球史上最伟大的球员之一都觉得自己的水平还不够好，那你我也一样。这是我的亲身经历。

When I was on tour I used to drive players crazy by chasing down everything, keeping the point alive, hanging in there. I waged a war of attrition. Nowadays? The legs and lungs aren’t what they were twenty years ago, and I’ve had to be honest with myself and admit it. So I looked to tinker with my mechanics like Rafa because I needed to end points sooner in the seniors matches that I play. To do that, I needed to hit bigger from the middle of the court and hope the ball either didn’t come back or came back weak so I could reduce the running I had to do.

>我以前在巡回赛期间总是让球员们抓狂，我总是在场上穷追不舍，努力让每一分延续下去，死守到底。我打的是持久战。但现在呢？我的腿和肺活量已经不如二十年前了，我不得不诚实地面对这个现实。因此，我像纳达尔那样开始调整自己的技术动作，因为我需要在参加的老年人比赛中更快结束每一分。要做到这一点，我必须从球场中央击出更大力量的球，希望对方回球要么不回来，要么回得很弱，这样我就能减少奔跑的距离。

When I started to tinker with my stroke I was driving my wife, Mrs. G., crazy because I was walking around the house all the time with a tennis racket in my hand, practicing hitting a flatter forehand and swinging at an imaginary ball.

>当我开始对我的击球动作进行一些调整时，我妻子格太太（Mrs. G.）被我弄得快要发疯了。我总是拿着网球拍在家里走来走去，练习击出更扁平的正手球，还对空气挥拍，仿佛真的有球在那儿。

I am proud to say I never broke a lamp and also managed to develop a harder, flatter, more forceful forehand. The Aussies call it putting in the “hard yards,” and I did my version of hard yards in our living room, dining room, and kitchen. Then out to the court.

>我可以自豪地说，我从未打破过一个灯泡，同时还成功地练出了一个更硬、更扁平、更具冲击力的正手击球。澳大利亚人称之为“硬练”，而我则在客厅、餐厅和厨房里完成了自己的“硬练”。然后，我便走向球场。

Be like Rafa—dissatisfied with your game! Become a good, hardworking tinkerer always looking to improve and you will become a better player who wins more often.

>要像纳达尔一样——对自己的球技感到不满！成为一个认真努力、不断改进的“修补匠”，你将逐渐成为一名技术更出色的球员，赢得更多比赛。

>[!note]- “tinkerer”翻译为“修补匠”既保留了原意，又符合中文表达习惯，形象地传达了不断改进、精益求精的含义。

And speaking of getting better, Novak Djokovic offers an amazing lesson for all of us when it comes to setting goals—namely, don’t settle for results that are below your full potential; don’t get comfortable. And maybe at one point, the Djoker had gotten comfortable.

>说到提升自己，诺瓦克·德约科维奇为我们所有人提供了一个令人惊叹的关于设定目标的课——也就是说，不要满足于低于你全部潜力的结果；不要变得舒服。也许在某个时候，德约科维奇也曾一度变得舒服了。

Do you remember when he was the perennial #3 on tour—2007, [[null|]]2008, 2009, and 2010? Do you also remember that in December 2010 he led Serbia to victory against France in the finals of the Davis Cup championship? And then do you recall what happened? He couldn’t lose.

>你记得他在巡回赛中长期排名第三的那些年吗？2007年、2008年、2009年，还有2010年？你还记得2010年12月，他带领塞尔维亚在戴维斯杯决赛中击败法国夺冠的情景吗？然后你还记得接下来发生了什么吗？他似乎再也无法输掉比赛了。

In 2011 he won ten tournaments, including the Australian Open, Wimbledon, and the U.S. Open, and set a world record for the most prize money in a single season ($12 million), which can happen when you win 41 matches in a row, second only to John McEnroe’s 42-match winning streak.

>2011年，他赢得了十项赛事，包括澳大利亚公开赛、温布尔登锦标赛和美国公开赛，并创下了单赛季奖金最高的世界纪录（1200万美元）。这种情况通常发生在你连胜41场的时候，仅次于约翰·麦肯罗保持的42连胜纪录。

Why did this stunning upgrade occur? Yes, he went on a gluten-free diet, and I believe it made a huge difference including helping his breathing. Yes, he bought into believing in his serve. Novak tinkered with his service motion and it wasn’t working, so he went back to his original motion, which did. He went from being the only player in the Top 50 during the first three quarters of 2010 to average more double faults than aces to having a much more consistent, reliable, productive serve. Both of these changes were important, but I think the biggest change was inside the Djoker’s head.

>为什么他的表现会有如此惊人的提升？是的，他开始实行无麸质饮食，我相信这对他有巨大帮助，包括改善了他的呼吸状况。是的，他真正相信自己的发球。诺瓦克曾尝试调整他的发球动作，但效果不佳，于是他重新回到原来的发球方式，效果果然不错。他从2010年前三个季度时排名前50名的球员，转变为双误次数比ACE球还多的球员，最终拥有了更加稳定、可靠和高效的发球。这两项改变都很重要，但我认为最大的变化发生在德约科维奇的内心。

Leading Serbia to Davis Cup glory made something happen in his head that shook him up, got him raising his sights and goals, made him dig deep inside for big results outside that became historic. Being triumphant in the Davis Cup made him hungrier—I think—to be the best on the A.T.P. Tour.

>带领塞尔维亚赢得戴维斯杯冠军，让他内心发生了某种变化，这种变化震撼了他，促使他提高目标，渴望取得更加辉煌的成绩，这些成绩后来成为了历史。赢得戴维斯杯冠军让他更加渴望——我想——成为ATP巡回赛中最好的选手。

Maybe The Djoker had gotten settled mentally into a #3 spot and then the Davis Cup championship smashed the comfort zone and played the biggest part in his turnaround. Something really significant happened to Djokovic’s attitude. I’m not sure he even fully understands it all, but that’s my guess, because the difference in a world #1 and a world #3 is usually mental. Djokovic became his own prophet; he saw the promised land and moved there.

>或许，德约科维奇在心理上已经适应了世界排名第三的位置，而戴维斯杯冠军赛彻底打破了他舒适区，这在很大程度上促成了他的逆转。他的心态发生了某种真正重要的转变。我不确定他是否完全理解这一切，但这是我猜测的，因为在世界排名第一和世界排名第三之间，通常差别就在于心理层面。德约科维奇成为了自己的先知，他看到了应许之地，并且迈步前往那里。

Something similar may have happened to the so-called perennial #4, Andy Murray, who’d lost four Grand Slam finals including the 2012 Wimbledon championship against Roger Federer. Earlier Murray had brought Ivan Lendl on board—8 Grand Slam singles championships—to help him get over the hump of being the best player never to have won a major. Ivan had also lost his first four appearances in Slam finals but kept battling and believing to go on and win those eight championships. As a [[null|]]coach—and a good one—he instilled in Andy the same massive no-quit relentlessness, something that may have been missing in the Scot. And it started, believe it or not, with Murray’s four-set loss to Federer in the 2012 Wimbledon finals. Lendl recognized in the loss that Andy had actually been very competitive for the first time in a Slam finals appearance—he won the first set and was close to winning a couple of others.

>类似的事情也可能发生在那位所谓的常青不老四号选手安迪·穆雷身上。他曾四次在大满贯决赛中失利，包括2012年温网决赛输给罗杰·费德勒。此前，穆雷曾请来伊万·伦德尔——这位拥有八座大满贯单打冠军头衔的传奇人物——帮助他克服“天赋异禀却从未赢得过重要赛事冠军”的心理障碍。伦德尔也曾经历过在大满贯决赛中前四次都失利，但他始终坚持不懈、相信自己，最终赢得了那八座冠军奖杯。作为一名优秀的教练，伦德尔将这种永不放弃、坚持不懈的精神灌输给穆雷，而这种精神或许正是穆雷所缺乏的。令人难以置信的是，这一切的起点，正是穆雷在2012年温网决赛中以四盘大战输给费德勒的那一战。伦德尔从这场失利中意识到，穆雷在大满贯决赛中第一次表现得如此具有竞争力——他赢下了第一盘，并且在其他几盘中也接近取胜。

From my conversations with Ivan I believe he focused on the positives with Andy after that Wimbledon defeat, got him thinking about the greatly improved performance vis-à-vis the previous appearances in Slam finals, where he lost in really noncompetitive straight sets. The proof may be this: a couple of weeks after the Wimbledon defeat, Andy easily beat Fed in the gold medal match at the 2012 Olympics. Although it is not a Grand Slam event, Ivan Lendl basically declared it a “major” and said that Andy had won his first Grand Slam! With that declaration he essentially called Murray a Grand Slam champion. Did that take a load of Murray’s shoulders? You decide.

>从我和伊万的交谈中，我倾向于认为他在温网失利后，着重引导安迪关注积极的一面，让他思考自己在大满贯决赛中的表现相较以往已经有了显著提升。此前他在大满贯决赛中屡屡以毫无悬念的直落两盘告负，而这次却明显进步了。证据或许就在这里：温网失利数周后，安迪在2012年奥运会金牌赛中轻松击败费德勒。虽然这并不是大满贯赛事，但伊万·伦德尔基本上将其定义为“大满贯”并表示，安迪赢得了他人生中的首个大满贯！通过这一声明，他实际上已经将穆雷称为大满贯冠军。这是否减轻了穆雷的压力？你来评判。

Strange as it sounds (because when you see him sitting in the friends’ box, watching a match, Ivan appears to be so serious and gruff), he taught positive thinking to Andy Murray. Nevertheless, the years ahead will tell us whether he can retain that Lendl-like toughness, tenacity, and positive attitude and win a bunch of Slams.

>听起来很奇怪（因为在朋友席位上看到他时，伊万看起来那么严肃、严厉），他却教会了安迪·穆雷积极思考。然而，未来的岁月将告诉我们，他是否能继续保持伦德尔那样的坚韧、顽强和积极心态，赢得众多大满贯冠军。

The lesson? You are better than you think you are; you can play at a higher level than you think you can; and you have the potential to beat so-called better players. If you’re a competitor and not just playing for exercise (and “cardio” tennis is great), don’t accept where you are; don’t settle for less. Shake yourself up in whatever manner it takes and you’ll get results. Like Murray and the Djoker you can turn things around and beat players who are beating you.

>教训是：你比自己想象的要强；你可以发挥出比自己想象中更高的水平；你有能力击败那些所谓的更强对手。如果你是真正有竞争意识的选手（而不仅仅是为锻炼身体而打球——“有氧”网球也很棒），那就不要满足于现状，不要接受自己目前的水平；不要安于现状。无论用什么方式，都要不断激励自己、挑战自己，你终将有所收获。就像穆雷和德约科维奇一样，你也可以扭转局势，击败那些现在击败你的对手。

This “don’t settle for less” attitude was displayed best by a player I like to call “Sparky” because of her great intensity and energy on the court. Justine Henin, who although sort of petite—about 5’ 5” and 125 pounds—played supersized, big bang tennis. She could have settled for being a counterpuncher as everybody told her to be (given her size), but she said no.

>这种“不要将就”的态度，最典型的例子是一位我习惯称之为“火花”（Sparky）的球员。她球场上展现出的激情和能量令人印象深刻。尽管朱婷·亨in（Justine Henin）个头不算高——大约1.65米，体重57公斤——但她打的是大开大合、极具冲击力的网球。所有人都建议她以防守反击为主（考虑到她的体型），但她却拒绝了。

Justine had a different kind of game in mind for herself and used her unbelievable timing to crack serves and forehands, and develop one [[null|]]of the best-ever backhands women’s tennis has seen. She willed herself to become a dominant offensive player by not going with conventional wisdom or listening to most of the experts. I love that. She overcame the odds and won seven Grand Slams with a bigger brand of tennis in spite of her smaller stature. Big Game—Small Package.

>朱婷为自己设想了一种截然不同的打法，她凭借令人难以置信的时机把握，打出威力十足的发球和正手击球，并发展出了女子网球史上最为出色的反手之一。她不遵循传统智慧，也不听从大多数专家的建议，而是坚定地立志成为一名具有统治力的进攻型选手。我非常欣赏这一点。她克服了种种不利条件，凭借更具侵略性的打法赢得了七座大满贯冠军，尽管她的身材并不高大。大场面，小个子。

Henin rose above her height with her mind. Tough to do, but she did. And, maybe you can do it in your own way. There are times you have to follow your gut, to do what you know is right for you and commit to it like Justine did early in her career.

>亨因用她的头脑超越了她的身高。这很难做到，但她做到了。也许你也可以用自己的方式做到这一点。有时候你必须听从直觉，做你知道对自己正确的事，并像亨因职业生涯早期那样坚定不移地执行下去。

Now, let’s focus on one aspect of another female superstar’s greatness that you should bring into your game, or rather into your head; Serena Williams (and Venus too) have serious short-term memory loss. By that I mean when things go bad in a point, game, set, or match, they have this ability to mentally wipe the slate clean—to forget about it immediately and not get ruined. Club players? We miss a few shots and lose a couple of games and it gets in our mind; we lose confidence, get rattled, and dial it down. Believe me, I know. That was me on tour plenty of times. As you’ll read later in Winning Ugly, when you get down on yourself—start beating yourself up mentally—there are now two players on the court trying to take you down. And one of them is you.

>现在，让我们聚焦于另一位女超级明星的伟大之处，这也是你应该带入你比赛，或者说带入你脑海中的东西；塞雷娜·威廉姆斯（以及维纳斯也一样）有着严重的短期记忆障碍。我的意思是，当比赛中某一分、某一盘、某一局甚至整场比赛形势不妙时，她们有能力在心理上“擦掉黑板”——立刻忘记这些不愉快的事情，不让自己因此崩溃。普通球员呢？我们错过几球、输掉几盘，这些就会在心里留下阴影；我们会失去信心，情绪紧张，进而放慢节奏。相信我，我太了解这一点了。在巡回赛中，我经常就是那个样子。正如你将在《赢在不漂亮》后面读到的，当你开始责怪自己、在心理上折磨自己时，实际上已经有两名球员在场上试图把你击败。其中一位就是你自己。

Keep Serena’s and Venus’s memory loss—the ability to look ahead, not behind—in your mind and copy it. When either sister gets off her game she does not lose faith; she keeps taking her big cuts and going for her shots without anxiety over the score or those mistakes. Each point starts fresh in her mind with no taint from a mistake previously.

>将塞雷娜和维纳斯的记忆力丧失——也就是能够向前看而非向后看——铭记于心，并加以效仿。当其中任何一位姐妹的状态出现波动时，她并不会因此失去信心；她依旧大胆地进行强力击球，不因比分或之前的失误而感到焦虑。在她心中，每一分都是全新的开始，之前的失误不会留下任何阴影。

It’s great short-term memory loss, and it helps them stay committed to what they want to do. Opponents know that; they know that Serena, in particular, can be very lethal in that situation because she will not give up on herself, stays totally committed to and believes in her game, and comes out fighting harder and better than before. That’s scary when you’re on the other side of the net.

>这算是短期记忆丧失的好处，它帮助他们始终专注于自己想要做到的事情。对手们也清楚这一点；他们知道，塞雷娜尤其如此，在这种情况下她往往极具威胁性，因为她从不放弃自己，完全投入并相信自己的打法，而且会比之前更努力、更出色地迎战。当你站在网的另一边时，这确实令人感到害怕。

Nevertheless, if you watch enough matches you’ll always see something you never thought you’d see, and during the 2012 French Open I saw it: Serena Williams lost a first-round match to a player ranked 111th in the world, even though Serena had never lost in the first [[null|]]round of a Slam—ever!—and was up a set and leading the second set tie-breaker 5–1. How could this happen? Simple: The tide turned and sometimes you can’t turn back the tide even when you are Serena Williams and your opponent has a triple-digit ranking.

>然而，如果你看得比赛足够多，你总会看到一些你从未想过会看到的场面。2012年法网期间，我就亲眼目睹了这样一幕：塞雷娜·威廉姆斯在第一轮比赛中输给了世界排名111位的选手。要知道，塞雷娜此前在大满贯赛事的第一轮从未输过，而且当时她还领先一盘，第二盘抢七比分是5比1！这是怎么发生的呢？其实很简单：局势发生了逆转，有时候即使你是塞雷娜·威廉姆斯，对手的排名再高，你也没法把潮水重新挽回来。

A bad call here, an unforced error there, and a player, Virginie Razzano, who gets in the zone and has “home town” support and suddenly things get out of control. It may take a long time but it happens quick. And it did.

>这里一个糟糕的判罚，那里一次非受迫性失误，一位球员——维多利亚·拉扎诺，进入状态，拥有“主场”支持，突然间局势失控。这可能需要很长时间，但一旦发生，来得很快。而且确实发生了。

>[!note]- 译者注：
此处“home town”支持意指主场观众的支持，中文中通常用“主场优势”或“主场球迷支持”来表达，此处保留原意，以突出主场氛围对比赛的影响。

Serena was within two points of winning the three-hour match a bunch of times and then saved seven match points against herself. Razzano finally won it on the eighth attempt. It was a devastating blow for Serena, right?

>塞雷娜在长达三小时的比赛中，多次仅差两分就能夺冠，甚至一度挽救了七个赛点。最终拉扎诺在第八次尝试中才成功拿下比赛。这对塞雷娜来说，是不是个巨大的打击呢？

Nope. Just the opposite.
>不，恰恰相反。

I loved her response to the media right after the match: “Ya know, that’s life.” The lesson? Winning is hard enough when you believe in your shots. It’s impossible when you let yourself become a doubter. Remove doubt with your own short-term memory loss and I promise you’ll get results worth remembering. A good way to do that is after a loss to write down what you did right during the match; take something positive out of the negative.

>我特别喜欢她在比赛后对媒体说的那句话：“你知道，这就是生活。” 这个教训是：当你相信自己的球技时，赢已经很难了；而当你让自己变成怀疑者时，就更不可能赢了。用你自己的短期记忆丧失来消除怀疑吧，我向你保证，你会得到值得铭记的成果。一个很好的方法是在输掉比赛后，写下你在比赛中做对了什么；从负面经历中提取正面的东西。

In Serena’s case she did plenty right to get that close to winning. She kept fighting big when she faced match point against her over and over and over and over. Big pluses. In your own case look at your pluses, build on them. Look at your negatives, fix them. Build and fix. Move on. Tell yourself, “That’s life.”

>塞雷娜在比赛中确实做得很对，才得以接近胜利。当她多次面对赛点时，依然奋力拼搏，这种表现堪称加分项。在你自己的比赛中，也要先审视自己的优势，不断强化它们；再看看自己的劣势，想办法解决。不断建设、不断修正，然后继续前进。告诉自己：“这就是生活。”

Serena hates to lose any match, especially in a Slam, but in her mind this terrible loss was not something she would use to beat herself up with. In fact, she used it to get better—a lot better. Instead of heading back to New York, she headed to the Paris tennis academy of Patrick Mouratoglou for some consulting and intense upgrading of her game. She used the pain—and don’t let her “That’s life” comment suggest there wasn’t big pain—of the French loss to gear up massively and improve for the upcoming tests at Wimbledon, the 2012 Olympics, and the U.S. Open.

>塞琳娜非常讨厌输掉任何一场比赛，尤其是在大满贯赛事中。但对她而言，这次可怕的失利并不是用来责备自己的东西。事实上，她用这次失败来让自己变得更强——变得更强很多。她没有回到纽约，而是前往巴黎的帕特里克·莫瑞托格鲁网球学院，寻求一些指导，并对自身的技术进行深入的提升。她利用了这次法国失利带来的痛苦——别被她那句“这就是生活”所误导，那可是一场非常痛苦的经历——来为即将到来的温布尔登网球赛、2012年奥运会以及美国公开赛做好充分准备。

I believe her fighting spirit, her grit, and her strength of character was ignited by that loss in the French to Razzano because what followed was unbelievable: Serena won the Wimbledon singles and doubles championships, the Olympics singles and doubles gold medals, and the [[null|]]U.S. Open singles championship! I believe it was directly related to her reaction to the loss in Paris.

>我认为正是那场在法网输给拉扎诺的比赛，点燃了她的战斗精神、坚韧和坚强的品格。因为接下来发生的事情简直难以置信：塞雷娜赢得了温布尔登单打和双打冠军、奥运会单打和双打金牌，以及美国公开赛单打冠军！我相信这一切直接与她在巴黎失利后的反应有关。

Club players who suffer a tough loss usually get depressed and out of it for weeks because it creates devastation in their mind. Not Serena. She refuses to beat herself up. Do the same in your own game.

>那些在比赛中遭遇惨败的业余选手通常会陷入沮丧，连续几周都难以摆脱，因为这种失败会在他们心中造成巨大的打击。但塞雷娜不会这样。她从不自责。在你自己的比赛中也这样做吧。

>[!note]- 译者注：
此处“beat oneself up”意为自责、自我惩罚，翻译时采用更符合中文表达习惯的说法。

Now, let me give you what is still my biggest complaint about club players and your game: Too often you have the risk vs. reward calculation backward. Namely, when you don’t have an advantage in the point—like when you’re forced over to the doubles alley in the backcourt—you try to rip the impossible one-in-twenty lucky shot like one you made a couple of years ago. Conversely, when you get a ball right smack in the middle of the court—just back of the service line—suddenly you think, “Ooooooh, I don’t wanna miss this easy one,” and play it safe, just push it over the net. I say: “Why? What are you thinking?” That’s the time to get more aggressive, take some risk, up the ante. The ball’s in your strike zone and you’ve got a much better chance to go for a productive forcing shot or winner. It’s an easier shot that offers an easier reward. Take it!

>现在，让我告诉你我仍然对俱乐部球员和你们的比赛最大的抱怨：你们常常把风险与回报的计算弄反了。具体来说，当你在比赛中没有优势——比如你被迫退到双打线的后方——你却试图像几年前那样打出那记几乎不可能的一次幸运球，比如那个一二十次才出现一次的球。相反，当你拿到一个正落在球场中央的球——就在发球线后面——你却突然想：“哦哦哦，我不想错过这个简单的球。”于是你选择保守，轻轻一推，把球打过网。我说：“为什么？你在想什么？”这个时候，你应该更加主动，承担一些风险，把压力提高。球已经进入你的击球范围，你有更大的机会打出一个能得分的主动进攻球。这其实是一个更容易击中的球，带来的回报也更容易实现。去打吧！

The lesson? Don’t bunt when the ball’s teed up for a home run; don’t go for the home run when a single keeps the inning going.

>教训是：当球高高悬在击球区，准备轰出全垒打时，不要选择安打；当一支安打足以延续半局时，也不要一味追求全垒打。

But club players often get cautious when they should get aggressive and aggressive when they should be cautious. (The same thing happens on higher bouncing balls: You stand there and let the ball play you and try make a great shot out of nothing. Don’t do that. Run back a few steps or more and return a neutral, higher bouncing ball to your opponent.)

>但业余球手常常会在该主动进攻时变得犹豫不决，在该谨慎防守时却过于激进。（同样的情况也发生在面对高弹跳球时：你站在原地，让球带着你跑，然后试图凭空打出精彩的一击。不要这样做。后退几步，甚至更多，把一个中性、高弹跳的球回给对手。）

Maybe you’ve been taught never to retreat. Well, I say retreat and stay in the point by returning a neutral ball and wait for your chance. When you see that chance arrive—like a ball in the middle of the court— then step it up and force things to happen. Federer may be able to hit a winner from anywhere in the stadium including the hot dog stand, but club players should be more realistic in analyzing when to up the risk to produce the reward.

>也许你曾被教导永远不要后退。但我想说的是，后退一点，把球回得很平淡，然后留在这个回合中等待机会。当你看到机会来了——就像一颗落在球场中央的球——就立刻行动起来，迫使局面发生变化。费德勒或许能在球场的任何地方击出制胜球，甚至从热狗摊上也能击出，但普通球员在分析何时该提高风险以换取回报时，应该更加现实一些。

Finally, my last lesson here comes from an unlikely tennis source: Metallica, my favorite band, and their drummer, Lars Ulrich, who’s my buddy. (His dad, Torben, was a top Danish tennis pro back in the 1950s and ’60s and maybe the most eclectic guy the tour has ever seen.) Lars [[null|]]and each member of Metallica are still touring the world to huge crowds even though the guys are not teenagers anymore. But their passion is still huge. Lars is in his late forties, and he still brings it every single night—full sweat, playing as hard as he ever has in front of crowds bigger than they’ve ever been. They collectively do not take it for granted and give it every ounce they’ve got. Like Rafa, a million percent.

>最后，我要在这里分享的最后一课来自一个意想不到的网球来源：我最喜欢的乐队金属乐队（Metallica），以及他们的鼓手拉尔斯·乌尔里希（Lars Ulrich），他也是我的好朋友。（他的父亲托尔本·乌尔里希（Torben Ulrich）曾是上世纪50年代和60年代丹麦顶尖的网球选手，也许是有史以来巡回赛上最另类的家伙之一。）拉尔斯和金属乐队的每一位成员如今虽然已经不再是青少年，仍然在全球范围内巡演，吸引着大批观众。但他们的热情依旧不减。拉尔斯已经年近四十，每晚依然全力以赴，满头大汗地在比以往更大的观众面前尽情表演。他们从不把这一切当作理所当然，而是倾尽所有去奉献。就像拉法（Rafa）一样，百分之百如此。

The lesson? In tennis and in life when you do something you love, appreciate it and respect it by giving it all you can. Never take it for granted because soon enough it will be taken away. Time is tough. Every time you go out on the court be thankful. Be grateful. Believe me, I am. If you are lucky enough to be a tennis player you should recognize that it’s a privilege to be able to walk out on that court and play the game.

>教训是什么？在网球中，在生活中，当你做着自己热爱的事情时，要心怀感激，尊重它，全力以赴。永远不要把它视为理所当然，因为很快它就会被夺走。时间很残酷。每次你踏上球场，都要心怀感恩，心怀感激。相信我，我就是这样想的。如果你有幸成为网球选手，你应该意识到，能够走上球场、打这项运动是一种特权。

I’m also grateful that I get to analyze twenty-first-century tennis on television around the world. Tennis is a moving escalator that’s always changing; the players, technology, strokes, courts, rackets, the balls. It’s something I love to be a part of. There’s only one thing missing: I love being nervous. I love waking up at 3:30 A.M. anxious about an upcoming match I’m playing (when I was on tour) or one for a pro I’m coaching.

>我也很感激能够在全球的电视上分析二十一世纪的网球。网球是一台不断变化的移动电梯，球员、技术、击球方式、球场、球拍、球，一切都处在变化之中。我非常享受成为其中一员。唯一缺少的就是——我热爱紧张的感觉。我热爱凌晨3:30醒来，为即将进行的比赛感到焦虑（当我还在巡回赛时，是为我自己的比赛，而现在则是为我正在指导的球员的比赛）。

There’s nothing like those nerves and the excitement that comes with knowing that in a few hours two men enter the arena and only one man leaves a winner. Television broadcasting is great. I have fun with it. I make some great picks and sometimes I make a knucklehead pick, but it does not make me nervous. Dissecting players’ games and talking strategy and tactics is fun, but it’s not like being in the ring; it’s not like those nerves you feel before a fight. That’s what’s fun.

>没有什么比得上那种紧张感，以及知道自己在几个小时后，两名男子即将进入赛场，只有一人能带着胜利离开的那种兴奋感。电视转播很棒，我从中享受乐趣。我有时能做出很棒的预测，有时也会做出一些傻乎乎的预测，但这并不会让我感到紧张。分析球员的比赛、讨论策略和战术很有趣，但那并不是真正身处赛场的感觉；它不像拳击前那种紧张感，不像面对战斗前那种心跳加速的感觉。正是这种感觉，才让人觉得有趣。

And that’s why playing tennis is so great at whatever level you play. A 3.5 club player can have great fun and excitement playing a ladder match or tournament just like a pro on tour. Hopefully, Winning Ugly in the twenty-first century will add to your fun and excitement by giving you more mental-management skills that can make you a better player right now—today. Believe me, you are not far away from beating those players who have been beating you. Winning Ugly tells you how. Good luck.

>这就是为什么无论你在哪个水平打网球都如此精彩。即使是3.5级的球员，也可以在爬梯赛或比赛中享受到如同职业选手般的乐趣与激情。希望《丑胜》（Winning Ugly）在二十一世纪能为你增添更多乐趣，通过传授更多心理管理技巧，让你立刻成为更出色的球员——从今天开始。相信我，你距离战胜那些一直战胜你的人已经不远了。《丑胜》会告诉你该怎么做。祝你好运！[[null|]]



# [[null|]][[null|]]Preface to the First Edition 第一版前言

# Winning Ugly?赢得很丑？

“How in the hell does this guy win? He hits like a caveman who found a tennis racket!” That’s the incredulous opinion I overheard during the finals of a Volvo Tennis/San Francisco Classic while Brad Gilbert was battling and beating Anders Jarryd to win the championship and $32,000. The prize was part of $1.5 million he would win during the year. My reaction surprised me. As one of thousands of fans there to see our hometown hero (Brad lives just outside of San Francisco in San Rafael, California) I didn’t like hearing him criticized. However, I had to admit the caveman reference wasn’t altogether inappropriate. Brad does have a unique style.

>“这人是怎么赢的？他打球的样子就像一个发现了网球拍的原始人！”这就是我在沃尔沃网球赛/旧金山经典赛决赛中听到的惊讶评价，当时布拉德·吉尔伯特正在与安德斯·雅里德激烈交锋，并最终赢得了冠军，以及他当年将获得的150万美元奖金。我的反应让我自己都感到惊讶。作为一名在场观看我们家乡英雄（布拉德住在旧金山附近的加州萨拉弗利）的数千名球迷之一，我不喜欢听到他被批评。然而，我不得不承认，把他说成是“原始人”的比喻并非完全不恰当。布拉德确实有他独特的风格。

It got me thinking. Why does he win? How does he beat players like Boris Becker who are supposedly better than him? Looks are deceiving, and with Brad (whose style has been described as “ugly”) looks are very deceiving. He has won over $5 million in tournament prize money. He was in the Top 10 internationally for five years and rose as high as number four in the world in 1990. He is an Olympic medalist and has successfully represented the United States in Davis Cup competition many times. Brad Gilbert has been one of the top players in tennis for almost ten years. All because he was able to beat players the experts said he shouldn’t have been able to beat. How does he do it?

>这让我开始思考起来。他为什么能赢？他是如何击败像贝克尔这样 supposedly 更强的选手的？外表往往具有欺骗性，而布拉德（他的打法曾被形容为“丑陋”）的外表更是极具欺骗性。他赢得了超过500万美元的赛事奖金，曾连续五年位列世界前十，1990年甚至曾达到世界第四的排名。他是奥运奖牌得主，多次代表美国参加戴维斯杯比赛。布拉德·吉尔伯特在过去近十年里一直是网球界的顶尖选手之一。这一切都因为他能够击败那些专家认为他不该赢的选手。他是如何做到的？

Brad’s success comes from the fact that he is a thinking person’s tennis player. In tennis, he is the best in the world at the mental side of the game. What spectators see are his strokes, and those strokes aren’t always pretty. What people don’t see are the mental machinations that lead him to victory, what goes on in his head before, during, and after a match. [[null|]]Most tennis players are mentally lazy on the court. Brad uses this to his advantage and believes you can do the same. He utilizes every second of a match to try and figure out a way to gain an advantage. Without overwhelming physical gifts or astonishing shots, Brad wins because he outthinks and outplans opponents.

>布拉德的成功源于他是一个善于思考的网球选手。在网球比赛中，他在心理层面堪称世界顶级。观众看到的是他的击球动作，而这些动作并不总是赏心悦目。人们看不到的是那些引导他取得胜利的心理运作，以及他在比赛前、比赛中和比赛后脑海中所进行的种种思虑。[[null|]]大多数网球选手在球场上心理上都比较懒惰，而布拉德则善于利用这一点，并相信你也可以做到。他在每一场比赛的每一秒里都努力寻找获取优势的方法。他并不具备令人惊叹的天赋或惊人的击球技术，但他能取胜，是因为他比对手想得更周全、计划得更缜密。

Tim Gallwey’s book, The Inner Game of Tennis, focused on the right-brain, or intuitive process. Brad’s focus is on the left-brain, or analytical process. Gallwey pursued the joys of playing. Brad pursues the joys of winning. Gallwey was concerned with the “inner game.” Brad is concerned with knowing what’s going on right in front of him and taking advantage of it.

>提姆·加尔维的著作《网球的内心游戏》关注的是右脑，也就是直觉思维的过程。布拉德则专注于左脑，也就是分析性思维的过程。加尔维追求的是打球的乐趣，而布拉德追求的是获胜的乐趣。加尔维关注的是“内心游戏”，而布拉，德则关注的是当下发生的一切，并善于利用这些机会。

Winning Ugly tells how to do the same thing with your own tennis game. Brad describes his formula for making the most out of what you’ve got. And it works. In writing this book with him I learned about a part of the game I had never focused on before: the mental aspect of tennis. My game improved, and so will yours.

>《赢得很丑》讲述了如何在自己的网球比赛中做到这一点。布拉德描述了他如何最大限度地利用自己所拥有的条件。而且，这方法确实有效。在与他合著这本书的过程中，我了解到自己以前从未关注过的网球比赛的一部分：网球的心理层面。我的球技提升了，你的球技也同样会提升。

After you’ve put Brad’s formula to the test, if anyone ever accuses you of “winning ugly” just say, “Thank you. I’ve been working on it.”

>在你尝试过布拉德的公式之后，如果有人指责你“赢得很丑”，就回应说：“谢谢，我一直在努力。”

Steve Jamison史蒂夫·贾米森



# [[null|]][[null|]]McEnroe: A Master Loses Ugly
>麦肯罗：一位大师的丑陋失利

John McEnroe was having his worst nightmare. Except it wasn’t a nightmare. It was the Masters at Madison Square Garden in front of thousands of his loyal, screaming, stomping New York City fans. Tonight McEnroe, defending champion and ranked number two in the world, was slowly realizing that he was going to lose to a guy he didn’t like and whose game he despised. That guy was me. It was humiliating for him and he was seething.

>约翰·麦肯罗正在经历他最可怕的噩梦。不过，这并不是噩梦，而是在纽约市麦迪逊广场花园举行的美网赛事，面前是成千上万热情呐喊、跺脚支持他的忠实球迷。今晚，这位卫冕冠军、世界排名第二的麦肯罗正慢慢意识到，他将输掉这场球赛，输给他一个他不喜欢、而且他根本看不惯的对手。那个人就是我。对他来说，这是一次极大的羞辱，他的怒火正在熊熊燃烧。

His eyes gave him away first. They had the look of a kid who had just set fire to the neighbor’s cat: scared and mean. His face had that hateful McEnroe scowl. On a changeover he snarled over in my direction, “Gilbert, you don’t deserve to be on the same court with me!” He was heading over the edge. We crossed within a few feet of each other. In case I’d missed his point he added, “You are the worst. The ——— worst!”

>他的眼神首先出卖了他。那眼神就像一个刚刚把邻居家的猫烧掉的孩子：既害怕又充满敌意。他的脸上挂着麦肯罗那种令人讨厌的皱眉表情。在一次换边休息时，他朝我这边吼道：“吉尔伯特，你不配和我站在同一个球场上！”他快要崩溃了。我们相距仅几步之遥。为了确保我明白他的意思，他又补充道：“你是最差的。最——最差的！”

McEnroe continued to go downhill, complaining about the electronic line-calling machine, arguing heatedly with a spectator during the second set, receiving a code of conduct warning later for a “visible obscenity.” He was completely nuts. Screaming at himself, slamming his racket, storming around and taunting the crowd (his own fans!). At times it looked like he was having a public nervous breakdown.

>麦克纳尔（McEnroe）的情况继续恶化，他抱怨电子线裁判机，第二盘比赛中与一名观众激烈争执，后来还因“明显粗俗的言行”收到了一次行为规范警告。他完全疯了。他对着自己大喊大叫，砸着球拍，怒气冲冲地在场内来回奔跑，还嘲讽观众（他的粉丝们！）。有时候看起来，他似乎正在经历一场公开的神经崩溃。

It was one of his better performances. But, in the end, the guy who didn’t “deserve to be on the same court” with him won in three tough sets. Well, actually, two tough sets. The third was a breeze: 5–7, 6–4, 6–1. By match point John was a pussycat. But that wasn’t the end.

>这是他表现得不错的一场比赛。但最终，那个“不配和他站在同一个球场上”的人，以三盘苦战获胜。其实，只是两盘苦战，第三盘则轻松取胜：5–7，6–4，6–1。到了赛点，约翰却像只胆小的猫咪。但这并不是结局。

Afterward, he dropped a bombshell. McEnroe announced his “retirement” from tennis at the age of twenty-seven. And he blamed part of it on me! Mac explained that he needed a sabbatical because, “When I start losing to players like him \[“him” being yours truly\] I’ve got to reconsider what I’m doing even playing this game.” If he thought I’d be [[null|]]insulted he was wrong. In fact, I loved it. In seven previous matches I had won only one set. That victory in the Masters was a great win. Too great as it turned out, but more on that later.

>随后，他扔下了一颗重磅炸弹。麦肯罗在二十七岁的时候宣布“退役”了。他还部分归咎于我！麦克解释说，他需要休个假，因为“当我开始输给像他这样（‘他’指的是我）的球员时，我不得不重新考虑自己到底在做什么，甚至是否还该继续打这项运动。”如果他认为我会因此感到被冒犯，那他错了。事实上，我反而非常喜欢这种说法。在之前的七场比赛中，我只赢过一盘。那场大师赛的胜利是一次巨大的胜利。后来才发现，这胜利太大了，不过关于这点，我们稍后再谈。

### 0.1 The Bank Doesn’t Think I’m Ugly
>0.1 银行不认为我丑

John McEnroe and I have never exactly been what you’d call best friends. I guess that can happen when two people don’t like each other. He thinks I’ve got bad strokes. I think he’s got a bad attitude. But the truth of the matter is that McEnroe is right. On paper he shouldn’t lose to me. And neither should Becker, Agassi, Connors, Chang, Edberg, Courier, Forget, Sampras, Stich, Wheaton, and a lot of other guys with better strokes and more so-called natural ability.

>约翰·麦肯罗和我从未真正算是最好的朋友。我想，当两个人彼此不喜欢时，这种情况就可能发生。他认为我的击球技术不好，而我认为他的态度很差。但事实是，麦肯罗说得对。纸面上，他不应该输给我。同样，贝克尔、阿加西、康纳斯、张德培、埃德伯格、库尔库尔、福雷特、萨姆普拉斯、施特希、惠特森，以及许多其他击球技术更出色、所谓天赋更强的选手，也不应该输给我。

Fortunately for me, tennis matches aren’t played on paper. They’re played on tennis courts. And because they are I’ve been able to beat those players and others to the tune of $5,000,000. By 1991 I was eighth on the all-time prize money list. When you add in the endorsements and exhibitions that resulted from those victories my total income from tennis by 1993 is close to $8,000,000. Winning ugly? All the way to the bank.

>幸运的是，网球比赛并不是在纸上进行的。它们是在网球场上进行的。正因为如此，我才能击败那些选手和其他人，战绩达到800万胜场。赢得难看？那也是赚到手软。

It happened because I’ve used whatever talent and skills I do have in a calculated way that maximizes their potential; that gives me my best chance to win. It’s why I’ve been able to beat players who are supposedly “better” than me. You can do the same. Make the most of what you’ve got. Play better tennis without better strokes.

>这之所以会发生，是因为我以一种计算好的方式使用了我所拥有的所有天赋和技能，从而最大化它们的潜力；这给了我赢得比赛的最佳机会。这也是为什么我能够战胜那些被普遍认为“比我更强”的选手。你也可以做到这一点。充分发挥你所拥有的能力，无需更好的击球技巧，就能打出更出色的比赛。

### 0.2 The New, Improved You: 20 Percent Better
>0.2 你升级版：提升20%

First, I want to tell you one thing about your game. The way to make the biggest improvement in the shortest time is to better understand and use the opportunities for gaining an advantage that exist in every match you play. The big opportunities and the small opportunities. Especially the small opportunities, the ones players neglect because of [[null|]]ignorance or laziness. If you want to call that winning ugly, go ahead and get ugly. Develop your powers of observation and analysis and then use the information, and your chances of winning will go up by 20 percent or more.

>首先，我想告诉你关于你比赛的一件事。在最短的时间内实现最大的进步，最好的方法就是更好地理解和利用你在每一场比赛中都存在的、获得优势的机会。这些机会有大有小。尤其是那些小机会，球员们常常因为无知或懒惰而忽视它们。如果你想称之为“赢得很丑”，那就去赢吧，赢得丑一点也没关系。提升你的观察力和分析能力，然后利用这些信息，你赢得比赛的机会就会增加20%或更多。

### 0.3 Brain-Dead Tennis: The Unaware Player
>0.3 脑死亡式网球：无意识的球员

Most weekend players, most club players, most recreational players are brain-dead on the tennis court. They go out and run around with no plan, no thought, no nothing. They give it as much study and consideration as jumping rope. And that’s why they can be had. With two players of roughly equal ability the one who’s aware of and takes advantage of the dynamics, opportunities, and openings before, during, and after the match will win.

>大多数周末选手、俱乐部选手、休闲选手在球场上都像是大脑停机了一样。他们一上场就盲目奔跑，毫无计划、毫无思考、毫无头绪。他们对比赛的投入程度，跟跳绳差不多。正因如此，他们才容易被击败。在两名实力相当的选手之间，谁能在赛前、赛中和赛后更早察觉并利用对手的动态、机会和漏洞，谁就能赢得比赛。

The person driving the fancy little sports car may look like he (or she) should come in first. I’ll put my money on the grease monkey in the modified stock car who knows how to get around the track. It’s the same for tennis. Pretty strokes are okay. But there’s a lot more to the game than that.

>驾驶那辆精致小跑车的人看起来似乎应该赢得比赛。但我更看好那辆改装赛车里的“油污小子”，他知道如何在赛道上穿梭。网球也是一样。漂亮的击球动作当然很好，但这项运动远不止于此。

Smart Tennis Is a Three-Step Process
>聪明的网球打法是三步流程

1\. Recognize your opportunity. 认清你的机会。

2\. Analyze your options. 分析你的选择。

3\. Capitalize on the opportunity by using the best option. 抓住机会，选择最佳方案。

Recognize. Analyze. Capitalize. That applies before, during, and after a match to all aspects of the game: mental, physical, and emotional. It even involves your equipment. Example? Opportunity exists early with the spin of the racket. You should recognize it. Depending on your opponent and the conditions, you should choose and capitalize on the right option.

>识别。分析。把握。这适用于比赛前、比赛中以及比赛后，适用于比赛的各个方面：心理、身体和情绪层面。它甚至涉及你的装备。例如？机会在比赛早期就可能通过球拍的旋转出现。你应该识别出这个机会。根据对手和场地状况，你要选择并把握住最合适的选项。

At tennis clubs and public courts the wrong choice is made about 85 percent of the time. Coming up I’ll tell you how to get the most benefit out of that situation. It’s a very small element of your total match, but [[null|]]it is an element that contains opportunity. You just have to know what to do with it.

>在网球俱乐部和公共球场上，人们大约85%的时间都做出了错误的选择。接下来我会告诉你如何从这种情况下获得最大的好处。这只是一个你整场比赛中非常微小的部分，[[null|]]但它确实是一个包含机会的环节。你只需要知道该如何利用它。

And there are scores of other openings a smart player uses to gain an edge. I believe if you recognize and take advantage of those opportunities in enough ways and enough times you’ll probably win.

>还有许多其他聪明球员用来获取优势的开局方式。我认为，如果你在足够多的场合、以足够多的方式识别并利用这些机会，你大概率就会赢。

“I don’t think his game is so good. But he makes up for it with his mental ability.”
>“我不觉得他的技术有多好，但他靠心理素质弥补了这一点。”

—David Wheaton, following a loss to Brad Gilbert
>——大卫·惠特曼，在输给布拉德·吉尔伯特之后

Unlike jumping rope, a tennis match doesn’t begin when it starts and end when it stops. Tennis begins off the court, continues through your pre-match regimen and into the match, and goes on after you’ve won (or lost) the final point. Smart players know how to prepare correctly for a match and once the contest is under way how to control their emotions. They know how to think their way through a match, avoiding low percentage shots that carry unnecessary risk at inappropriate times. Smart players observe what’s going on in a match and analyze the information. They know how to capitalize on what they know.

>与跳绳不同，网球比赛并不在比赛开始时才开始，也不在比赛结束时才结束。网球比赛其实从球场外就开始了，延续到赛前的准备程序，贯穿整个比赛，并在你赢得（或输掉）最后一分之后依然继续。聪明的球员知道如何正确地为一场比赛做好准备，一旦比赛开始，他们也懂得如何控制自己的情绪。他们知道如何在比赛中通过思考来应对局势，避免在不恰当的时刻做出风险过高、得不偿失的击球动作。聪明的球员会观察比赛中的每一个细节，并分析所获取的信息。他们懂得如何利用自己掌握的知识来取得优势。

Obviously, it takes some discipline, commitment, and effort. I mention this because most players will bust their buns to improve a particular stroke. They’ll take lessons, hit on the ball machine, bang against the backboard, practice in the hot sun. Blood, sweat, and tears, the whole bit. Then having improved whatever stroke they’re trying to perfect, they won’t spend a minute figuring out how to use it to maximum effect during a match. Brain-dead. Hard workers, but brain-dead.

>显然，这需要一定的纪律性、承诺和努力。我之所以提到这点，是因为大多数球员都会拼了老命去提升某个特定的击球技术。他们会请教练、在球机上反复练习、对着墙板击球、在烈日下苦练。流血流汗，一切为了技术的提升。但一旦他们把某个击球技术练好了，却不会花哪怕一分钟去思考如何在比赛中充分发挥它的威力。脑子僵了。他们是努力的工人，但脑子却像死机了一样。

>[!note]- 译者注：
此处“brain-dead”并非字面意思，而是指思维僵化、缺乏战术意识。

Ahead in this book are the opportunities that you should be aware of, the options you have, and the ways to capitalize on them from pre-match through post-match. How to mentally and physically prepare for battle on the court. How to play against all types of tennis styles. How to recognize important dynamics within a match. How to handle pressure and psyching. And more.

>本书将为你揭示你应该注意的机会、你拥有的选择，以及如何从赛前到赛后充分利用这些机会。如何在球场上做好心理和身体上的准备，如何应对各种类型的网球打法，如何识别比赛中的关键动态，如何应对压力和心理调节。还有更多实用内容。

It’s the nuts and bolts of what I’ve learned at all levels from junior [[null|]]tennis to a Top 5 ranking and a bank account I never dreamed possible. And you can apply it to your game. Some of the ideas are obvious. Others aren’t so obvious. Some of it will apply to your game and some of it won’t. But all of it is directed to the most neglected aspect of tennis: the mental part of your game. Let’s start before the beginning.[[null|]]

>这就是我从青少年网球到跻身世界前五、拥有我从未想过能拥有的银行账户这一路上所学到的点点滴滴。你可以把这些经验应用到自己的比赛中。有些想法显而易见，有些则不太明显。有些内容适用于你的比赛，有些则未必。但所有这些内容都指向网球最被忽视的部分——你比赛中的心理层面。让我们从比赛开始之前就开始吧。



# [[null|]][[null|]]I

>[[空值|]][[空值|]]我

# The Early Edge: The Match Begins Before the Match Begins

>先发制人：比赛在比赛开始前就已经开始了

“I don’t think much of Brad Gilbert.”

>“我对布拉德·吉尔伯特没什么好印象。”

—John McEnroe[[null|]]

>——约翰·麦肯罗



# [[null|]][[null|]]1

>[[null|]][[null|]]1 /no_think

# Mental Preparation: The Pre-Match Advantage

>心理准备：赛前优势

## 1 Turning Pro: Young and Innocent

>1 走向职业：年轻而天真

One of the first lessons I learned when I turned pro in 1982 was how much of an edge could be gained before the match even got started. It became obvious to me that for the best players in the world their match had begun a long time before the first serve. They came ready to play and wanted to grab me by the throat as soon as they could.

>我转职业的1982年，学到的第一课就是：比赛甚至还没开始，就已经能取得巨大的优势。很快我就明白，世界上顶尖的球员们其实早在第一发球之前就已经开始了比赛。他们做好了准备，一有机会就想要掐住我的脖子。

As a member of the tennis teams at Foothill Junior College and Pepperdine, I liked to just show up and play. I’d settle into the match mentally and physically during the first set. A lot of times I could get away with it because my opponent was doing it too. Do you approach your matches the same way?

>在弗oothill社区大学和佩珀丁大学的网球队伍中，我习惯于简单地到场打球。我通常在第一盘中逐渐进入比赛状态，身心都慢慢适应。很多时候，我都能这样应付过去，因为对手也是一样。你们打球时也是这样应对的吗？

On tour this wasn’t such a good idea. The slow start didn’t work against McEnroe, Lendl, Connors, and some of the veterans. By the time I got settled into some of those matches, the match was already over. One time I started out by losing the first sixteen points of the match. It was over so fast I almost didn’t need to take a shower afterward. Brutal—I was learning the hard way.

>巡回赛时，这可不是个好主意。慢热的战术在面对麦肯罗、莱德利、康纳斯，以及一些老将时并不奏效。等我终于进入状态，比赛早已结束。有一次，我甚至在比赛开始时就失去了前16分，比赛结束得如此之快，我几乎都不需要赛后洗个澡。真是残酷——我正在用最硬的方式学习。

The top players came expecting to have me for lunch, and they’d been thinking about taking that first bite since they found out I was on the menu. Four or five games to work up an appetite? They arrived ready to eat. The main course? Glazed Gilbert.

>顶级选手们来的时候，原本是想把我当午餐吃的，他们从得知我被列入菜单起，就一直在盘算着第一口该吃什么。四五个回合就能激起他们的食欲？他们来了，已经做好了大快朵颐的准备。主菜？焦糖吉尔伯特。

Being down a couple of breaks early, with no rhythm, no plan, no [[null|]]continuity, put me at too great a disadvantage. I was clobbered regularly by the smart guys on tour. They knew something I didn’t.

>开局就落后几个破发点，节奏全无，毫无计划，也没有连续性，这让我陷入了极大的劣势。我经常被巡回赛中那些聪明的选手们打得措手不及。他们掌握着我所不知道的东西。

>[!note]- 译者注：
此处“smart guys”意指技术高超、心理素质强的选手，中文中可译为“聪明的家伙”或“高手”，为保留原文语气，选择前者。

## 2 Start Your Match Before It Begins

>2 比赛尚未开始，先开始你的比赛

What I discovered by looking, listening, and losing was simple. The guys making money out there started honing in on their target (me, for example) before the target was even in sight. The smart ones were consciously and subconsciously reviewing information about the opponent ahead of them as soon as they knew who that player was. The process began hours before the match. The smart players wanted to seek and seize advantage as early as possible. And they wanted to do it in as many ways as possible. For them, one of the big opportunities was good mental preparation. And that means early mental preparation.

>通过观察、倾听和失败，我发现了非常简单的一个道理。那些赚到钱的选手们会在对手（比如我）甚至还未现身之前，就开始锁定目标。聪明的选手们一旦知道对手是谁，就会有意识或无意识地提前收集关于对手的信息。这个过程早在比赛前数小时就开始了。聪明的选手希望尽早寻找并抓住优势，而且他们希望用尽可能多的方式做到这一点。对他们来说，其中一个重要的机会就是良好的心理准备，而这意味着尽早进行心理准备。

## 3 When Does Your Warm-up Begin?

>3 你的热身什么时候开始？

Let me tell you when the warm-up doesn’t begin. It doesn’t begin when you arrive on the court. It may for your opponent, but it shouldn’t for you. A smart player starts to prepare for the match on the way to the match, or even before. The warm-up should continue on into the locker room and out onto the court.

>让我告诉你，热身什么时候开始。它并不是你在来到球场时才开始的。也许你的对手会在那时开始热身，但你却不应该如此。一个聪明的球员会在前往比赛的路上，甚至在比赛之前就开始准备。热身应该延续到更衣室，甚至继续到球场上。

The warm-up begins with your brain. Your mind is usually the last part of you to get activated (if it gets activated at all). Players stretch incorrectly for a minute, hit a couple of forehands, and three serves, and it’s “Let’s start.” They barely warm up the body, but that’s more attention than they give to their mental preparation. The mind is a terrible thing to waste, and tennis players waste it all the time.

>热身从你的大脑开始。通常，你的大脑是你最后被激活的部分（如果它真的被激活的话）。球员们花一分钟时间错误地拉伸，击几个正手球，发几个球，然后就说“我们开始吧”。他们几乎只对身体进行了热身，却给心理准备的注意力更少。大脑是个难以浪费的东西，而网球选手却总是把它浪费掉。

Get into the habit of evaluating your opponent and thinking about the match before you arrive at the court. If you drive to the match your car is the place where your warm-up begins. If you walk to the courts, then the sidewalk is where it happens. No matter what, your warm-up starts on the way to the match.

>养成在抵达球场前就评估对手、思考比赛的习惯。如果你开车前往比赛，那么你的热身就从车上开始；如果你步行前往球场，那么马路就是你的热身场所。无论怎样，你的热身都从前往比赛的路上开始。

>[!note]- 译者注：
此处“热身”不仅指身体上的准备，更强调心理上的准备和策略思考。

[[null|]]For me it can begin even earlier than that. The night before a match I’ll be in my hotel room thinking about the next day’s competition. I’ll actually play points out in my mind. I can see myself making shots and winning points. I visualize points we’ve played in the past. I’ll see myself making specific shots against that player. It’s almost like watching a videotape of segments of a match. In the morning I’ll continue the process.

That little five-minute warm-up you see before a match begins for the players on tour is probably misleading. It looks like we just trot out to the court with that big bag over our shoulder, hit for a couple of minutes, and start the match. For most of us the process has been going on throughout the day—hitting, stretching, loosening up, a massage, and most of all, that mental review.

>你在比赛中看到的那短短五分钟热身，其实很可能是个误导。看起来我们只是拎着那个大包走上球场，击球几分钟，就准备开赛了。但对大多数人来说，这个过程其实从一整天就开始了——击球、拉伸、放松，按摩，最重要的是那番心理回顾。

## 4 The Pre-Match Mental Checklist

>4 比赛前心理检查清单

Whether I won or lost to a player in our last match, I want to think about the reasons. How did I beat him? What does he do with his shot selection and pattern? Does he attack? Is he a Retriever? Does he serve big? What’s his return of serve like? Did I make mistakes against him last time? What kind and why? What shots are his best? His worst? Was he forcing me to do something that bothered me? Does he start strong and get too cautious on pressure points? Was it a close match? Were the points long? I review everything that pertains to my opponent’s game (as far as strokes and shot tendencies are concerned).

>无论我上一场比赛是赢了还是输了，我都想好好想想其中的原因。我是怎么击败他的？他的击球选择和击球模式是怎样的？他是主动进攻型的吗？他是“拾取者”（Retriever）吗？他的发球威力大吗？他的接发球怎么样？我之前跟他交手时有没有犯错？犯了什么错误？为什么？他哪些击球最出色？哪些最差？他有没有逼迫我去做一些让我不舒服的事情？他是不是一开始打得强势，但在关键分上变得过于保守？这场比赛是不是非常胶着？比分是不是一直拉锯？我回顾所有与对手打法相关的内容（尤其是他的击球方式和击球倾向）。

It is also important to consider the “personality” of the game your opponent produces. What does he do to affect the atmosphere, mood, or tempo of the match? Is she very slow between points? Does he get emotional? Does she protest a lot of calls? Is he great when he gets a lead, but not so great when he’s losing? Do your opponents give you a lot of small talk on changeovers, taking your mind off the match like McEnroe tried to do to me? Do they always show up ten minutes late? Do they rush through the warm-up and want to start the match as soon as possible?

>此外，还要考虑对手在比赛中所展现出的“个性”。他会在比赛中影响气氛、情绪或节奏吗？他之间分的过渡是否非常缓慢？他是否容易情绪化？她是否经常对裁判的判罚提出抗议？他在领先时表现非常出色，但在落后时却不太行？你的对手是否在换边时跟你聊很多闲话，让你分心，就像麦肯罗曾经试图对我做的那样？他们是否总是迟到十分钟？他们是否在热身时匆匆忙忙，希望尽快开始比赛？

[[null|]]Prepare yourself mentally for the “stuff” certain players bring with them into the match. I want to be mentally and emotionally set for the fast play of Andre Agassi or the deliberate methodical match tempo of Ivan Lendl. I want to be ready for the temperamental outbursts of Connors and McEnroe or the stonefaces of Michael Chang or Jim Courier. It makes a big difference to me because I’m better able to control my own game plan, tempo, and composure if I know what’s likely to be happening on the other side of the net. Believe me, it can make a huge difference, as you’ll see later.

## 5 The Game Plan

>5 比赛策略

This process of review will lead me right into the equally important process of planning my strategy:

>这个回顾过程将自然而然地引导我进入一个同样重要的环节——制定我的战略计划：

1\. What do I want to make happen?

>1\. 我想要什么结果？

2\. What do I want to prevent from happening?

>2\. 我想阻止什么发生？

By evaluating my opponent I start solidifying my own approach to the match. As I review their game style and strokes I’m preparing my basic game plan. If they broke down my backhand last time I’ll be thinking about how to prevent the same thing from happening this time. If their serve is weak I’m alerting myself and going over how to take advantage of that. I’m planning a specific approach for that specific player. All of this before I even see them at the court.

>通过分析对手，我开始逐步确立自己在比赛中的应对策略。当我回顾他们的打法和击球方式时，其实是在为自己的基本战术做准备。如果他们上次破了我的反手，我会思考如何避免再次发生；如果他们的发球较弱，我会提醒自己并仔细考虑如何利用这一点。我为每一个特定的对手制定具体的应对方案。这一切，甚至在我还未见到他们站在球场上之前就已经开始了。

## 6 Set Your Compass 设置你的指南针

Your body will try to do what your mind tells it to do. In this prematch review you’re programming your mind to give the body correct information once the match begins and things start happening quickly under fire. You’re setting the course you want to take to arrive at your destination. That destination is victory.

>你的身体会试图去做你的大脑告诉它要做的事。在赛前回顾中，你是在为你的大脑编程，让它在比赛开始后，当局势迅速升温、压力倍增时，能够向身体提供正确的信息。你是在设定你想要走的路线，以抵达那个目的地。那个目的地就是胜利。

[[null|]]In its most basic form your plan evolves as you answer these questions:

1\. What is my opponent’s best weapon?
>1\. 我的对手最强的武器是什么？

2\. Where is my opponent weak?
>2\. 我的对手哪里最弱？

3\. What is my best shot and how can I direct it at my opponent’s weakness?
>3\. 我最强的球是哪一种？我该如何将它击向对手的弱点？

4\. What can I do to keep my opponent away from my own weakness?
>4\. 我该怎么做才能让对手远离我的弱点？

Your pre-match effort creates a mental compass. You know where you want to go and how you’re going to get there. There may be detours along the way, your opponent may present some surprises, you may get lost, but the basic route is laid out in your head in advance and your mental compass keeps you on course. (Coming up I’ll show you how following this procedure helped me beat both Boris Becker and Jimmy Connors, in totally different ways.)

>你赛前的努力为你打造了一枚精神指南针。你知道自己想要去哪儿，也知道如何抵达。途中可能会有绕行，对手也许会出其不意地制造一些变数，你也许会迷失方向，但你大脑中早已规划好了基本的路线，这枚精神指南针会一直指引你前行。（接下来我将向你展示，正是通过这种方式，我分别以完全不同的方式战胜了贝克尔和康纳斯。）

Even if you tend to play the same people over and over (your tennis buddies) it is still important to zero in on the specific player for a particular day. Get your mind on that one player. If you’re playing each other regularly it can be even more advantageous to review and refine your tactics because you’ve got a backlog of information. That’s when it really starts to get interesting.

>即使你总是和同样的人打球（比如你的网球好友），在特定的一天里，仍然要专注于那个具体的对手。把你的注意力集中在这个球员身上。如果你经常和对方交手，那就更有必要回顾并完善你的战术，因为你已经积累了不少信息。这时候，比赛才真正变得有趣起来。

Watch the tour players. The great ones are very intent on getting off to a good start because they know that it can often set the tone for the entire match. Getting the other player under your thumb right out of the chute puts them in a “catch up” position. Sometimes they’ll recover. A lot of times they won’t. And that’s what you want to do to your opponent. As early as possible you want to force them to be considering the idea that “maybe it’s just not gonna be my day today.” It can happen very early, believe me. Your pre-match preparation and visualization can make you the one somebody else is trying to catch up to.

>观察职业选手的表现。那些顶尖选手非常注重开局，因为他们知道一个好的开局往往能为整场比赛定下基调。比赛一开始就把对手牢牢控制在自己的节奏中，能让他们陷入“追赶”的被动局面。有时候他们能扭转局势，但大多数时候他们不会。这就是你想要对对手做到的。越早越好，你要让对手开始思考“也许今天真的不是我的幸运日”。相信我，这种情况可能在比赛刚开始不久就发生。你的赛前准备和心理预演，可以让你成为别人想要追赶的对象。

You’re thinking, “Hey, Brad. Gimme a break! I’ve got a life. I can’t be thinking about tennis all the time.” That’s right. But what I’m talking [[null|]]about takes less than ten minutes of attention on the way to your match and then the effort of following a plan once you get there. Maybe you can squeeze that in for opponents you really want to beat. By giving yourself a good chance to start right you’re giving yourself a good chance to finish right. That’s worth the extra attention.

>你可能会想：“嘿，布拉德，行了吧！我还有生活要过，不能一直想着网球。”没错，但我要说的这些，只需要在前往比赛的路上花不到十分钟的注意力，然后到了现场按照计划去做就可以了。也许你还能挤出这点时间，去对付那些你特别想击败的对手。如果你能让自己在比赛一开始就处于正确的状态，那你就有更大的机会在最后也保持正确的状态。这额外的注意力是值得的。

Let me take you through my own mental preparation exactly as I’ve used it before important matches.

>让我带你详细了解一下我之前在重要比赛中所使用的心理准备方法。

## 7 Preparation on Tour: Becker and Connors (Different Strokes for Different Folks)

>7 巡回赛中的准备：贝克尔与康纳斯（因人而异）

When I started seriously using my pre-match opportunities in this way I started winning more often. Let’s say my opponent in the round of 16 at the U.S. Open was Boris Becker (because it was). My own mental review before I got to the locker room at Flushing Meadow would go like this:

>当我开始以这种方式认真利用赛前的准备机会时，我开始赢的次数更多了。假设我在美国公开赛16强赛中的对手是鲍里斯·贝克尔（因为确实如此），那么在我到达法拉盛草地网球场更衣室之前，我内心的回顾会是这样的：

Becker can attack my weak second serve. He knows how to exploit it for maximum effect. This is a polite way of saying he creams it. Therefore, I don’t want to let him see many seconds. That means I want to get my first serve in more consistently, to put a premium on reducing first serve faults. Obviously, I’ll have to hit with less abandon and take fewer risks on my first serve, but the reward will be that he doesn’t get the opportunity to make me eat my second serve.

>贝克尔可以攻击我的第二发球弱点。他知道如何最大程度地利用这一点。这是一种委婉的说法，意思是他会狠狠地击打我的第二发球。因此，我不想让他看到太多第二发球。这意味着我要更加稳定地发出第一发球，把减少第一发球失误放在优先位置。显然，我必须在第一发球时打得更保守，减少冒险，但这样做的回报是，他没有机会让我吃掉我的第二发球。

Also, I want to serve to his forehand, try and get him missing some forehand ground strokes. Why? I know that Boris has a great forehand. It anchors the rest of his game. But if he starts screwing up with it, everything else can suffer. He gets frustrated when the forehand doesn’t meet his expectations.

>此外，我还想用发球攻击他的正手位，设法让他在正手底线球上出错。为什么？我知道波比的正手非常出色，它是他整个打法的核心。但如果他在正手上频频失误，其他技术环节也会受到影响。当他发现正手球没有达到自己的预期时，就会感到沮丧。

I’ve learned (and tell myself again in the pre-match review) that when he starts to miss on the forehand side, the rest of his game can start to wobble. That’s when he’ll start screaming at himself in German. So I want to be sharp serving to his forehand without getting too ambitious, get a high percentage in.

>我学会了（并在赛前回顾时也不断提醒自己），当他开始在正手位失误时，他的整场比赛就会开始出现波动。这时他就会用德语对自己大喊大叫。因此，我想在发球时针对他的正手位保持敏锐，但不要过于冒险，争取打出高成功率的球。

Also, I should give him no pace. Boris will win any battle over who [[null|]]can hit the ball hardest. Without pace he still wants to hit the ball hard and often overhits as a result. Especially on the forehand side.

>此外，我不能给他任何节奏。鲍里斯会在谁击球更有力的较量中赢得一切。即使没有速度，他仍然想大力击球，结果常常出现过击。尤其是正手一侧。

Before I get to the court I go over all of that in my mental review. I also remind myself to try and open up the court on Boris by hitting intelligently to his forehand. This means that I know from past matches with him that when I hit wide to the forehand (either with my serve or a groundstroke) his tendency, or shot pattern, is to hit it crosscourt right back to my forehand. If it’s even a little short I’ll look to hit a forehand approach (my best weapon) down the line to his backhand. Then I’ll follow it in and camp near the alley.

>在我踏上球场之前，我会在脑海中回顾所有这些内容。同时，我也会提醒自己，要尝试通过聪明的击球方式，向波比的正手位施压。这意味着我从以往与他交手的经验中了解到，当我把球打到他正手位的外侧（无论是发球还是底线击球）时，他的习惯性反应，或者说击球模式，就是把球斜线回击到我的正手位。如果球稍微有点低，我就会考虑用我的最佳武器——正手截击球，沿着底线打到他的反手位。然后我会迅速跟进，靠近网前的边线区域。

So, my game plan when I serve should regularly follow this pattern: Serve wide to the Becker forehand. Look for an opportunity to hit a forehand approach shot to his backhand. Then follow it in and camp near the alley. That’s my 1–2–3 punch.

>因此，我发球时的战术应该按照这个模式来进行：发球到贝克尔的正手位宽处。寻找机会打一个正手切入球到他的反手位。然后跟进到网前，靠近边线。这就是我的一、二、三连击战术。

Two elements come into play with that combination that I’ve experienced in past matches. First of all, Boris is going to have to move from his forehand grip (under the racket) to his backhand grip (somewhat over the racket) while moving from the deuce court to the ad court on the full gallop. In the past I’ve seen that he doesn’t always do that well. It’s just a little glitch that shows up from time to time. And, if he does make the grip change and take a good cut at the ball his tendency is to go down the line —not all the time, but that’s his first choice.

>这种组合在我过去比赛中出现时，会涉及到两个因素。首先，波比在从发球区移动到接发球区的过程中，必须从正手握拍（拍面下方）转换为反手握拍（拍面略上方）。过去我曾观察到，他并不总是能很好地完成这个转换动作，这只是一个偶尔会出现的小失误。而且，如果他成功完成握拍转换并全力击球，他的第一反应通常是向直线方向击球——并非每次都是如此，但这是他的首选方式。

Watch him on the tube. You’ll see he favors that shot. What happens when he does? There I am camping near the alley. Bingo. Forehand volley to an open court. I’ve run that combination of shots successfully on Boris many, many, many times.

>看他打球的时候。你就会发现他偏爱那个球。他一打出这个球，会发生什么呢？我就在边线附近等着。 bingo（命中）。正手网前击球，打向一个空当的场地。我多次、多次、多次成功地用这个组合球对付过鲍里斯。

>[!note]- “Bingo” 是英语中表示“命中目标”的俚语，此处翻译为“命中”以传达其含义，同时保持口语化表达。

And, on his serve I tell myself, “Boris has a huge serve. Don’t try to do too much with it. Get the ball back in play. Make him play an extra ball or two each point. Keep the ball in play. That’s the key for me against Boris, making him run down an extra ball over and over again. Becker can get frustrated and I want to frustrate him. Obviously, if he’s swarming winners I’m in trouble, but I tell myself that in the match ahead I’ve got to stay in every point possible. Keep cool. Be patient. Don’t try to make things happen too quickly.

>而在他发球时，我会告诉自己：“博里斯的发球非常强势，不要试图在发球上做太多文章。只需把球回击到场上，让他每一分多打一两个球。保持球在场上，这是我在对阵博里斯时的关键策略，让他不断跑动去接那些额外的球。贝克尔很容易沮丧，而我则想让他感到沮丧。当然，如果他频频打出ACE球，我就麻烦了，但我会告诉自己，在接下来的比赛中，我必须尽可能地在每一分中保持主动。保持冷静，要有耐心，不要急于求成。”

## 8 [[null|]]Busting My Butt for Boris

>8 [[null|]]为博里斯拼命练球 /no_think

I also think about the “personality” of the competition and the competitor ahead. Boris Becker is like a thoroughbred. His physicality, power, and movement are so pure that it can be intimidating. He’s big and he plays big. I get myself ready for any Becker match by reminding myself, “Don’t be impressed. Don’t let his presence be overwhelming. Keep your eye on your game plan and not on his game.” I have to do that or I’d take one look at what Becker brings onto the court and retire. (And that’s important in your own tennis. Don’t be impressed until after the match. Never before.)

>我也常常思考对手和比赛本身的“个性”。比如博里·贝克尔，他就像一匹纯种赛马。他的体能、力量和移动都如此纯粹，甚至会令人感到压迫。他体型高大，打球也气势磅礴。每次面对贝克尔的比赛，我都会提醒自己：“不要被他所震慑，不要被他的存在感压倒。专注于自己的战术，而不是他的打法。”如果我不这么做，只需看一下贝克尔走上球场时带来的气势，我可能就会立刻退赛。（这一点在你自己的网球生涯中同样重要。不要在比赛前就被对方震慑，只有在比赛结束后才允许自己感到震撼。）

I want him to see me busting my buns for everything, throwing everything back. I want to show him that I’m going to compete on every single point of every single game if the match goes on for a week. I want him to believe that I will never give up. I want him to get the feeling that I’m not going away. That I’m permanent. I know that Boris can get frustrated out on the court. Against someone like Michael Chang this wouldn’t matter. With Becker it can be very important. He doesn’t have great patience if points, games, and the match drag on.

>我想让他看到我全力以赴、拼尽全力的样子，把所有东西都扔回去。我想让他明白，如果比赛拖到一周，我也会在每一分、每一局中全力以赴地竞争。我希望他相信我永远不会放弃。我希望他感受到，我不会轻易离开，我是个“常驻”的存在。我知道鲍里斯在场上很容易沮丧。但面对像迈克尔·张这样的对手，这并不会影响太多。可面对贝克尔，这却可能非常关键。他如果比赛拖得时间太长，比分胶着，就很容易失去耐心。

I know that if he gets impatient he’ll start missing shots because he’s trying to end it quick. As I mentally program myself for Becker’s game I’m getting ready to try to exploit both his physical and emotional tendencies and weaknesses.

>我知道，如果他变得不耐烦，就会急于结束比赛，从而开始频频失误。在我在心理上为贝克尔的打法做准备时，我正准备利用他身体和情绪上的倾向与弱点。

>[!note]- 译者注：
此处“mental programming”翻译为“心理上为……做准备”更符合中文表达习惯，同时保留了原文的战术思维过程。

My Mental Review and Game Plan for Becker
>我的心理回顾与比赛计划

1\. Increase my first serve percentage. Don’t try to hit aces or service winners. Don’t let him see many second serves.

>1\. 提高我的一发得分率。不要刻意去击出发球直接得分或发球抢攻。不要让他看到太多二发。

2\. Serve to his forehand regularly. Look for short crosscourt return. Approach down the line. Camp near the alley.

>2\. 经常发球到他的正手位。寻找短的斜线回球。上网向底线方向移动。靠近边线站位。

3\. Try to create mistakes on his forehand. Don’t give him any pace. Repeat: No Pace.

>3\. 尽量在他正手位制造失误。不要给他任何速度。重复：没有速度。

4\. Don’t try to do too much with my service return. Get it back. Make him hit another shot.

>4\. 别试图在接我的发球时做太多事情。把球接回去，让他再击一次球。

5\. Work hard on every point. Show Boris some hustle. He gets impatient.

>5\. 每一分都要全力以赴。让鲍里斯看看你的拼劲。他很容易不耐烦。

[[null|]]6\. Don’t be impressed with anything he does. Let him pound the ball as hard as he wants. But, make him pound it over and over! Always try and make him hit one more shot.

7\. Boris can get frustrated if things don’t go his way. Try and make him stay around longer than he wants to. If he starts screaming at himself in German he’s getting wobbly.

>7\. 如果事情不如博尔西所愿，他很容易就会沮丧。试着让他多待一会儿，比他原本想的更久。如果他开始用德语对自己大喊大叫，那就说明他开始动摇了。

>[!note]- 译注：此处“wobbly”原意为“摇晃的”，在此语境中意指“心态不稳定”或“情绪波动”，译为“开始动摇”更符合中文表达习惯。

Does it work? Yes. Does it work all the time? Yes. You won’t win every time, obviously. But my procedure will improve your chance of winning every time. And that’s all I’m trying to do when I play, improve my chance of winning.

>这方法管用吗？管用。它一直管用吗？也管用。显然，你并不是每次都能赢，但我的这套程序能提升你每次获胜的可能性。这就是我打球时唯一想做到的，就是提高我获胜的几率。

## 9 U.S. Open 1987—Grandstand Court  
Becker vs. Gilbert  
Round of 16

>9 美国公开赛 1987年——观众席球场  
贝克尔 vs. 吉尔伯特  
16强赛

I’ve used this plan very effectively against Becker. Most notably in the round of sixteen in the 1987 U.S. Open. At the time he was ranked number four in the world. I was still trying to crack into the Top 10.

>我曾用这个计划非常有效地对付过贝克尔。最显著的例子是1987年美国公开赛16强赛。当时他的世界排名是第四位，而我还在努力跻身世界前十。

Boris had rolled over me in the first set 6–2 and then won a tiebreaker for the second set. Now he was leading 3–0 in the third. I was very close to the point of no return and Boris knew it. He could end this match very soon.

>博里斯在第一盘以6比2轻松战胜了我，随后又在第二盘抢七中胜出。现在第三盘他以3比0领先。我几乎已经接近无法挽回的境地，博里斯也清楚这一点。他很快就能结束这场较量了。

Boris exudes so much confidence when he’s ahead that it can break down your belief in yourself. His hair gets redder. His eyelashes get so white they almost disappear against his pale skin. He’s physically bigger than me and he carries himself even bigger. A pure athletic aura surrounds him when he’s rolling, and you can feel it across the net. He has tremendous presence. When he’s running with a lead the confidence he displays goes beyond arrogance. He tells you with his body language that he knows he is better than the rest. Except I didn’t believe it.

>博里斯领先时表现出的自信如此强烈，以至于会动摇你对自己的信念。他的头发变得愈发红润，睫毛白得几乎在苍白的皮肤上消失不见。他个头比我高大，而且举止更显气派。当他奔跑时，身上笼罩着一种纯粹的运动员气质，这种气势能透过球网让你感受到。他气场强大，令人难以忽视。当他带着领先优势奔跑时，他所展现出的自信早已超越了自负。他通过肢体语言告诉你，他知道自己比其他人更优秀。只是，我当时并不相信这一点。

The third set continues with me serving. I hold to stay in the match at 3–1, but I knew if Boris holds to go up 4–1 I’m history. Then he makes a serious mental mistake. Boris seems to have a lapse in concentration. He plays a very casual service game, almost lethargic. He [[null|]]donates a couple of points with double faults and suddenly I’m back on serve at 2–3. There’s hope in my mind because of what I know (and have reviewed) about Becker. Suddenly I can see a way to win. Here’s how.

>第三盘我继续发球。我以3比1保住发球局，试图维持比赛的主动权，但我清楚地知道，如果贝克尔能将比分拉大到4比1，我基本上就完了。就在这时，贝克尔犯下了一个严重的心理失误。他似乎出现了注意力不集中的状况，发球局打得非常随意，几乎像是懒散地应付。他接连出现双误，送出几个关键分，比分突然变成了我以2比3重新取得发球权。我的内心燃起了希望，因为我清楚（并且反复研究过）贝克尔的弱点。突然之间，我仿佛看到了赢球的路径。具体是这样……

Our scheduled afternoon match had been delayed because of rain. As a result we started very late and in extreme heat and humidity. The temperature that Monday in New York was pushing 90°. On the grandstand court it felt like 190°. It was like playing on a muggy day in the jungle, a jungle that had jets flying low overhead every fifteen minutes. Concentration was difficult.

>我们原定的下午比赛因为下雨而推迟了。因此，我们开始得非常晚，而且是在极端的高温和湿度中。那周一纽约的气温已经高达90华氏度。在看台球场上，感觉就像有190华氏度。那感觉就像在湿热的丛林里比赛，而且每隔十五分钟，丛林上空还会飞过低空的喷气式飞机。专注起来非常困难。

Even though I don’t like hot weather I felt this could work in my favor. I knew that a few weeks earlier I had beaten Boris in Washington, D.C., in this same kind of weather when he fell apart in the last set. I felt that if I could somehow claw out of this third set it might have a very negative effect on Boris. It might get him thinking about our last match in hot humid weather and what had happened.

>虽然我不喜欢炎热的天气，但我觉得这可能对我有利。我知道几周前我在华盛顿特区的相同天气条件下曾击败过鲍里斯，当时他在最后一盘彻底崩溃了。我觉得如果我 somehow 能够从这盘第三盘中摆脱困境，可能会对鲍里斯产生非常负面的影响。这可能会让他回想起我们上一次在闷热潮湿天气中的比赛，以及当时发生了什么。

I believed he had already made the assumption that he was going to win in straight sets. If I could steal this set after he was so close to winning the match (up two sets with a 3–0 lead) he might get upset with himself. If I could make him play more tennis in these conditions he might get very upset with himself.

>我认为他已经假设自己会以直落三盘赢得比赛。如果我能在他在两盘领先（3-0）这样接近获胜的情况下偷走这一盘，他可能会对自己感到沮丧。如果我能让他在这样的状态下多打一些网球，他可能会对自己非常不满。

And I knew what that could mean. This became a great motivator. I honestly felt that I was nearing an opportunity to get into Becker’s mind, to upset his composure in a big way. And this opportunity existed because I was way behind. Or, more to the point, it existed because Boris was so close to winning the match that he could taste it.

>我知道这可能意味着什么。这成了我极大的动力。我真诚地感觉到，我正接近一个机会，去洞察贝克尔的内心，彻底打乱他的冷静。而这个机会之所以存在，是因为我落后太多。或者说，更准确地说，是因为博里斯已经非常接近赢下比赛，几乎能尝到胜利的味道。

If I could get him feeling that things were dragging on in this grandstand heat and humidity, the quality of his game would drop because the quality of his thinking would drop. I had seen it happen before. I told myself if I could win the third set I could win the match. Sound too optimistic? Here’s what happened.

>如果我能让他的感觉是，这场比赛在如此闷热的环境中拖拖拉拉地进行，他的比赛质量就会下降，因为他的思维质量也会下降。我以前见过这种情况。我告诉自己，如果我能赢下第三盘，就能赢下整场比赛。听起来太乐观了吗？接下来发生了这样的事。

At 2–3 I held serve to even the set at three games apiece. We’re now two hours and thirty minutes into the match. I’ve won three games in a row and it’s gotten his attention. Now, we each hold serve, but Boris is becoming increasingly unhappy and irritable. Twice he starts screaming [[null|]]something in German. I make a little joke and tell myself the translation is “My feet are burning up. I want to stick them in ice.” Boris picks up the pace and is playing more quickly. As though he wanted to end it as soon as possible.

>在2-3局时我成功保发，将局数扳成三局平手。现在比赛已经进行了两个小时三十分。我连赢三局，终于引起了他的注意。现在我们各自保发，但波比的神情越来越不愉快，情绪也变得焦躁不安。他两次开始用德语大声喊叫[[null|]]，我开个玩笑，心想他大概是在说：“我的脚好烫，我想把它们放进冰里。”波比加快了节奏，打法也变得更急躁，仿佛他只想尽快结束这场比赛。

His composure is changing. We each hold again. And again. Suddenly it’s 6–all and we’re in another tiebreak.

>他的镇定正在发生变化。我们再次发球，又再次发球。突然比分来到6-6，又进入了一个新的抢七局。

Boris seems to get himself together and grabs an early mini-break. He’s serving at 2–1. It’s exactly where I don’t want to be, down a mini-break in a tiebreaker that could send me back home. But Boris now plays two points that became crucial in the outcome of the match, both for what they did to the score and what they did to his composure.

>博尔西似乎逐渐找回状态，率先取得了一个小局优势。比分是2-1，他发球。这正是我不愿看到的局面，在抢七中落后一个局分，这可能意味着我将提前回家。但博尔西接下来的两个得分成为整场比赛结果的关键，不仅改变了比分，也影响了他的心态和冷静程度。

Serving at 2–1 in the tiebreak Boris double-faults. He puts me back on serve and I don’t even have to swing my racket. It’s a major error on his part. I believe it was brought on because he was getting impatient, rushing things just a little bit. He wanted to get the job done too quickly. Perfect.

>在抢七局以2-1领先时，鲍里斯出现了双发失误。他再次将发球权交还给我，我甚至不用挥动球拍。这是他的一大失误。我认为这可能是由于他变得不耐烦，做事有些仓促。他想尽快结束比赛。完美。

Then immediately another critical exchange at 2–2. Boris serves and attacks the net. As he moves to his right (just inches from the net), his feet slip out from under him and he crashes to the ground. I see him fall, and as he frantically struggles to get back up, I hit a backhand crosscourt lob that sends him racing toward the baseline. He doesn’t get there. At the service line his feet go out from under him again. And again his body slams into the court. This time he doesn’t get up.

>紧接着又是一次关键的得分点，比分是2-2。波比发球并上网强攻。当他向右移动（距离网线仅数英寸之遥）时，脚下一滑，整个人重重地摔在了地上。我看到他摔倒，他焦急地挣扎着想要爬起来，我则打出一记反手斜线吊高球，将他逼向底线。他没能到达那里。在发球线附近，他的脚再次失去平衡，身体再次重重地撞在场地上。这次，他没能站起来。

Becker is lying face down on the court screaming uncontrollably at himself in German. He’s fallen so hard that his gold watch has slid down from his wrist to around his fingers. He’s furious with himself and about what’s happening. He raises up to one knee and lets out a terrible scream. Nobody in tennis has a scream as fearful as Becker’s when he loses control. It’s the sound of pure and total anguish. It is beautiful to hear. Boris is coming apart.

>贝克尔面朝下躺在球场上，用德语对着自己尖叫不止。他跌得如此之重，金表从手腕滑落，几乎到了指间。他对自己愤怒不已，也对眼前发生的一切感到暴怒。他挣扎着撑起一条腿，发出一声令人毛骨悚然的尖叫。在网球界，没有人能在失控时发出比贝克尔更恐惧的尖叫。那声音纯粹而彻底地展现了痛苦。听来真是令人动容。鲍里斯彻底崩溃了。

At 3–2 in the breaker I serve again to his forehand. Boris nets it and immediately unleashes another bloodcurdling screech. This time I can’t hear him. Another huge jet is directly overhead and obliterates all other sound. It’s a very strange sensation. The court is actually vibrating from the jet’s noise. I can see Boris screaming, but I hear nothing except the [[null|]]huge noise of the jet. Boris takes a vicious swipe at the court with his racket. I know he feels like he’s drowning and can’t swim. No matter how hard he tries, he feels things are only getting worse.

>破发局3-2时，我又一次将球发至鲍里斯的正手位。鲍里斯将球击出界，随即发出一声令人毛骨悚然的怒吼。这次我却听不到他的声音。另一架巨大的喷气式飞机正飞越头顶，其轰鸣声完全盖过了其他一切声响。这种感觉非常奇怪。球场实际上因飞机的轰鸣而微微震动。我能看到鲍里斯在嘶吼，但我只听到那巨大的飞机轰鸣声。鲍里斯愤怒地挥动球拍，狠狠地抽打着球场。我知道他感觉自己仿佛在水中挣扎却无法游动。无论他如何努力，他都感觉情况只会越来越糟。

It’s interesting how distractions affect you when you’ve got some momentum and are winning points. The heat, the humidity, the incredible white noise of the jets flying overhead, none of it bothered me. But when you’re struggling like Boris was struggling it is almost impossible to stay focused. The distractions become crippling. Your mind gets totally berserk. You just want to get out of there.

>当你处于顺风阶段、正在赢分时，一些干扰因素对你产生的影响很有趣。烈日、高温、头顶飞机轰鸣的白噪音，这些都没能让我分心。但当你像波比那样陷入困境时，几乎就无法保持专注。干扰变得令人难以承受。你的大脑完全失控，只想赶紧离开那里。

>[!note]- “berserk” 翻译为“失控”更符合中文语境，传达出思维混乱、无法控制的状态。

We continue to 5–4 in the tiebreak on my serve. I remember my game plan is to go selectively to his forehand. And that’s what I do. Boris nets it, 6–4. Set point now, and again I decide to go to his forehand with my serve. Again he nets it! The tactic works beautifully.

>我们继续在抢七中以5比4领先，发球局由我掌控。我记得我的战术是选择性地打他的正手位。我就是这么做的。波利斯将球打到了网前，比分变为6比4。现在是赛点，我又一次选择用发球攻击他的正手位。他再次将球打到网前！这个战术效果极佳。

I’ve cracked through to win the breaker and with it the set 7–6 (7–4). Boris’s march to victory has been slowed. I’m still alive. More than alive. I know I can win the match.

>我终于突破了这一局，以7–6（7–4）赢下了这一盘。鲍里斯通往胜利的步伐被我放慢了。我还没死，而且远不止是活着。我知道，我有能力赢得整场比赛。
