It feeds on itself. Over the months and years a player lets the weaker side kind of wither on the vine. Lack of attention equals lack of effectiveness.

>你之所以会发现一名球员在多年比赛过程中，其弱项和强项之间出现明显差距，其中一个原因是：他们一直在追求击打自己喜欢的球，回避自己不喜欢的球。这种倾向会不断强化自身。数月、数年过去，球员的弱项便如同被忽视的藤蔓一般逐渐枯萎。缺乏关注就等于缺乏效果。

>[!note]- 译者注：
此处“withering on the vine”为英语习语，意指被忽视而逐渐衰败，译为“被忽视的藤蔓”既保留原意，又符合中文表达习惯。

In a match it might make sense to run around it. In the warm-up give the weaker side a little real attention. Among other things it will build up your confidence. You’ll get used to hitting it. And the warm-up is a regular, no penalty opportunity to work on it.

>在比赛中，也许绕过去会更合理。在热身时，给较弱的一侧多一些真正的关注。这样做不仅能增强你的信心，还能让你逐渐习惯击打这种球。而且，热身时间是一个没有惩罚、可以反复练习的机会。

## 6 Thirty Seconds: Overheads

>6 秒三十：上旋球

Overheads get ignored in the warm-up, especially by B and C level players. Here’s why. It may be the shot you hit worst and hate most. Nobody likes to look bad in front of their opponent. Nobody likes to look bad in front of themselves. That overhead can make you look real bad. It’s the most difficult shot to time right, especially when you’ve just started. So a lot of players just don’t want to mess with it. What do they do? They wait until it counts to hit their first overhead. This is not intelligent. They figure it’s just easier to skip it entirely. Don’t.

>在热身时，很多人会忽视过顶击球（overhead），尤其是B级和C级选手。这是为什么。这可能是你打得最差、最讨厌的球。没有人愿意在对手面前出丑，也没有人愿意在自己面前出丑。一个过顶击球可能会让你看起来非常糟糕。这是最难把握时机的球，尤其是在你刚开始的时候。因此，很多选手干脆就不想碰这个球。他们是怎么做的呢？他们等到真正需要的时候才去打第一个过顶击球。这样做并不明智。他们觉得干脆跳过这个球更简单。不要这样做。

The overhead warm-up does two things. Obviously, it warms up the overhead. But it also starts warming up your serve. It gets you doing all the things you need to be doing during the serve: looking up, following through, transferring your weight. It gives you just a little more time to bring the serve up to speed. Your objective is modest. Just make solid contact on the swing. Don’t worry about power. Don’t worry about great angles. Just take a relaxed cut at the ball and don’t get fancy.

>上旋球的热身动作有两个作用。显而易见，它能让你的上旋球动作热起来。但更重要的是，它也帮助你开始热身发球。它让你在发球时做所有必要的动作：抬头、跟进、转移重心。它给你一点额外的时间，让你的发球动作更流畅。你的目标很简单，就是在挥拍时做好扎实的击球动作。不必担心力量，也不必追求美妙的击球角度。只需放松地击球，不要刻意追求花哨的动作。

## 7 Fifteen Seconds: The Toss

>7 十五秒：抛球 /

>[!note]- 本节标题“Fifteen Seconds”意为“十五秒”，在网球比赛中指发球前的短暂准备时间，译为“十五秒”更符合中文表达习惯。

As the toss goes so goes your serve. When was the last time you actually practiced your toss? Before you serve, practice your toss four or [[null|]]five times. Just focus on doing it smoothly and putting the ball where you want it. Toss it up. Then catch it without moving your feet. It’s a great way to improve your serve with very little effort. (And during the match if your serve starts to give you trouble, slow things down and take a couple of practice tosses. I’ve found it helps to get things back on track. If your toss is all over the place it’s impossible to have a reliable serve.)

>发球的成败取决于抛球。你上一次真正练习抛球是什么时候？在发球前，练习抛球四到五次。只需专注于动作流畅，把球抛到你想要的位置。把球向上抛起，然后不移动双脚接住它。这是一种以极小的努力就能提升发球质量的好方法。（而且在比赛中，如果你的发球开始出现问题，不妨放慢节奏，做几次练习抛球。我发现这样做有助于迅速回到正轨。如果你的抛球杂乱无章，就不可能有稳定的发球。）

Finally, on this much overlooked but crucial part of the game, don’t be afraid to stop your service motion if the toss is terrible. Catch it! Toss it again. Club players will chase a bad toss into the locker room trying to salvage it.

>最后，关于这项常被忽视却至关重要的技术环节，不要害怕在发球动作进行中停下来，如果抛球非常糟糕的话。接住它！重新抛一次。普通球员会追着一个糟糕的抛球冲进更衣室，试图挽救局面。

>[!note]- 译者注：
此处“club players”指业余球员或普通球员，而非专业选手。

## 8 One Minute: The Serve

>8 分鐘：發球

I want you to hit four serves to both courts, both wide and down the center. Most club players tend to hit just to the deuce court. I want you to hit to both courts for this reason. The service warm-up is aimed primarily at setting up a good comfortable motion for yourself early in the match so you don’t needlessly double-fault.

>我希望你向两个发球区发四个发球，包括两侧边线和中路。大多数业余球员习惯只发向优势区（deuce court）。所以我希望你向两个发球区发球，就是为了打破这种习惯。发球热身的主要目的是在比赛初期就建立一个舒适、稳定的发球动作，这样你就不会无谓地出现双发失误。

Hit your initial serves with an easy, relaxed motion. Keep your wrist very loose—almost floppy. Hit your first three or four serves with almost a lazy motion. Aim for the service line or beyond. Then gradually increase velocity.

>用轻松、放松的动作击打你的第一发球。保持手腕非常松散——几乎是软塌塌的。用几乎懒洋洋的动作击打前几记发球，目标是发球线或更远的位置。然后逐渐加快击球速度。

>[!note]- 译者注：
此处“lazy motion”意为动作轻柔、不费力，而非真正懒惰，故用“懒洋洋”表达其轻松自然的状态。

You want to help yourself as much as you can for that first service game. You want to be comfortable with the stroke and you don’t want to be serving to the ad court for the first time during the match, so serve to it in the warm-up.

>你希望在第一盘发球局中尽可能帮助自己。你希望对自己的发球动作感到舒服，也绝不想在比赛中第一次发球就发向对方的发球区，因此在热身时就应先发向那个方向。

>[!note]- 译者注：
此处“ad court”指网球比赛中对方的发球区，因网球场地的“ad”（发球区）位置在右半场，故译为“对方发球区”。

## 9 Thirty Seconds: Service Return

>9 秒三十：发球接发球

When your opponent is hitting serves during the warm-up, don’t catch them, hit them back. Practice hitting their serve with a good service return. I believe it can be one of your most important weapons and it is seldom worked on. Work on it. And work on it in the warm-up. Hit some focused, rhythmic, and connected returns. Remember, this [[null|]]is probably the shot you’ll hit first (especially if you follow my later advice). The time to get it going is in the warm-up.

>当对手在热身时发球时，不要去接球，而是将球回击回去。练习用高质量的接发球回击他们的发球。我相信这可以成为你最重要的武器之一，但很少有人专门训练这项技术。要多加练习，尤其在热身时更要多练。击出一些专注、有节奏、连贯的回球。记住，这[[null|]]可能是你第一拍就要击出的球（特别是如果你按照我之后的建议去做的话）。要让这项技术进入状态的最佳时机就是在热身时。

That’s the “quick-and-slick-it’ll-do-the-trick” 7-minute-and-45-second Microwave Warm-up. Yes, it may take a couple of minutes longer if your opponent wants to hit some overheads or anything else. But your game, at least, will be ready to roll. Do this and you come out hot (or at least warm).

>这就是那种“快速简便、立竿见影”的7分45秒微波式热身。是的，如果对手想打一些高球或者其他动作，可能还需要多花个几分钟。但至少你的比赛状态已经准备就绪，随时可以开打。这么做，你就能一上来就火力全开（至少也是热身到位）。

## 10 Learn About Your Opponent: The Quick Study

>10 了解你的对手：速成学习

Another objective during this final stage of the pre-match opportunity is to see if your opponent is showing you anything about their game that you can use against them. This is important with someone you haven’t played before. Here’s how to do it.

>这一阶段的赛前准备的另一个目标是，观察对手是否在比赛中透露出一些可以用来对付他们的信息。这一点在你之前从未与之交手过的对手身上尤为重要。下面是具体做法。

Hit a ball down the middle. Which way do they step? Do they run around the backhand? If you’ve never seen your opponent before, they’ve just told you something; namely, they favor one side over the other.

>击球打向球场中央。他们是怎么移动的？是向后场跑动，绕到反手位吗？如果你之前从未见过你的对手，他们刚刚已经告诉你了一些信息；也就是说，他们更习惯用某一侧击球。

>[!note]- 译者注：
此处“favor one side over the other”意为“更倾向于使用某一侧”，在网球术语中，即“习惯性使用某一侧击球”。

When they’re at the net volleying, throw a surprise lob up. Do they cover smoothly? Are they quick or clumsy with their footwork?

>当他们网前截击时，突然打出一个出其不意的高球。他们能否顺畅地补位？他们的步法是敏捷还是笨拙？

Also, when they’re at the net, hit a ball low to their feet. Do they bend their knees or do they drop the racket head (the lazy man’s volley).

>此外，当他们跑到网前时，击一个低球到他们的脚边。他们是弯曲膝盖，还是把球拍头往下压（懒汉式的截击）。

>[!note]- 译者注：
此处“lazy man’s volley”是网球术语，指一种不规范、省力的截击方式，直译为“懒汉式的截击”既保留原意，又符合中文表达习惯。

During the rally pay attention to how they move and how they strike the ball when they’re moving. Are they sharp on one side and inconsistent on the other? Do they hit one shot more than another—slice, topspin, or flat? Do they take big, looping strokes or short, compact strokes? Remember the following Gilbert Golden Rule: The strokes that are prettiest in the warm-up are the ugliest under pressure. Never be too impressed by what you see in the warm-up. But do remember any little problems or glitches in their strokes or movement that you might want to exploit in the match.

>在接发球的对拉过程中，注意观察对手的移动方式以及他们在移动中击球的技巧。他们是单侧动作利索而另一侧不一致吗？他们是否更倾向于击某一类球——比如切削、上旋或平击？他们是用大而弧线型的击球动作，还是短而紧凑的击球方式？请记住吉尔伯特的黄金法则：在热身时看起来最漂亮的击球动作，在压力下往往会变得最丑陋。不要过于被你在热身时看到的击球表现所迷惑。但请务必记住他们在击球或移动中出现的任何小问题或小瑕疵，这些都可能在比赛中成为你可利用的弱点。



# [[null|]][[null|]]5

>[[空|]][[空|]]5

# Four “Nervebusters”: Overcoming Pre-Match Nervousness

>四招“神经杀手”：克服赛前紧张情绪

Finally, we deal with the issue of nervousness at the beginning of a match. You know what nerves are, don’t you? Nerves mean you’re afraid, you fear something. Do you know what fear is? It’s a defense mechanism. It’s your body’s way of gearing up for trouble. When your mind or body senses danger, fear is the natural way to get ready to deal with it—the “fight or flight” mechanism. The adrenalin starts pumping. The senses are heightened. All systems are go! Only sometimes they don’t.

>最后，我们来谈谈比赛开始时的紧张情绪。你了解什么是紧张吗？紧张意味着你害怕，你担心某些事情。你知道恐惧是什么吗？恐惧是一种防御机制，是你的身体在准备应对麻烦的一种方式。当你的大脑或身体察觉到危险时，恐惧就是一种自然的准备反应——“战斗或逃跑”机制。肾上腺素开始涌动，感官变得敏锐，所有系统都已就位！不过，有时候它们却并不奏效。

It’s natural to feel nerves before a big match, whether the cause is a certain player you really want to beat or a tournament you’ve entered. In those situations, if you don’t feel nerves you’ll lose. The quality players are helped by nerves because they use the good part and control the bad part. They know that the energy from nerves can work for you, making you try harder, focus sharper, and do more. That’s the good part. The bad part can cause damage.

>在一场重要比赛前感到紧张是很自然的事情，无论这种紧张是因为你特别想击败某个对手，还是因为你参加了一个重要的赛事。在这种情况下，如果你没有紧张感，那你反而会输掉比赛。真正优秀的选手之所以能从紧张中受益，是因为他们懂得利用紧张带来的正面作用，同时又能控制它的负面影响。他们清楚紧张所释放的能量可以为你所用，促使你更加努力、专注度更高、表现更出色。这就是紧张的积极面。而消极面则可能带来伤害。

The damage is major, too. Uncontrolled nervousness makes your legs heavy and your feet stick to the ground. Nerves take the oil out of your strokes. They freeze you up. Ever hear about “The Elbow”? Nerves. And it happens at all levels of tennis. Maybe most of all among the pros, because of the stakes involved.

>伤害可不小。无法控制的紧张感会让你双腿沉重，双脚仿佛粘在地面上。紧张会抽走你击球的流畅度，让你僵住。你听说过“肘部”（The Elbow）吗？就是紧张。这种状况在网球的各个级别都会发生，也许在职业选手中尤为明显，因为他们的比赛压力最大。

Nerves can make you stop thinking on the court and try stupid shots. A nervous player will kick himself afterward and say, “ Why did I try that drop shot from behind the baseline? ” Nerves, that’s why. Nerves can make you really brain dead. What was easy to do suddenly isn’t so easy.

>紧张会让你在球场上停止思考，做出愚蠢的击球动作。一个紧张的球员事后往往会责怪自己，说：“我为什么要从底线后方尝试那记挑高球？”就是因为紧张啊。紧张会让你大脑一片空白。原本简单的事情，突然变得困难起来。

Maybe the worst case I ever saw happened to Miloslav Mecir. He [[null|]]got “The Elbow” big time. During a World Team Cup match he was serving to Connors 5–3 in the third set to win the match and the title for Czechoslovakia. All he had to do was hold serve. Nerves got him. He started double-faulting. By the end of his service game he had double-faulted four times! During his next service game (at 5–all) he started faulting again. It got so bad he started serving underhand on second serves just to get the ball in. There was no other way he could get it in the box. It was painful to watch because you felt so sorry for the guy. He was a great player, but nerves took their toll on him and he lost the match because he couldn’t control them.

>也许我见过的最糟糕的情况发生在米洛斯拉夫·梅奇（Miloslav Mecir）身上。他当时真的“吃了肘子”（The Elbow）。在一次世界团体赛中，他以3:5领先对手康纳斯（Connors），在决胜盘中为捷克斯洛伐克队争夺冠军。他只需要保住发球局就能赢下整场比赛。然而，紧张情绪击垮了他，他开始频频双误。到了他的发球局结束时，他已经出现了四次双误！接下来的发球局（比分是5:5），他再次失误频频。情况糟糕到他甚至开始用反手发球来接第二发球，只为把球发进界内。因为除此之外，他已经没有别的办法能将球发进发球区了。看他的比赛实在令人心疼，因为你会为他感到惋惜。他是一名优秀的球员，但紧张情绪最终压倒了他，他无法控制自己的情绪，最终输掉了比赛。

>[!note]- “The Elbow” 是一种比喻说法，指球员在比赛中因紧张或失误而“被肘击”（即遭遇严重挫折），这里采用中文中类似的表达方式“吃了肘子”来传达原意。

I’ve had my own experience with nerves. I played Davis Cup for the United States in ’86 when we lost three to two to Australia. And I lost both of my matches. It was a horrible feeling. I was depressed and embarrassed. Inside I felt like I’d let my country down, and nerves were a big part of it. I had wanted to win for the U.S.A. so much that I was tight, nervous, anxious, wanting to end points too soon. Nerves will do that.

>我也有过紧张的经历。1986年，我代表美国参加了戴维斯杯，最终我们以2比3输给了澳大利亚。我参加了两场比赛，结果都输了。那种感觉非常糟糕。我感到沮丧和羞愧，内心觉得自己辜负了国家，而紧张在其中扮演了很大角色。我如此渴望为美国赢得胜利，以至于我变得紧绷、焦虑、紧张，甚至急于结束每一分。这就是紧张带来的后果。

That experience was my worst in tennis, because the losses laid on me for three years. That’s a long time to wait to redeem yourself. When I finally got invited back I had learned a lot. I won because I had a ferocious desire to prove myself and I was much better at handling nerves. Let me tell you what I do.

>那次经历是我打网球最糟糕的一次，因为那三年的失利一直压在我身上。三年时间等待东山再起，实在太漫长了。当我终于再次受邀参赛时，我已经学到了很多。我之所以能赢，是因为我有强烈的愿望要证明自己，而且我更能控制紧张情绪。让我告诉你我是怎么做到的。

During the warm-up nerves will start affecting you immediately. Your feet don’t want to move. You can’t catch your breath. Your movement is stiff. And so are your strokes. I have four very simple and mechanical things I do to loosen the grip of nerves before a match.

>在热身时，紧张感会立刻影响到你。你的脚不想动，喘不过气来，动作变得僵硬，击球也变得生硬。我有四个非常简单且机械性的方法，可以在比赛前缓解紧张感。

## 1 Gilbert’s Nerve Busters

>1 格利尔的“神经轰炸”术

## 2 1\. Breathe Like You’ve Got Asthma

>2 1\. 呼吸仿佛你得了哮喘

When you get nervous your breathing pattern changes. It’s the first thing you should check. Your breathing tells you about the level of your anxiety. And, more important, your breathing can help you control your [[null|]]anxiety. When you’re nervous you take short, shallow, quick breaths. And that’s exactly how you feel. Everything is jumpy, racy, and out of sync when you get nervous.

>你一紧张，呼吸模式就会发生变化。这是你应该首先检查的。你的呼吸能告诉你焦虑的程度。更重要的是，你的呼吸还能帮助你控制焦虑。当你紧张时，你会呼吸得又短又浅，而且很快。而正是这种感觉，让你觉得一切都不对劲——紧张时，整个人都变得慌乱、兴奋，节奏也乱了。

When that happens, control your breathing. Take smooth, rhythmic, deep breaths. You can start even before you’re on the court. Before a match that really has me tight I make sure that I’m breathing correctly—deep, smooth, rhythmic. I sound like I’ve got asthma, but it helps reduce my nervousness.

>当这种情况发生时，控制你的呼吸。深呼吸，节奏平稳，有规律地呼吸。你甚至可以在踏上球场之前就开始这么做。在那些让我非常紧张的比赛中，我一定会在比赛前确保自己呼吸正确——深、平稳、有节奏。听起来好像我得了哮喘，但这样做能有效缓解我的紧张情绪。

## 3 2\. Get Happy Feet

>3 2\. 欢快脚步

Let me tell you about footwork. It is the most important part of the game. It’s everything. And nerves can destroy it. Good footwork gets you to the right position in time. If you’re there in time you have options. If you have options you have a better chance of winning the point. You can slice the ball. You can hit over the ball. You can hit it early. But mainly you can hit it squarely. Options. The more options you have the less likely you are to be attacked effectively and the more likely you are to control the point. That’s what good footwork can do for you. Here’s what bad footwork can do to you.

>让我来谈谈步法。它是比赛中最重要的部分，一切的关键所在。而紧张情绪却会摧毁它。良好的步法能让你及时到达正确的位置。如果你及时到位，你就有选择的余地。有了选择，你就更有可能赢得这一分。你可以削球，可以击球过网，可以提前击球，但最主要的是你可以正拍击球。这就是选择。你拥有的选择越多，就越不容易被对手有效攻击，就越有可能掌控这一分。这就是良好的步法能为你做到的。而糟糕的步法却可能对你造成什么影响呢？

If you have bad footwork and get to the ball late, or wait for the ball to come to you (or don’t have yourself in the best position to hit the shot you want), you have fewer options. The ball is playing you. If it’s tight to your body all you can do is fight it off. Bad footwork hurts your timing, your balance, your power, and your consistency.

>如果你的步法不好，总是慢半拍，或者等着球飞到你面前（或者你并没有处于最佳位置去击打你想要的球），那么你的选择就会变少。球在掌控着你。如果球紧贴着你的身体，你只能硬着头皮去击打。糟糕的步法会破坏你的节奏、平衡、力量和稳定性。

>[!note]- 译者注：
此处“ball is playing you”意为“球在主导局面”，译为“球在掌控着你”更符合中文表达习惯。

Pre-match tension can absolutely kill footwork. Nerves make your feet stick to the court like you’ve got Velcro on the bottom of your shoes. Nerves make you stop moving. Your legs get sluggish. Short, quick, light steps become lunging, slow, big steps. And it goes right through your body. The strokes are bad. The timing is bad. The contact is bad. And your confidence evaporates because you can sense that “I’m just not hitting the ball today.” Bad footwork.

>赛前紧张绝对会毁掉你的步伐。紧张会让你的脚像鞋底粘了魔术贴一样牢牢粘在球场上。紧张会让你停止移动，双腿变得迟钝。原本短促、轻快、灵活的步伐，会变成大步跨出、动作迟缓、步伐沉重。这种状态会从脚一直蔓延到全身。击球质量变差，节奏失控，触球也不够精准。你的信心也随之消失，因为你隐约感觉到“今天就是打不好球。”糟糕的步伐。

How do you get good footwork? Simple. Think “toes.” If you stay on your toes (the balls of your feet) you have to be taking light, quick, short steps. Heels are for digging in, for carrying the load, for holding [[null|]]your ground. Being on your heels is how you get passed in tennis. And nerves put you back on your heels quicker than anything else. So when it comes to good footwork, think like a ballet star—get up on your toes.

>如何才能拥有良好的步法？很简单。想“脚尖”。如果你始终站在脚尖上（即脚掌部位），你就必须迈轻、快、短的步子。脚跟是用来发力、承担重量、保持防守位置的。在网球中，站稳脚跟意味着你被对手突破了。而紧张感会让你比任何事情都更快地回到脚跟上。因此，谈到良好的步法，你就要像芭蕾舞者一样思考——站上脚尖。

I consciously tell myself to move my feet. It’s an order I give to myself when I’m nervous. “Bounce. Get up on your toes, Brad. Stay light on your feet! Bounce.” I keep telling myself to bounce, to stay light, to keep moving. It helps me work into good footwork and out of nervousness. I want happy feet, not heavy feet.

>我有意识地告诉自己要动脚。这是我在紧张时给自己下的指令。“跳起来，布拉德，站上脚尖，保持轻盈！跳！”我不断告诉自己要跳，要轻盈，要持续移动。这有助于我进入良好的步法，摆脱紧张情绪。我想让双脚快乐，而不是沉重。

Movement reduces nerves. Lack of movement increases nerves. You’ve heard people say, “He got nervous and froze up.” They’re not talking about body temperature. They’re talking about movement, or lack of it.

>运动能缓解紧张，缺乏运动则会加剧紧张。你一定听过人们说：“他紧张得僵住了。”他们不是在说体温升高，而是在说运动，或者缺乏运动。

You’ll notice in your own game that when you’re feeling good, when you’re confident and ahead, you move naturally and smoothly. But mainly you move. Same with the pros. When they’re relaxed they don’t just stand there leaning back on their heels. They’re up and bouncing, moving.

>你很快就会在自己的比赛中注意到，当你感觉良好、信心十足并且领先时，你的动作会自然流畅。但更重要的是，你一直在移动。职业选手也是一样。当他们放松时，不会只是站着倚在脚后跟上，而是会跃跃欲试、不断移动。

It’s the player who’s tight, afraid, and tied up by tension (or just exhausted) who stands there flatfooted. And you can fight the tendency to freeze up when you’re nervous by reminding yourself (ordering yourself) to get up on your toes and be light on your feet.

>就是那个紧张、害怕、被压力束缚住（或者只是筋疲力尽）的球员，才会站在那儿双脚沉重。而当你感到紧张时，要想办法克服那种僵住的倾向，提醒自己（命令自己）要踮起脚尖，轻盈地移动。

Remember this not only during the warm-up, but in the pressure points of the match. Watch Courier before a big point. Watch him shift from foot to foot, knees bent, bouncing on his toes. Is he nervous? You bet. But he knows how to deal with it. The good ones never just stand there. They fight nerves and stay loose by moving. And so should you. Talk to yourself. Tell yourself, “Toes not heels.”

>记住这一点不仅在热身时，更要在比赛的关键时刻。在大比分关键分到来之前，观察科里尔的表现。看他如何在双脚之间来回移动，膝盖微屈，脚尖轻轻弹动。他紧张吗？毫无疑问。但他知道如何应对。真正优秀的人从不会只是站在原地不动。他们会与紧张作斗争，通过移动保持放松。你也应该如此。对自己说话，告诉自己：“脚尖，不是脚跟。”

There is another benefit to doing this. It is good body language. It tells your opponent that you’re loose (even if you’re not). It tells them you’re not tired (even if you are). It tells them you’re confident and positive (even if you aren’t).

>这样做还有一个好处。它能展现出良好的身体语言。它告诉对手你很放松（即使你其实并不放松）。它告诉对手你并不疲劳（即使你确实很累）。它告诉对手你充满信心且积极乐观（即使你其实并非如此）。

>[!note]- 译注：此处“good body language”译为“良好的身体语言”既保留原意，又符合中文表达习惯。

In addition to the good it does your own game it works on your opponent’s mind. Especially late in the match. You’ve been in a battle for three sets. Your opponent is looking for some sign of fatigue, or nerves, or resignation in you. They look across the net and see the opposite. [[null|]]You’re up (on your toes)! You’re energized (or at least look like you are)! You’re bouncing lightly from foot to foot. Your body language is telling them that you’re ready and eager to fight to the end. Believe me, it does have an effect not only in the beginning of a match, but during it.

>除了对自身比赛有好处外，这种状态还会对对手的心理产生影响，尤其是在比赛后期。你们已经鏖战三盘，对手正试图寻找你身体或精神上的疲态、紧张或放弃的迹象。但他们望向网的另一边，看到的却是截然不同的画面：[[null|]]你站得笔直（脚尖点地）！你精神抖擞（至少看起来是这样）！你轻盈地在双脚之间跳跃。你的肢体语言正向他们传递出你准备战斗到底、充满斗志的信息。相信我，这种状态不仅在比赛开始时有效，在比赛进行中同样能产生影响。

## 4 3\. Read the Label

>4 3\. 看清楚标签

During the initial part of the warm-up, when I’m anxious and trying to settle down, there’s another little trick I use to fight nerves. I read the print on the ball. I try to see “Wilson” or “Penn” or “Slazenger” as the ball is coming at me. You’ll find that trying to see the writing not only helps get your eyes tracking the ball, but it gets your mind off nerves and onto something else. At first, you probably won’t see anything. Your eyes will improve and you’ll start seeing a blur. If that’s as far as you get, fine. What matters is that you’ve taken your mind off a problem by distracting it. Nerves aren’t what you’re thinking about. The label is what you’re thinking about.

>在热身的最初阶段，当我感到紧张、努力让自己平静下来时，我会使用另一个小技巧来缓解紧张情绪。我会看球拍上的字样。当球朝我飞来时，我努力辨认出“Wilson”、“Penn”或“Slazenger”这几个字。你会发现，尝试看清这些字不仅有助于让你的眼睛跟上球的轨迹，还能让你的思绪从紧张中转移出来，专注于其他事情。一开始，你可能什么都看不见，眼睛还没适应，只能看到一片模糊。如果你只能做到这一步，也没关系。重要的是，你已经通过分散注意力，将思绪从问题上转移开了。紧张并不是你所想的，而是那些标签——那些字——才是你所关注的。

## 5 4\. Sing a Song

>5 4\. 唱首歌

This one sounds a little strange. I distract myself from nerves by singing under my breath early in the warm-up. I’ll get a Tom Petty song going through my head and sing the lyrics to myself. I’m just trying to keep everything calm and easy to start with. And believe it or not, this little trick works for me. So when you go out on the court and you feel that pre-match tightness, remember my four Nerve Busters:

>这听起来有点奇怪。我通常在热身开始时就用哼歌的方式来缓解紧张情绪。我会在脑海中播放一首汤姆·佩蒂的歌，然后自己轻声哼唱歌词。我只是想让一切从轻松开始，保持平静。相信你可能会觉得难以置信，但这个小技巧对我真的有效。所以当你踏上球场，感到赛前那种紧绷的时候，记住我的四个“紧张粉碎机”：

1\. Breath like you’ve got asthma

>1\. 呼吸仿佛你得了哮喘

2\. Get happy feet

>2\. 保持快乐的双脚

3\. Read the label

>3\. 看标签

4\. Sing a song

>4\. 唱首歌

You may think it’s amazing that I can even hit the ball with all that breathing, bouncing, reading, and singing going on. I use it selectively. Mainly, I’m checking myself out to see how I’m moving and whether [[null|]]nerves are a problem that day. If I need to help myself get through nerves during a warm-up or during a match, I’ll go to this list, starting with my breathing and my footwork. I want to get them under control immediately, before they cost me points.

>你可能会觉得奇怪，我一边呼吸、跳跃、观察、还哼着歌，一边击球，怎么可能做到呢？其实我只是有选择地使用这些方法。主要目的是检查自己当天的移动状态，以及紧张是否会影响我的发挥。如果我在热身或比赛中需要克服紧张情绪，我会立刻调出这个清单，从呼吸和步伐开始，先将它们控制住，这样它们才不会在我比赛中造成失分。

## 6 Your Mind Can’t Think of Two Things at Once

>6 你的大脑一次只能专注于一件事

Obviously nerves reduce your effectiveness during the match as well as before the match. When I feel pressure during play there is something else I consciously do to reduce my tension.

>显然，紧张情绪会在比赛期间以及赛前降低你的表现效果。当我比赛中感到压力时，我会有意识地采取另一种方式来缓解自己的紧张情绪。

I know that nerves get worse when I think about them, when I start worrying about the consequences. I reduce that tendency by really focusing on my game plan at pressure points in the match: What am I trying to do with this serve? What am I looking to take advantage of in my opponent’s shots? I stay focused on my game plan. I divert my attention away from nerves and toward the next point (where it should be anyway!). By thinking about that, I’m not thinking about nerves.

>我知道，当我开始担心后果时，紧张感会变得更糟。为了减少这种倾向，我在比赛中关键点上会专注于自己的战术计划：我这一记发球想要达到什么目的？我想要利用对手哪些击球的弱点？我保持专注，专注于自己的战术计划。我把注意力从紧张感上转移开，放到下一个得分点上（这本来就应该如此）。当我这样思考时，我就没有在想紧张。

## 7 The Connors Approach to Nerves

>7 康诺斯应对紧张的方法

There’s one other approach to nerves that you can consider, but it’s only worked for one player that I know of: Jimmy Connors. He has a unique attitude about pressure. His perspective is this: Pressure usually represents opportunity. The more pressure the more opportunity. And he loves opportunity.

>还有一种应对紧张的方法，你可以考虑，但据我所知，只有一个人成功运用过，那就是吉米·康纳斯。他对待压力有独特的态度。他的观点是这样的：压力通常意味着机会。压力越大，机会越多。而他特别喜欢机会。

He figures, how much opportunity can there be playing the finals of the East Podunk/Fred and Millie’s Donut Shop and Radiator Repair Tennis Tournament? Not much. No fame, fortune, or flattery. How much pressure? Not much. On the other hand, how much opportunity is there playing the finals of the U.S. Open? Maximum. Careers are made there. Legends are created there. How much pressure? Maximum.

>他想，能在东蒲墩（East Podunk）/弗雷德和米莉的甜甜圈店及散热器修理网球赛决赛中打球，能有多少机会？不多。没有名声、财富或赞美。压力有多大？也不大。另一方面，能在美国公开赛（U.S. Open）决赛中打球，又会有多少机会？那是最大的机会。职业生涯往往在那里诞生，传奇人物也往往在那里成形。压力有多大？那是最大的压力。

Jimmy just sees the opportunity. He doesn’t focus on the pressure. He knows that in the most pressurized situations he can do himself the greatest good in terms of his career. And he really got himself jacked up when something big was at stake. Match point in a Slam event? He [[null|]]couldn’t be having more fun. It just came natural for him. For the rest of us it’s not so natural. Try my Nerve Busters. They work.

>吉米只看到机会，他不会去想压力。他知道在最紧张的情况下，对自己职业生涯来说，最有益的其实是自己。而且，当有重大分胜负的时候，他反而更加兴奋。在大满贯赛事的赛点上，他[[null|]]快乐得不得了。对他来说，这一切都那么自然。对我们其他人来说就不那么自然了。试试我的“神经爆破术”，它们真的有效。

## 8 Ready for the Contest

>8 准备迎战

Now you’re ready to begin. You’re ready to take advantage of your opponent immediately when the match gets going. You’re ready to jump off to a dominating start; maybe up a break, with good rhythm, good concentration, a focus on your game plan, and confidence. Now let the games begin.

>现在你已经准备好了。当比赛开始后，你可以立刻抓住对手的弱点。你已经准备好从一开始就占据主导地位：也许领先一个破发，节奏良好，注意力集中，专注于你的战术计划，充满信心。现在，比赛正式开始了。

Next: A smart start to your match.

>下一部分：如何智慧地开启你的比赛



# [[null|]][[null|]]6

>[[null|]][[null|]]6

# Start Smart: Grabbing the Early Lead

>聪明开局：抢占先机

Mental Preparation, Tools of the Trade, Stretching for Success, Winning the Warm-up—everything you’ve read in those chapters so far gives you the foundation for the best possible opening to the match, and one that carries forward into the competition. You’ll start strong, physically and mentally primed to find a quick break and a pattern that can give you a substantial edge for winning the first set. You are creating positive momentum for yourself.

>心理准备、训练工具、拉伸以求成功、赢得热身——到目前为止，你所读过的这些章节为你奠定了赢得比赛最佳开局的基础，这种开局也将延续到整个比赛中。你将从一开始就处于强势状态，身体和精神都做好了准备，迅速取得破发并找到一种能让你在首盘占据明显优势的节奏。你正在为自己创造积极的势头。

I know that if I can get off to a good early start in the match it improves my chances for winning the first set. Let me show you why statistically this is so important to me and why it can give you a real advantage.

>我知道，如果我在比赛中能有一个良好的开局，就能提高我赢下第一盘的概率。让我告诉你为什么从统计学角度来看，这一点对我如此重要，以及为什么它也能为你带来实实在在的优势。

According to the A.T.P. (Association of Tennis Professionals) a Top 20 player who wins the first set will go on to win the match 89.6 percent of the time! At the U.S. Open when Jimmy Connors has won the first set he has won the match eighty-eight out of ninety times! Winning the first set, then, carries significant weight. And getting off to a strong early start significantly boosts my chances of winning the first set. That’s why I’m so conscientious about preparing for the beginning of a match. It’s why you should do the same.

>根据ATP（职业网球联合会）的统计，排名前20的选手如果赢下首盘，赢得整场比赛的概率高达89.6%！在美国公开赛上，当吉米·康纳斯赢下首盘时，他几乎总是能赢得整场比赛——90次中有88次！由此可见，赢下首盘具有非常重要的意义。而一开场就占据主动，会大大提升我赢得首盘的概率。这就是为什么我对每一场比赛的开局准备如此认真，也希望大家能同样重视这一点。

## 1 Hit the Deck Running

>1 立即扑向底线

>[!note]- 这里“hit the deck running”是网球术语，意指球员在击球后迅速向底线方向移动，准备接应下一球。

This next step is the point of transition from the pre-match preparation into the heart of the contest. I consider the first two games to be included in this transition. I use this period to gain an edge that may be [[null|]]ignored by the other player. I’m looking for those moments in a match where my opponent is probably asleep at the wheel or doesn’t understand and exploit the dynamics of the situation.

>接下来的这一步，是比赛准备阶段向比赛核心部分过渡的关键点。我认为前两局应该包含在这个过渡阶段中。我利用这段时间获取一种优势，这种优势可能是对手所忽视的。我寻找比赛中那些对手可能反应迟钝、或者不理解并无法利用局势动态的时刻。

At the top levels of tennis this doesn’t happen often, but as you go through the ranks more and more opportunities are overlooked. The difference between a Top 10 player and a player trying to break into the Top 25 isn’t tennis strokes. It’s tennis smarts, the mental part of the game. I found that out in my own journey from the tennis wilderness into the Top 10. For recreational tennis players it’s the same. Lots of opportunities get ignored because a player’s mental game is weak.

>在职业网球的顶尖水平上，这种情况并不多见，但随着你不断晋级，越来越多的机会会被忽视。顶级前10名球员与试图打入前25名的球员之间的差距，并不在于网球技术动作，而在于网球智慧，也就是这项运动的“心理层面”。我在从网球荒原走向前10名的个人旅程中，就深刻体会到了这一点。对于业余网球选手来说，情况也是一样的。很多机会被忽视，是因为球员的心理素质不够强大。

The “transitional” period presents opportunity. Several different elements are present for the first and only time and they can be an asset or a liability depending on how you approach them. Very likely your opponent doesn’t recognize them. And if they do they aren’t sure how to capitalize on them.

>“过渡期”提供了机会。在这一阶段，会出现几种不同的要素，而这几种要素只会在这一阶段出现一次。它们对你来说可能是资产，也可能是负担，这取决于你如何应对。很可能你的对手并不意识到这些要素的存在。即使他们意识到了，也未必清楚该如何加以利用。

>[!note]- 译注：此处“过渡期”指比赛中某一特定阶段，例如发球局或接发球局的转换时刻，译文保留原意并符合中文表达习惯。

Before you’re caught up in the heat of the battle you’ve got this in-between period where it’s valuable to know what to do to gain advantage.

>在你真正陷入比赛激烈对抗之前，有一个中间阶段，这时候知道该做什么才能占据优势是非常有价值的。

How to Get the Best Possible Start

>如何获得最佳开局

1\. Never serve first.

>1\. 别先发球。

2\. Start your match like Ivan Lendl.

>2\. 以伊万·伦德尔的方式开局 /no/

3\. Play the first two games correctly.

>3\. 正确打好前两盘。

4\. Utilize the first pit stop.

>4\. 利用第一个换边休息机会。

## 2 1\. Never Serve First

>2 1\. 永远不要先发球

Here’s a bet you can win 90 percent of the time with your tennis friends: “What choices does the player who wins the spin of the racket (or toss) have?” Most of the time they will say the answer is to serve/receive or select which side to begin the match on. Wrong! There is one other option. It is to give the first choice to the other player. Knowing all of this and how to use it can be important. As you’ll see.

>你可以在和网球朋友打赌时，有90%的机会赢：「赢得球拍旋转（或抛掷）的一方球员有哪些选择？」大多数人会回答说，选择发球/接发球或选择先发球的一方。错了！还有一个选项：把第一选择让给对方球员。知道这一点并懂得如何运用它，是非常重要的。这一点你很快就会明白。

Most club or public courts players like to win the spin of the racket so they can elect to serve first. I believe that you should almost always choose [[null|]]to receive serve first. But it’s a big thing for most players, serving first. When your opponent spins the racket and informs you that your choice of “up” or “down” is wrong, have you ever caught yourself wondering if they may already have “hooked” you so they can serve first? That’s okay. Let them. Don’t argue with a player who wants to serve first. In fact, you can help them to do it by electing to receive serve when you win the spin.

>大多数在俱乐部或公共球场打球的玩家都喜欢赢得发球权，这样他们就可以先发球。我认为，你几乎总是应该选择先接发球。但对大多数玩家来说，先发球是个大事情。当你的对手挥动球拍并告诉你你选择“上”或“下”是错误的时候，你有没有想过他们可能已经“钩住”了你，以便获得先发球权？没关系，让他们去吧。不要和想先发球的球员争执。事实上，当你赢得发球权时选择接发球，反而可以帮助他们实现这个愿望。

They may think they get an advantage by serving first, but in most cases they don’t. In fact, they get a disadvantage. At the pro level in men’s tennis it’s generally better to serve first in a match. With some exceptions that isn’t the case in recreational tennis. Here’s why.

>他们可能认为先发球能占得先机，但实际上大多数情况下他们反而处于劣势。事实上，职业男单比赛中，通常先发球更占优势。在业余网球中，这种情况则不总是如此，也有一些例外。以下就是原因。

## 3 Six Reasons You Shouldn’t Serve First

>3 你为何不该先发球的六个理由

1\. THE FIRST SERVE IS NO BIG THREAT

>1\. 第一发球并非大威胁

You’ve heard the expression, “First one to hold serve wins!”? At times it’s almost true at the club level. How big a weapon is your opponent’s serve? Or yours? The perceived advantage of serving first may apply in men’s professional tennis, but it really doesn’t anywhere else. (Incidentally, if you have a great serve, congratulations, but you’re the exception.) Find out for yourself. Keep track of your service holds over several matches. Here are some of the pros averages for holding serve: Edberg, 85.2; Stich, 87.2; Courier, 84.5; Agassi, 80.2; Becker, 84.4; Me, 77.9 (source: A.T.P. statistics, 1991). How do you match up?

>你听过“先发球者胜”这句话吗？在俱乐部级别的比赛中，这句话有时几乎是真的。你对手的发球有多厉害？或者你的发球呢？在男子职业网球中，先发球可能确实有一定优势，但在其他级别比赛中，这种优势其实并不存在。（顺便说一句，如果你发球非常出色，恭喜你，但你属于少数例外。）亲自去验证一下吧。在几场比赛中记录你发球局的得分情况。以下是部分职业选手的发球局得分平均数据：埃德伯格（Edberg）85.2%；施蒂希（Stich）87.2%；库尔库奇（Courier）84.5%；阿加西（Agassi）80.2%；贝克尔（Becker）84.4%；我自己则是77.9%（资料来源：ATP统计数据，1991年）。你又表现如何呢？

2\. THE SERVE IS WEAKEST EARLIEST

>2\. 发球是最早变弱的

Okay, very likely the typical club player’s serve isn’t much of a weapon under the best of circumstances. It’s even less threatening when a player isn’t warmed up, isn’t into the match, isn’t relaxed, and isn’t really comfortable with their serve, which exactly describes the conditions for your opponent serving first in the match. It’s amazing anybody ever wins their first serve to open the match.

>好吧，通常来说，业余球员的发球在最好的情况下也不是什么强有力的武器。当一名球员还没热身、还没进入状态、还没放松，而且对自己的发球也不太自信时，这种发球就更不具威胁性了。而这种情况恰恰就是你对手在比赛开始时发球的条件。真不知道到底是谁能赢下第一分发球，开启整场比赛。

>[!note]- 译者注：
此处“winning their first serve”指赢得第一分发球权，即成功发出第一分球。

At the pro level there’s a very careful approach to the serve. You’ll see in their warm-up just prior to the match that the serve may get the most [[null|]]attention of all. In most cases it must be working and working well to win, so they make sure it gets lots of attention before play starts. Even then a pro isn’t serving at his or her strongest early in the match and, obviously, neither are you or your opponent. A mediocre serve that can’t overcome all the early handicaps becomes useless, even a liability.

>在职业比赛中，发球是一项非常讲究的技术。你可以在比赛前的热身中看到，发球往往会受到最多的关注。在大多数情况下，发球必须运作良好才能赢得比赛，因此他们会在比赛开始前投入大量精力进行练习。即便如此，职业选手在比赛初期也未必发挥出最强水平，显然，你和你的对手也一样。如果发球水平一般，无法克服比赛初期的各种劣势，那么它就会变得毫无用处，甚至成为一种负担。

3\. YOU GET MORE WARM-UP TIME

>3\. 你有更多热身时间

The serve is a complex physical movement. It is one shot where you can’t just stick your racket out in front of you and block the ball back over the net.

>发球是一项复杂的身体动作。它是一记你不能只是把球拍举在身前，然后把球挡回网对面的球。

But one thing is certain. The serve is easier if you’re warmed up. Letting your opponent serve first (and relatively “unwarmed up”) gives you additional time to loosen up, to relax into the match, to hit more balls, and to get ready to serve up to your potential. You’re stealing just a bit of real match play time to settle down. This will be an asset when you step up to the line to serve that second game of the match.

>但有一点是肯定的：如果你已经热身，发球会更容易。让对手先发球（而他们相对而言还没热身），这就为你争取到了额外的时间，让你放松下来，适应比赛节奏，多击几个球，从而准备好发挥出自己的最佳水平。你是在偷取一点真正的比赛时间来让自己冷静下来。这将在你站到发球线前，准备发第二个局的球时，成为你的优势。

4\. THE PSYCHOLOGICAL EFFECT

>4\. 心理影响

Think about how you’d react if your adversary on the court chose to let you serve first. What goes through your mind? “Hey, you don’t think I can serve? I’ll show you what I can do!” That, or something like it, is a natural reaction. It’s also a dumb reaction.

>想象一下，如果你的对手在球场上选择让你先发球，你会怎么反应？你的脑海里会浮现什么想法？“嘿，你是不是觉得我发球不行？我倒要让你看看我到底能做什么！”类似这样的想法，是很自然的反应。但同时也是个愚蠢的反应。

Trying to serve harder is a big mistake. It’s a formula for faulting. That’s why you want to give your opponents the chance to psych themselves out by having them serve first.

>试图发球更用力是一个大错误。这只会导致发球出界。这就是为什么你希望让对手先发球，给他们心理上设下陷阱的机会。

>[!note]- 译者注：
此处“psych themselves out”意为通过心理战术让对手自我怀疑或失去信心，译为“心理上设下陷阱”以符合中文表达习惯。

5\. LOSING THE FIRST GAME ISN’T LOSING

>5\. 输掉第一盘并不等于输掉整场比赛

One of the easiest times to break serve is on your opponent’s first service of the match. Especially if it’s the first game of the match. However, if they hold you haven’t lost a thing. They’ve simply done what they’re supposed to do; namely, hold serve.

>破发最容易的时机，往往出现在对手比赛的第一分发球上。尤其是如果这是比赛的第一个发球局。然而，如果他们成功保发，你并没有失去什么。他们只是做了他们应该做的事——那就是保住发球局。

Technically, the match is “on serve” even though you haven’t served [[null|]]a single tennis ball. Tactically, if your opponent holds in that first service game of the match it does the least damage.

>从技术角度来说，比赛已经“进入发球局”，即使你还没发出任何一个网球。从战术角度来看，如果对手在比赛开始的第一个发球局中成功保发，造成的伤害是最小的。

6\. THE QUICK EARLY BREAK CAN BE VERY IMPORTANT

>6\. 早期快速破发往往至关重要

My whole approach to winning is based on grabbing small opportunities as I go along—getting them, holding them, and looking for more of them. Each one separately may not seem very significant, but taken together they give you a substantially bigger chance of victory. That’s why this first game of the match can be important.

>我赢得比赛的整个策略，就是一路抓住那些小机会——抓住它们，保持住它们，再寻找更多的机会。每一个单独的机会可能看起来并不起眼，但合起来它们却能大大增加你获胜的可能性。这就是为什么比赛的第一分往往如此重要。

Most players begin a match with a general feeling that if they lose serve it’s no big deal. It was how I approached it when I played college tennis. “Plenty of time to get it back later.” Maybe. Maybe not. What you do know is that by grabbing a quick service break you have given yourself a real advantage.

>大多数球员在比赛开始时，通常都会抱有一种普遍的想法：如果失掉发球局，也没什么大不了。我大学时期打球时就是这么想的。“之后还有机会重新夺回优势。”也许吧，也许不是。你唯一可以确定的是，如果你能迅速拿下对手的发球局，你就已经为自己赢得了实实在在的优势。

One of the goals of pre-match focusing, the stretching, the planned warm-up, and electing to receive first is to get this advantage. If you are successful (and you should be 25 to 35 percent of the time) you will have given yourself a better chance of winning the match before you even serve.

>预赛前的专注、拉伸、计划性的热身以及选择接发球，其中一个目标就是获得这种优势。如果你成功了（你应该有25%到35%的机会成功），那么你甚至在发球之前就已经为自己赢得了赢得比赛的更好机会。

You’ve put yourself in a strong position for what follows. You may have a break in hand. You may have forced your opponent to get aggressive too soon (trying to break back immediately). This may help you to hold your own first serve as they force shots and commit errors. If you get lucky they may even press a little harder on their second service game of the match and give you some easy opportunities to break for a second time. This is your ideal scenario. And it does happen.

>你已经为自己接下来的表现奠定了一个强有力的基础。你可能握有破发的优势，也可能迫使对手过早地变得激进（试图立刻反破）。这可能会帮助你在对手被迫击球、频频失误的情况下，更好地保住自己的第一盘发球。如果你运气不错，他们甚至可能在本盘的第二盘发球局中更加紧张，从而给你创造一些轻松破发的机会。这就是你最理想的局面。而且，这种情况确实会发生。

Think about where this can put you: up three games to none with two breaks! This is a huge advantage and puts you in a position where anybody betting ten cents on the match would bet on you. You should win the match. You may not, but the odds really go in your favor. A player up two breaks in the first set should win that set. A player who wins the first set is the odds-on favorite to win the second set. And unless you’re playing a best of five sets that’s all you need to win.

>想想看，这种情况能把你带到哪里：领先三盘，两个破发！这是一个巨大的优势，会让你处于一种位置，哪怕有人只押了十分钱，也会押在你这边。你应该赢下这场比赛。你也许会输，但胜算确实大大偏向你这边。一名在第一盘领先两个破发的球员，应该能赢下这一盘。赢得第一盘的球员，基本上就是赢下第二盘的热门人选。除非你打的是五盘三胜制，否则这已经是你赢下整场比赛所需要的全部。

This is your goal. You’re thinking, “That’s no goal. That’s a fantasy.” Not really. It’s not a simple goal, of course, but it’s what you’re trying to [[null|]]accomplish. You want to get out of the gate fast. You want to stuff your opponent as early and as hard as you can. Get them on the run. A good player on a good day might get out of your grasp. Most of them won’t if you continue to exploit opportunities and avoid the temptation to get mentally lazy about paying attention to the “outer game.” Instead of starting the match with the idea that you’ll work your way into it, begin with the goal of working your opponent out of it.

>这就是你的目标。你可能会想：“这算什么目标？这不过是个幻想。”其实不然。当然，这并不是一个简单的目标，但正是你试图实现的东西。你希望一开始就迅速进入状态，尽早、狠狠地压制对手，让他们陷入被动。在状态极佳的一天里，一位优秀的球员也许能从你手中逃脱。但只要你持续抓住机会，避免在关注“外在比赛”的过程中变得心理懒惰，大多数对手都难以逃脱你的掌控。不要以“我得慢慢进入状态”的想法开始比赛，而应该以“我要让对手尽快失去状态”作为你的目标。

I use the strategy of letting my opponent serve first about 15 percent of the time in my own matches. The reason for not doing it more often is obvious. The pros do warm up properly. And the pros do have a weapon with their serve (usually). However, there are exceptions even at the top levels of competition. I’ve never been concerned about letting Michael Chang or Jimmy Connors serve first. (You’ll notice that Chang and Connors rarely choose to serve first when they have the option, for the reasons I’ve just outlined.) I’ve also let McEnroe and Lendl serve first occasionally just to throw them a little curve right at the start of a match.

>我在自己的比赛中大约有15%的时间会选择让对手先发球。不经常这么做是有明显原因的。职业选手通常都会做好热身，而且他们的发球（通常）确实是一把利器。不过，即使是顶尖的比赛中也存在例外。我从不担心让迈克尔·舒特或吉米·康纳斯先发球。（你很快就会注意到，舒特和康纳斯在可以选择的情况下，很少主动选择先发球，原因正如我刚才所提到的。）我也偶尔让麦肯罗和莱德尔先发球，只是为了在比赛一开始给他们一点出其不意的变数。

How about letting someone like Boris Becker (with his huge serve) serve first? The potential advantage I gain when I use it is the same as the one for you. As big as his serve is, it isn’t at its best in the first games. And occasionally Becker’s serve acts up on him early in a match, so my choice can even be effective against him.

>那不如让像贝克尔这样的人先发球吧（他拥有威力巨大的发球）。当我使用发球权时所获得的优势，和你一样。虽然贝克尔的发球威力很大，但在比赛初期并不处于最佳状态。而且有时贝克尔在比赛刚开始时他的发球就会出问题，因此我的选择甚至可能对他有效。

>[!note]- 译者注：
此处“acts up on him”意为“出现失误”或“状态不佳”，根据上下文译为“发球就会出问题”更符合中文表达习惯。

One other option I use (but only rarely) when I win the toss is to hand over the choice to my opponent. It’s a little psychological ploy I use when things are still a little edgy at the beginning of a match. By giving up the decision to choose whether to serve or receive I may throw the opponent just slightly off balance. I’m basically saying, “Your serve isn’t a threat. Take your pick.” If they elect to serve first that’s what I want anyway. If they elect to have me serve first they’re admitting they also aren’t too sure about their serve. So it can backfire if I want to receive first. But usually they’ll choose to serve.

>我还有一个选择（不过很少使用），就是在赢得抛硬币后，我把选择权交给对手。这是一种小小的心理战术，通常在比赛刚开始时气氛还略显紧张时使用。通过放弃选择先发还是接发的权利，我可能让对手稍微失去平衡。我基本上是在告诉对方：“你的发球不是威胁，你随便选。”如果他们选择先发球，那正是我想要的；如果他们选择让我先发球，那就说明他们对自己的发球也不太自信。所以，如果我想接发球，这种策略可能会适得其反。但通常情况下，他们会选择先发球。

>[!note]- 译者注：
此处“backfire”意为“适得其反”，根据中文语境调整表达以符合自然习惯。

## 4 The Exceptions to the Rule

>4 规则的例外

Here’s when you shouldn’t let your opponent serve first. Obviously, if you play someone whose serve is really big and works as a weapon right [[null|]]from the beginning, don’t let them start using it against you immediately. Depending on your level of play and the opponent on a particular day this could be the case. But it won’t happen often.

>你不要让对手先发球。显然，如果你面对的是一个发球非常强势、发球本身就是武器的对手，就不应该让他们一上来就用这个武器对付你。具体是否如此，取决于你当天的水平和对手的状态。不过这种情况不会经常发生。

## 5 The Wind and the Sun

>5 风与太阳

The other situation occurs when you’re playing outside and the sun factors in. If it’s in the eyes of the server at one end you want to correctly exercise your options when you win the spin of the racket. As you know, you can choose to serve or receive, or to select which end of the court you start at. The choice determining which end of the court you start at is the most important one when the sun is a problem. Select the end that puts the sun at your back for the first game. (If the elements are a factor, never hand over the choice to your opponent.)

>另一种情况发生在你户外比赛时，阳光成为影响因素。如果阳光直射在发球端的发球手眼睛里，那么当你赢得球拍的旋转权时，就要正确地选择你的选项。正如你所知，你可以选择发球或接发球，也可以选择从场地的哪一端开始比赛。当阳光成为问题时，选择从哪一端开始比赛是最关键的决定。选择那个能让阳光在你背后的第一分发球端。（如果天气条件是影响因素，千万不要把选择权交给对手。）

Your opponent (if they’re paying attention) will then elect to have you serve first so they don’t have to serve into the sun immediately. That’s okay because you have then forced them to play the first game facing the sun. Throw them a few lobs in that first game and let them stare into the sun while they’re trying to hit an overhead in this earliest part of the match.

>你的对手（如果他们留意的话）接下来会选择让你先发球，这样他们就不用立刻面对太阳发球了。这也没关系，因为你已经迫使他们第一个发球局要面对太阳。在第一局中给他们几个大力平击球，让他们在试图击球的最初阶段直面阳光，无所遁形。

>[!note]- 译者注：
此处“lob”指网球中的“大力平击球”，即一种较浅的击球方式，常用于发球或接发球时。

You also may get lucky. Once you’ve chosen to keep the sun at your back, your opponent might still elect to serve first, into the sun! If that happens you can be reasonably sure the player is brain-dead.

>你也有机会碰上好运。一旦你决定让阳光在你背后，你的对手仍可能选择先发球，而且是朝着阳光的方向发！如果发生这种情况，你完全可以确定这位球员脑袋有问题。

>[!note]- 这里“brain-dead”是口语化表达，意为“头脑不灵光”或“反应迟钝”，译为“脑袋有问题”既保留原意，又符合中文表达习惯。

Wind can also be a factor outdoors. I played a match against Mark Edmunson in Las Vegas in winds that were gusting up to fifty miles per hour. On one point I ran over to hit a backhand and the wind suddenly gusted and blew the ball around to my forehand side! Later I hit a lob that went off my rim and sailed out over the side fence. The wind actually blew it back over the fence and onto Mark’s court for a winner. I eventually lost the match, but not before I got a pretty good case of windburn.

>风在户外也可能是影响比赛的一个因素。我在拉斯维加斯和马克·埃德蒙森打了一场球，当时风力强劲，阵风甚至达到每小时五十英里。有一次我冲过去击一个反手球，突然一阵强风袭来，把球吹到了我的正手一侧！后来我击了一个高球，球打在球拍边缘飞出了场地边线。风竟然把球吹回场地，落在了马克的场地上，直接得分。我最终输掉了比赛，但在那之前，我已经得了相当严重的“风吹伤”（风烧）。

If the sun isn’t a factor (a cloudy day, a night match), wind becomes the first consideration. If it’s blowing baseline to baseline I choose to start the match with the wind in my face. There’s more room for error because the wind will knock your ball down and keep it from going long. Typically the opponent won’t be as concerned about the wind as [[null|]]about the sun. They usually elect to serve first. This means that in the first game you’ve got them serving with the wind at their back. Tougher to get the ball in the service court in that circumstance. Your advantage.

>如果天气不是晴天（比如阴天、夜晚比赛），那么风就成为首要考虑因素。如果风是从底线吹向底线，我会选择迎着风开始比赛。这样失误的空间会更大，因为风会把球吹下来，防止它飞得太远。通常来说，对手对风的关注程度不会像对阳光那么高。他们通常会选择先发球。这意味着在第一局中，你面对的是迎着风发球的对手。在这种情况下，把球发进发球区会更加困难。这也是你的优势。

So as a general rule I like to start with the sun at my back and the wind in my face if I have the options. You should do the same. If it means you have to serve first, that’s okay. You’ve still gained the advantage.

>一般来说，我倾向于选择背对阳光、面对风的方向开始比赛，如果条件允许的话。你也应该这么做。如果这意味着你必须先发球，那也没关系。你仍然占据了优势。

## 6 2\. Start Your Match Like Ivan Lendl

>6 2\. 模仿伊万·伦德尔开局

Ivan Lendl is one of the most powerful sluggers ever to play tennis. When he really rips it you can almost hear the ball say ouch! He pulverizes it. But here’s what you don’t know. He never starts at full throttle. Ivan understands the importance of working his way up to maximum speed and power. He knows you’re never there in the first few games of the match. Nerves, the crowd, new conditions, and other factors almost always make it impossible to really let it out right away. Ivan doesn’t. He keeps his game very much under control initially.

>伊万·伦德尔是网球史上最具破坏力的强力型选手之一。当他全力击球时，你几乎能听到球发出“哎呀！”的声音，他把球打得粉碎。但你不知道的是，他从不一开始就全力以赴。伊万深知逐步提升到最大速度和力量的重要性。他知道，在比赛开始的前几局，你根本还没达到最佳状态。紧张、观众、新的场地条件以及其他因素，几乎总是让你无法立刻放开手脚。伊万不会这样做，他一开始就把比赛控制得很好。

During the first three or four games he’s hitting at about 65 to 75 percent of his full power. He very consciously resists the urge to really smack it. He wants to work his way into a good rhythm. He understands the correct method is to start below it and work up to it. It’s very hard to bring yourself down to your best pace if you start hitting too hard right away. You can spend the rest of the match looking for your rhythm. Ivan never makes that mistake.

>前三到四局，他击球的力量大概只发挥出满力的65%到75%。他非常有意识地克制住想要全力击球的冲动。他想慢慢进入一种良好的节奏。他明白正确的做法是先从较慢的节奏开始，逐步提升到最佳状态。如果你一开始就用力过猛，就很难让自己降下来，进入最佳节奏。你可能会在整场比赛中都在寻找这种节奏。而伊万从不犯这样的错误。

And that goes for his serve, too. By trying to slug the ball before your body and mind are really acclimated to the match you can take your service down for the whole day. The serve is complex. Even Lendl doesn’t go for it too early.

>这一点同样适用于他的发球。如果你在身体和心理真正适应比赛之前就急于发力发球，你整场比赛的发球状态都会受到影响。发球是一项复杂的动作。就连伦德尔也不会太早地投入发球。

>[!note]- 译者注：
此处“go for it”意为“投入、发力”，根据上下文翻译为“投入发球”更符合中文表达习惯。

When I played him for the first time at Hartford in ’82 I couldn’t believe the great Lendl was hitting such manageable shots. I was thinking, “Geez, they looked a lot heavier when I watched him play other guys.” This went on for about four games. In the fifth game he cranked it up. Suddenly a serve went by me that I didn’t even see. I was hitting everything late. He started swinging me wide left and right. I couldn’t get in position for anything. He was rolling. And he rolled right over [[null|]]me 6–2, 6–2. He had worked his way up to maximum speed and power and used it when it was there. Not before.

>第一次在哈特福德对阵他时，我简直不敢相信伟大的莱德尔竟能打出如此“好处理”的球。我当时心里想：“天哪，他打其他选手的时候，那些球看起来重多了。”这种情况持续了大约四局。到了第五局，他突然发力了。突然有一记发球擦过我的耳边，我甚至都没看到。我一直在最后时刻才击球，他开始把我打得左冲右突，我完全无法站稳位置。他完全掌控了局面，最终以6–2、6–2直落两盘击败了我。他把速度和力量提升到了最大，并在时机成熟时才使用它们，而不是在此之前。

Lendl’s approach is the correct one. Start below your maximum pace and rhythm in your first few games. Don’t try to cream those early shots. Let yourself build into a solid, comfortable “power” rhythm. It’ll happen if you let yourself resist the temptation to hit hard and win quick.

>莱德尔的方法是正确的。在你最初的几局比赛中，要从低于自己最大节奏和速度的状态开始。不要急于在早期击出强力的球。让自己逐渐进入一种稳定、舒适的“力量”节奏。只要你能抗拒住想要强力击球、快速取胜的诱惑，这种节奏自然就会形成。

>[!note]- 译者注：
这里的“cream those early shots”意为“急于在早期击出强力球”，根据中文表达习惯，意译为“不要急于在早期击出强力的球”。

## 7 3\. Play the First Two Games Correctly

>7 3\. 正确打好前两盘

The first two games of the match can set the temperature for what follows. The mental and physical attitude established here is valuable because it can start you on a course that makes winning a lot easier. You and the opponent serve and receive serve for the first time. You are both getting your “sea legs” and working yourself up to attack speed. There are a number of considerations I bring to these first games that incorporate Lendl’s approach and add to it.

>比赛前两分可以为接下来的局势定下基调。在这里建立的心理和身体状态非常有价值，因为它能让你踏上一条更容易获胜的道路。你和对手第一次发球和接发球。你们都在适应“海上的腿感”（即适应比赛节奏），并逐渐进入进攻状态。我在前两分中会考虑一些因素，这些因素融合了伦德尔的方法，并在此基础上加以补充。

>[!note]- “sea legs” 是英语中形容人在船上或水中适应环境、找到平衡感的习语，此处比喻球员适应比赛节奏和环境。

## 8 No Unforced Errors

>8 没有非受迫性失误

Remember during these first two games that you’re not as loose as you’ll be later. Even if you warm up correctly you still aren’t really set to let it all hang out. So no unforced errors. Don’t be afraid to swing at the ball, but don’t try to impress the other player. Just make some balls. The pattern you want to set in place is one of consistency, of success in getting the ball back over the net and into the opposite court.

>记住在前两分中，你还没有后来那么放松。即使你热身正确，你仍然没有真正准备好放开手脚。所以不要出现不必要的失误。不要害怕大力挥拍击球，但也不要试图去 impress（让对方印象深刻）对方球员。你只需要把球打回去就行。你想要建立的是一种稳定、持续的节奏，一种成功地将球击过网并回击到对方场地的模式。

## 9 Serve Sensibly

>9 智慧发球

When you’re serving for the first time it’s very easy to fault. Remember to avoid the temptation to try and hit big. This is the point in the match where careless flubs are made. Don’t put yourself in a position where the other player wins an early game just by standing there. Don’t give them freebee’s. Your goals should be modest: 1. Get the ball in. 2\. Get it to their weaker side.

>第一次发球时，很容易犯错。记住，要避免那种想要一击制胜的诱惑。这是比赛中最容易出现失误的时刻。不要让自己陷入这样的境地：对方仅仅站着不动，就赢得一分。不要给他们“白送”的机会。你的目标要简单：1. 把球发出；2. 发到他们较弱的一侧。

A lot of players are lackadaisical on their first service game. They don’t [[null|]]seem to care. If you let your opponent break you right out of the chute you’ve jacked them up. You’ve adrenalized them. You’ve given them the opportunity to pressure you. And pressure generally hurts your game. So be smart. Get the ball in. Keep the ball in play early on. Eliminate errors.

>很多球员在第一盘发球局时都显得懒散，似乎并不在意。如果你一上来就让对手破发，那就等于把对方打兴奋了。你让他们感到压力，而压力通常会对你的表现造成负面影响。所以要聪明一点，把球发好，尽早让球进入比赛状态，减少失误。

## 10 Don’t Relax

>10 别松懈

Players who serve first and hold (or receive first and break) almost always let down in the next game. There’s a sense of “Whew, I got through that. Now I can relax for a second.” Bang. The other player wins at love. Don’t let them off so easily.

>先发球并保发（或接发球并破发）的球员几乎总会在下一盘中掉链子。他们心里会有一种“呼，我挺过来了，现在可以稍微放松一下”的感觉。砰！对方直接以love（零分）赢下这一盘。别让他们这么轻易地喘口气。

>[!note]- 译注：“love”在网球术语中表示“零分”，即对方得分0分。

Remember, if you serve first and hold, a subsequent break is “longer.” By that I mean if you break them in that second game and then hold your own serve you’re up 3–0. Psychologically that seems a lot heavier than it is. It’s still just one break. But it’s three games difference. It feels worse to your opponent. And that means it is worse.

>记住，如果你先发球并保发，之后的破发就显得“更长”了。我的意思是，如果你在第二盘破发，然后又保住自己的发球局，你就以3-0领先。从心理上来说，这似乎比实际更沉重。它仍然是一个破发，但差距是三局。这对对手来说感觉更糟，而这也意味着它实际上更糟。

If you receive first and break (which is what you should try and accomplish) don’t get careless on your serve in that second game. The tendency is to get feeling a little too good a little too soon. Don’t go for aces. And depending on the level of your game, don’t be too ambitious. You’ll be surprised at what happens in your first service game if you just get your first serve in.

>如果你接发球局赢了（这是你应当努力做到的），那么在接下来的这一局发球时不要掉以轻心。人们往往会过早地感到自己状态很好，从而产生轻敌心理。不要一味追求发ACE球。根据你目前的水平，也不要过于激进。如果你只是努力把第一发球发出，你将会惊讶于第一发球局会发生什么。

## 11 Test Them Early

>11 早做测试

Also, I like to work my way to the net if I can early in these first few games. Don’t force it, but if the opportunity arises take it. I look to do it when I’m up 40-love and can spare a point. The other player is a little tight. They haven’t made any shots and they may jerk one that gives you the freebee.

>此外，我也喜欢在前几分比赛中尽早向网前移动。不要强求，但如果机会出现，就把握住。我通常会在自己40分、对方爱分（即0分）的情况下，有余力时选择上网。此时对方会有些紧张，他们还没有打出任何好球，可能会出现失误，给你一个“免费得分”的机会。

>[!note]- “freebee” 是网球术语，指无需努力即可获得的得分，此处译为“免费得分”以传达其含义。

## 12 4\. Utilize the First Pit Stop

>12 4\. 利用第一个补给站

I get asked why the pros take a rest after the first game. Why not just change ends without sitting down for a break? Good question, because [[null|]]in your entire life I’ll bet you’ve never seen a tour player skip the opportunity to sit down after playing that first game. It can’t be fatigue, can it? Answer: It’s not to rest. It’s to think.

>人们经常问我，为什么职业选手在第一盘结束后要休息一下。为什么不能直接换边而不坐下来休息呢？这是个好问题，因为在你的一生中，我打赌你从未见过任何巡回赛选手放弃在第一盘结束后坐下来休息的机会。难道是疲劳吗？答案是：不是为了休息，而是为了思考。

Changeovers are where you get yourself together mentally and emotionally. The first changeover has added value because it gives you an opportunity to check your bearings before anything too drastic has happened. I use it as an opportunity to establish again in my mind what it is I’m trying to do. I review my game plan.

>换边休息是让你在心理和情绪上重新整理的时机。第一次换边休息尤其重要，因为它给了你一个在事情变得太过激烈之前重新校准方向的机会。我会利用这段时间再次在脑海里明确自己究竟想要做什么，回顾我的比赛计划。

I take my sip of water, wipe off the sweat, settle down, but mainly I think. I review my strategy. What am I trying to make happen? What do I want to prevent from happening? On subsequent changeovers I want to review anything that might have occurred that surprised me. Has my opponent hurt me with anything? I give myself a little pep talk: “Stick to your game plan. Don’t get overeager. Be patient. Remember to get that first serve in and to the backhand.”

>我喝了一口水分，擦掉汗水，坐稳了，但更重要的是我开始思考。我回顾自己的策略：我想要促成什么？我想要防止什么发生？在接下来的换边时，我想回顾一下是否有什么事情出乎了我的意料。我的对手有没有用什么招数伤到我？我给自己打打气：“要坚持自己的战术，不要急躁，要有耐心。记住，要把第一发球发到对方反手位。”

That first pit stop is very important because the beginning of a match is a disruptive period. A lot of things are happening and a lot of adjustments are being made. It’s easy to lose your focus, your plan, your attitude. This first changeover is unique in that essentially you are still looking ahead. There is no strong pattern emerging in the game yet. You aren’t looking at what went wrong or right. You are still in the process of trying to force your game plan on the other player.

>第一个间歇非常重要，因为比赛一开始就是一个动荡的阶段。很多事情都在发生，很多调整也正在进行中。很容易就会失去你的专注、你的计划和你的态度。这个第一盘间歇的特殊之处在于，你实际上仍然在展望未来。此时比赛尚未形成明显的节奏或模式，你并没有去回顾刚才哪些做得对或哪些做得错，你还在努力将自己的战术强加于对手。

>[!note]- 译者注：
此处“force your game plan on the other player”意为“将己方的战术强加于对方”，译为“强加于对手”更符合中文表达习惯。

I realize tennis is a social event and that changeovers are where a lot of the socializing usually goes on at tennis courts. It’s a chance to catch up on the latest news and gossip, or to talk about the match itself. You probably aren’t prepared to put a towel over your head and start meditating. But a player dedicated to winning is willing to delay the small talk until after the match.

>我意识到网球是一项社交活动，换边休息时通常是球场上社交最频繁的时刻。这时大家可以聊聊最近的新闻和八卦，或者谈论一下刚刚进行的比赛。你大概还不会立刻拿出毛巾盖在头上开始冥想。但一名致力于赢球的球员，愿意把这种闲聊推迟到比赛结束之后。

You are still trying to steal that early lead. If you broke serve you want to hold. If you held (or lost) serve you want to break. That initial changeover gives you a chance to approach the job calmly with your plan clearly in mind. “Get my first serve in. Don’t get fancy. Make him play.”

>你还在试图抢得开局的领先优势。如果你破发了，你就要保住发球局；如果你接发成功（或失败）了，你就要破发。这个开局的转换为你提供了一个冷静地按照计划行事的机会。“第一发球尽量发出，不要玩花招。让他去接球。”

I think of the changeover as my isolation booth. I want to use what I’m learning in the match to my advantage. A smart tennis player is accumulating information as play goes on. And they’re using that [[null|]]information to their advantage. The changeover is where you have an uninterrupted time to review and plan.

>我认为换边休息是属于我的“隔离舱”。我想利用在比赛中所学到的东西来为自己谋利。一个聪明的网球选手会在比赛进行中不断积累信息，并且利用这些信息为自己争取优势。换边休息正是你拥有 uninterrupted（无干扰）时间来回顾和制定计划的时刻。

Watch the pros when you see a match on television. They’re not sitting there looking off into the crowd or talking to the ballboys. There is heavy concentration going on. That’s why the towel goes over the head occasionally. They want to really stop and think about what’s going on. Don’t waste that opportunity in your own matches.

>看电视上的职业比赛时，注意观察那些顶尖选手。他们不会坐在那里望着观众席发呆，也不会跟球童说话。他们正全神贯注地投入比赛中。这就是为什么他们有时会把毛巾盖在头上。他们想停下来认真思考局势。在你自己的比赛中，千万不要浪费这样的机会。

## 13 Play Tennis Like You’d Do Business

>13 比赛就像做生意

A smart businessperson constantly tries to think of ways to improve business. It’s amazing how they’ll suddenly get on the court and stop thinking. On the changeover they’ll sit there like a cow licking a block of salt. Not a thought in their head. A smart player constantly tries to improve their chance of winning. Always thinking. Always analyzing what’s happening.

>一个精明的商人总是不断想办法提升自己的生意。他们竟然会在某个瞬间突然走到球场上，完全停止思考。换边时，他们坐在那儿像头牛舔着一块盐块，脑子里一片空白。而一个聪明的球员则不断努力提高自己获胜的机会，始终在思考，始终在分析场上发生的一切。

It can even be a good idea to have a notebook in your equipment bag that you can refer to in the beginning of (and during) a match. It can list your strengths. Your opponent’s weaknesses. Your main goal against that player. Maybe a tip you got at your tennis lesson. Things you’ve thought out before the match. It’s important information that can easily get forgotten during the match. During the changeover take it out of your equipment bag and review it.

>甚至可以在你的装备包里放一个笔记本，在比赛开始（甚至进行中）时随时查阅。你可以列出自己的优势、对手的弱点、针对这位选手的主要目标，也许是你在网球课上学到的一个小技巧，或者你在赛前思考过的一些要点。这些都是在比赛中很容易被遗忘的重要信息。在局间休息时，把笔记本从装备包里拿出来，重新回顾一遍。

Adults at some of my tennis clinics have told me that they’re playing tennis to relax. That’s absolutely okay. I tell them when they start playing tennis to win remember some of the things I’ve said. I’ve always found winning a lot more relaxing than losing.

>在我的一些网球训练营中，有些成年人告诉我，他们打网球是为了放松。这完全没问题。我会告诉他们，当他们开始打网球以求胜利时，记得回想一下我曾说过的一些话。我一直觉得，赢得比赛比输掉比赛更让人放松。

One of the biggest misconceptions recreational tennis players have about the game is that there’s not much to it mentally. Some of the big names in tennis also felt that way. Fortunately for me.

>休闲网球选手对这项运动存在一个最大的误解，那就是它在心理层面并没有太多可做的事情。一些大名鼎鼎的网球选手当初也曾经这么认为。幸运的是，对我而言，

Next: Play smart during your match by taking advantage of what you see.[[null|]]

>下一部分：比赛时要聪明应对，利用你所看到的一切。



# [[null|]][[null|]]II

>[[null|]][[null|]]II

# Playing Smart: Finding a Way to Win

>聪明地比赛：找到一条取胜之道

“Brad’s very cunning at figuring out an opponent; probably better at it than any player in the world.”

>“布拉德非常擅长识破对手的破绽；可能比世界上任何一名球员都更擅长这一点。”

—Allan Fox, Instruction Editor, Tennis Magazine[[null|]]

>——艾伦·福克斯，《网球杂志》教学编辑



# [[null|]][[null|]]7

>[[null|]][[null|]]7

# The Key to Victory

>胜利的关键

The New York Times says I’m great at making my opponents play badly. Arthur Ashe agreed: “Brad doesn’t have any strokes you’d want to write home about.” They’re right. I don’t overpower people. I don’t have any flashy shots. I win because I have the ability to implement my basic game strategy successfully: maximize my strengths and minimize my weaknesses. That means I consistently get in a position where I’m hitting a shot I like rather than one I don’t.

>《纽约时报》说，我最擅长让对手打得不好。阿瑟·阿什也同意：“布拉德没有任何你想要写信回家炫耀的球技。” 他们说得对。我不靠力量压制对手，也没有什么花哨的击球动作。我之所以能赢，是因为我有能力成功地执行我的基本战术：发挥自己的优势，减少自己的弱点。这意味着我始终能处于击打自己喜爱的球而非不喜爱的球的位置。

At the same time I want to maximize my opponent’s weaknesses and minimize their strengths. I want them to be hitting shots they don’t like from positions they don’t want. I’ll lose if I go strength to strength. I’m good, however, at working my strengths against my opponent’s weaknesses.

>同时，我也想最大限度地利用对手的弱点，同时削弱他们的优势。我希望他们不得不在不想的位置上击打自己不喜欢的球。如果我一味地以强对强，我就会输。不过，我擅长用自己的优势去攻击对手的弱点。

>[!note]- 译者注：
此处“以强对强”意指双方在各自优势领域直接对抗，容易陷入僵局，而“用自己的优势去攻击对手的弱点”则更具有策略性和针对性。

The goal of my game plan is to turn the other player’s own game against him. I did it to McEnroe that night at the ’87 Masters in New York (more on that coming up), against Becker in the U.S. Open in ’87, and I’ve done it to many others elsewhere.

>我的比赛计划目标，就是让对手用自己的打法来对付自己。我曾在1987年纽约大师赛上对麦肯罗（后面会详细讲到）做到这一点，也在1987年美国公开赛上对贝克尔做到这一点，还对很多其他选手做过类似的事。

Ahead are my keys for taking a player out of their A game and making them try to beat you with their B game, for picking apart a player’s strokes and game plan and turning their advantage into your advantage.

>以下是我在如何让一名球员从他们的A级状态中退出，迫使他们用B级状态来赢你，如何拆解对手的击球方式与战术安排，将他们的优势转化为你的优势方面的建议。

## 1 Who’s Doing What to Whom? 谁在对谁做些什么？

In order to follow the plan successfully you need to understand what’s going on in the match, with your game, your opponent’s game, and [[null|]]with the interaction of the two. My coach at Pepperdine, Allen Fox, used to tell me, “Always be asking yourself during a match who’s doing what to whom.” That means knowing how and why points are being won and lost. It means knowing what’s going on out on the court.

>要成功执行计划，你必须了解比赛中正在发生的事情，包括你自己的比赛、对手的比赛，以及两者之间的互动。我在佩珀丁大学的教练艾伦·福克斯（Allen Fox）常说：“在比赛中，你始终要问自己，到底是谁在对谁做些什么。” 这意味着你要清楚地知道比分是如何赢来的、又是如何失掉的，也意味着你要清楚地了解场上的情况。

Are you losing points because your opponent is successfully attacking you at the net or eating up your second serve? Does the other player beat you from the baseline? Is your opponent crushing overheads? Are your forehands falling short? Are you getting passed when you come to the net (and on which side)? Is the other player running around the backhand? Are they great with the groundies until you pressure them at the net? What is their style of play—Retriever, serve volley, or something else?

>你是不是因为对手成功地在网前施压，或者吃掉你的第二发球而丢分？对方是不是从底线压制你？对手是不是在正手过顶击球时威力十足？你的正手是不是不够有力？你跑到网前时是不是经常被对手过掉（是在哪一侧）？对方是不是经常绕到你的反手位去？他们是不是在你上网施压之前，对地面击球都很出色？对方的打法风格是怎样的——是接发球上网型、发球上网型，还是其他类型？

The correct tennis response to any situation during your match can only be determined by knowing “who’s doing what to whom.” A successful player knows the answer. They may not know the exact numbers and percentages (e.g. 62 percent of first serves good), but they have a sense of it. It’s an ability you can develop if you work at it.

>你在比赛中面对任何情况时，正确的网球应对方式只能通过了解“谁在对谁做些什么”来确定。一位成功的球员知道答案。他们可能不知道确切的数字和百分比（例如，一发得分率是62%），但他们对此有直觉。这是一种你可以通过努力培养的能力。

Most recreational tennis players don’t know who’s doing what to whom during their match. They don’t pay attention. They don’t observe and analyze what’s going on. Believe it or not, I’ve even had a player at one of my camps tell me he didn’t realize his opponent was a lefty until the middle of the first set. “Now I know why those shots to the ad court kept coming back so good.” Right! He was hitting into the guy’s forehand thinking it was his backhand. He was not aware of what was happening out on the court.

>大多数休闲网球选手并不清楚在比赛中，对方到底在做些什么。他们不注意，也不观察和分析场上情况。相信我，我甚至在一次训练营中遇到过一位球员，他在第一盘进行到一半时才意识到他的对手是左撇子。“现在我知道为什么那些打到广告区的球总是能很好地回来。”没错，他一直在打对手的正手位，却以为对方是右手持拍，打到对方的反手位。他根本没意识到场上的情况到底如何。

## 2 The Combination to the Lock 锁定组合

Every player’s game is like a combination lock. Without the combination it’s tough to open, but with the combination it gets a lot easier. Knowing who’s doing what to whom gives you the combination to the lock.

>每一位选手的比赛都像一把组合锁。没有正确的组合，很难打开；但一旦掌握了组合，就轻松得多。了解对手在针对谁采取什么行动，就等于拿到了打开这把锁的密码。

>[!note]- 该句使用“组合锁”作为比喻，意指比赛中的策略和对手的意图，翻译时保留了比喻的生动性，同时确保读者能理解其隐含的战术含义。

For me that came relatively easy once I really started applying it during matches when I turned pro. When I was a kid growing up near Oakland I was a major sports nut. I was fanatical about the Raiders, Warriors, 49ers, Giants, and A’s. I always tried to figure out [[null|]]what was going to happen next in the games I saw, who was going to do what.

>对我而言，一旦真正开始在职业比赛当中应用这些方法，就变得相对容易了。在我小时候，住在奥克兰附近，我是个十足的体育迷。我对 Raiders（闪电侠）、Warriors（勇士队）、49ers（四十九人队）、Giants（巨人队）和 A’s（运动家队）都非常狂热。我总是试图去琢磨，我看到的比赛接下来会发生什么，谁会做些什么。

One of my idols was Kenny Stabler of the Raiders: The Snake. I’d watch him at quarterback and try to guess what play was going to get called. Would he throw to Casper or Balitnikoff, and in what pattern? Or, with the A’s, would Catfish Hunter throw a knockdown pitch right after somebody hit a home run off him? (Answer: Yes.) Or how would the infield and outfield position itself for various hitters in different situations? How would the Warriors get the ball to Rick Barry with ten seconds left in the game? It was just automatic for me. I loved to analyze what was going on out there.

>我最崇拜的偶像之一是 Raiders 队的肯尼·斯塔贝尔（Kenny Stabler），人称“蛇王”（The Snake）。我会看他作为四分卫在场上的表现，试图猜出接下来会叫什么战术。他会把球传给卡斯珀（Casper）还是巴利特尼克夫（Balitnikoff）？传球的路线又会是怎样的？或者在 A’s 队，当有人轰出全垒打之后，猫鱼·亨特（Catfish Hunter）会不会立刻投出一个让击球员跪地的“敲地球”（knockdown pitch）？（答案是：会。）又或者，不同击球员在不同情况下，内场和外场的防守位置会如何调整？在比赛还剩十秒时，勇士队（Warriors）是如何把球传给里克·巴里（Rick Barry）的？对我而言，这些都已经是下意识的反应了。我特别喜欢分析场上的各种情况。

Later, when I was starting out on tour, it was the same. I studied a lot—not books, but other players. Anytime I saw someone new I’d go watch them play and check out their game. Another player would say to me, “Hey, Boris Becker from Germany is on court 3,” and I’d go over to see him hit.

>后来，当我刚开始巡回赛的时候，情况也是一样。我花了很多时间学习——不是看书，而是观察其他球员。只要看到有新面孔，我就会去观看他们的比赛，研究他们的打法。另一个球员会跟我说：“嘿，德国的博里斯·贝克尔在3号球场。”我就会跑去观看他击球。

## 3 Fat Fritz and My Little Black Book 胖弗里茨与我的小黑本

Initially, I was watching more out of curiosity than with any great purpose. I didn’t work hard on using that information in my matches. This changed one day during my third month on tour. I played a match against Fritz Buehning. He was a big guy with a powerful serve. In fact, he was too big to get around the court well, but when his serve was working he didn’t need to get around the court very well. Fritz was built like a lineman for U.C.L.A., his alma mater. In fact, his nickname was “Fratz” (short for Fat Fritz). I figured he’d be a pushover.

>起初，我更多是出于好奇在观看比赛，而不是有明确的目的。我当时并没有认真利用这些信息来提升自己的比赛表现。这种情况在我巡回赛的第三个月发生了改变。那天我与弗里茨·布宁格（Fritz Buehning）打了一场比赛。他是个体型高大、发球极具力量的选手。事实上，他个头太大，很难在球场上灵活移动，但一旦他的发球状态良好，他就几乎不需要移动太多。弗里茨的身材像加州大学洛杉矶分校（U.C.L.A.）橄榄球队的前锋，他的母校正是这所大学。事实上，他的绰号叫“弗拉茨”（Fratz），是“Fat Fritz”的简称。我想他应该是个容易对付的对手。

Typical of my matches in those days I just showed up and expected that if I played well I’d win. I hadn’t given any thought to Fratz, his game, or how to exploit it. I lost quick in straight sets 6–2, 6–2. What burned me up more than losing was knowing that if I’d given some thought (before and during the match) to what I should be trying to do I’d have beaten him. A big guy like that who isn’t quick and doesn’t like to bend his knees? Piece of cake. Just start moving him around with [[null|]]groundstrokes and when he comes to the net give him some dippers that drop low and throw up an occasional lob. I didn’t give any thought to my tactics and got blown off the court.

>那会儿我打比赛通常都是随便到场，心想只要我打得不错就能赢。我根本没有想过弗拉茨（Fratz）这个人，他的打法，以及如何利用他的弱点。我很快以6比2、6比2直落两盘输掉了比赛。比输掉比赛更让我难受的是，我知道如果我在赛前和比赛中稍微思考一下该怎么做，我本可以击败他。像他这样个头大、不灵活、也不喜欢弯膝盖的选手？简直是小菜一碟。只要用平击球把他调动起来，等他上网时，就给他一些低平球，偶尔也来个高吊球，让他措手不及。我根本没有想过自己的战术，结果被直接打出了球场。

After that I got serious about collecting and using information about my opponents against my opponents. After that I started writing things down. It’s when I started my little black book.

>之后，我开始认真地收集并利用关于对手的信息来对付他们。从那时起，我开始把东西写下来。也就是我开始写我的小黑皮本的时候。

Now a lot of players have a little black book with names and numbers in it. Yannick Noah’s had so many in it he needed help just carrying it. But my little black book was different. It had names in it, but they were the names of tennis players. It had numbers in it, but they were the numbers of unforced errors, winners, and forcing shots on particular strokes. I counted mistakes on backhand volleys, overheads, forehand and backhand groundstrokes. I kept track of winners on those shots and watched what they liked to do in specific situations.

>现在，很多球员都有一个小黑本，里面写着名字和号码。雅尼克·诺阿（Yannick Noah）的本子多到连带携带都得有人帮忙。但我的小黑本却不一样。它里面也有名字，但那些名字都是网球选手的名字；它里面也有数字，但那些数字是失误次数、得分球和关键击球的数据。我会统计自己在反手网前球、正手高球、正反手底线击球时出现的失误，同时记录这些击球中的得分次数，并观察对手在特定情况下倾向于采取哪些行动。

Most recreational players watch a match like they’re looking at somebody play Nintendo at a video arcade. They just stare. I’d watch a match like I was studying for a history test. In 1984 when I first checked out Boris (in Philadelphia), I saw that when he was attacked on his backhand side he loved to go down the line. And that while he had a huge forehand, if he started to miss a few shots with it the rest of his game could suffer. As you saw, I later used that information about him against him to beat him several times, including the ’87 U.S. Open.

>大多数业余球员看比赛，就像在视频游戏厅里看别人玩《任天堂》游戏一样，只是傻傻地盯着。而我则像在准备历史考试一样观看比赛。1984年我第一次在费城观看博里斯的比赛时，就注意到当他被对手攻击到反手位时，他喜欢沿底线一路到底。同时，虽然他的正手威力巨大，但如果他连续失误几次，他的整体打法就会受到影响。正如你所看到的，我后来利用这些关于他的信息多次打败他，包括1987年的美国公开赛。

Numbers, patterns, and tendencies. I’d see Paul Annacone always try to come to the net on a big point and make a note of it. I’d watch McEnroe swing his can opener serve wide on the ad court when he needed a point. I marked down that Connors occasionally had trouble with shots that came in low to his forehand.

>数字、模式和趋势。我总能看到保罗·安娜康尼在关键分时总是试图跑到网前，并做下笔记。我会观察麦肯罗在需要得分时，总是用他那罐头开罐器式的发球，将球发到右侧发球区。我还记录下康纳斯偶尔会在正手区接低球时遇到困难。

## 4 The Tennis Lie Detector: Pressure

>4 网球的“谎言探测器”：压力

As I was watching and studying a player over several sets I’d see him fall into predictable patterns in certain situations. Especially under pressure. Pressure is the ultimate lie detector. That’s when strokes speak the truth. On a big point what does a player do? They avoid (or try to avoid) the [[null|]]shot they don’t feel comfortable with. That’s when the flaws are magnified. It’s when you see what their “true” game is like.

>我曾观察并研究过一名球员在多盘比赛中的表现，发现他在某些情况下会陷入可预测的模式，尤其是在压力之下。压力是终极的谎言探测器。这时，击球才会说出真相。在关键分上，球员会怎么做？他们会回避（或试图回避）自己不感到舒适的那记球。这时，他们的弱点会被放大。你也会看到他们“真实”的打法是什么样的。

Here’s how pressure works. When a player with a questionable backhand is up 4–2 in a set they may hit that backhand okay. No pressure, right? They’re relaxed and ahead. When they’re down 2–4 that backhand may get a little tentative because of nerves. And on a set point? Forget it. Pressure gets right to the heart of the matter. Under pressure is when a player will try like the devil to avoid hitting the shot they don’t really trust. I want to know what that shot is so I can try to force them to hit it under pressure.

>压力是这样起作用的。当一名反手不太稳定的球员在抢七中以4-2领先时，他可能还能打出不错的反手。这时候压力不大，对吧？他放松，而且领先。可当比分变成2-4时，他的反手可能会变得有些犹豫，因为紧张了。到了抢发球点（set point）呢？那就更不用说了。压力直接触及问题的核心。在压力下，球员会拼命想避开那些他们其实不太信任的球。我想知道到底是什么球，这样我就可以在压力下迫使他们打出这记球。

For example, if it’s your opponent’s backhand that is suspect, you know that they’ll try to run around it on a big point. That’s when I would try to set it up by swinging them a little wide on the forehand first. By pulling them wide on their forehand side and then hitting to the backhand you make it so they can’t run around it. Or if they do they’ll be hitting off balance. That’s how you use information about your opponent against your opponent.

>例如，如果对手的反手较弱，你便知道他们在关键分上会试图绕过反手来接球。这时我就会先用一点宽泛的正手球来“诱骗”他们。通过把球打到他们正手一侧的外侧，然后再转到他们的反手位，这样他们就无法绕开反手了。或者，即使他们试图绕开，也会处于失衡的状态。这就是如何利用对对手的了解来对付对手。

## 5 Agassi at First Glance 初看阿加西

I scouted Andre Agassi in ’86 at Stratton Mountain. Here was this skinny little kid with scraggly hair taking absolutely the best cut at the ball I had ever seen. Unbelievable! And people were telling me he wasn’t going to do much because he had kind of flopped out as a junior. I didn’t buy that.

>1986年我在斯特拉顿山（Stratton Mountain）观察安德烈·阿加西。当时他是个瘦小的男孩，头发乱糟糟的，但击球的方式却让我惊叹不已，是我见过最出色的击球。简直难以置信！当时还有人跟我说，他作为青少年选手时表现平平，没什么可期待的，因为他当时已经“栽了”。但我可不信这个。

Actually I had seen him for the first time when I was at the Alan King Classic in Las Vegas in 1981. I just happened to see him working out on a back court at Caesar’s Palace. Even then his power and ability to take the ball so early got my attention. He was eleven years old.

>事实上，我第一次见到他是在1981年位于拉斯维加斯的艾伦·金经典赛（Alan King Classic）期间。我当时恰好在凯撒宫（Caesar’s Palace）的后场看到他训练。那时的他就已经展现出惊人的力量，以及非常早地截击球的能力，让我印象深刻。他当时才十一岁。

It took me a while to put together that the kid with the bleached hair at Stratton Mountain was the same person I’d seen five years earlier in Las Vegas. Only now his hair was brighter and his strokes were bigger. In ’86 I wrote down that his groundstrokes were brutal. Especially from the mid-court, where he had this great ability to take the ball on the rise and just really “de-struct” it. He radiated confidence. He was hitting the [[null|]]ball a ton and I could imagine the pressure he could put on a player. I also made a note that his volleys didn’t look too strong, but that the rest of his game was so good it didn’t matter.

>我花了一段时间才意识到，那个在斯特拉顿山球场上有着漂白头发的少年，其实就是五年前在拉斯维加斯见过的那个人。只是现在他的头发更亮了，击球也更有力了。1986年我曾记录下，他的底线球非常可怕，尤其是从中场位置击球时，他有极强的能力在球还在上升时就击打，把球“彻底击溃”。他散发着自信的光芒，击球次数非常多，我仿佛能想象他能给对手施加多大的压力。我还特别记下，他的网前球看起来并不太强，但他的整体实力如此出色，这点并不重要。

When Andre lost in the finals of three slams (two French Opens and the U.S. Open) I thought maybe that his strokes were big, but his heart was small. Then he put it all together at Wimbledon in ’92 and showed what he had inside. And it’s a lot. Andre has a ton of heart.

>当安德烈在三项大满贯（两次法网和美网）决赛中失利时，我心想，也许他的球技很出色，但内心不够强大。直到1992年温网，他终于将一切整合到位，展现出他内心所拥有的力量。那可不少呢。安德烈拥有一颗强大的心脏。

>[!note]- 译者注：
此处“heart”双关，既指“心脏”，也指“内心、意志力”，在中文中通过“内心所拥有的力量”和“拥有一颗强大的心脏”来体现双重含义。

It’s funny. A lot of my early observations in my little black book still hold true. Some of them don’t. I saw Mats Wilander for the first time in Bangkok in ’81. I studied him while he played a match and didn’t see anything special—no big serve, no big power, no outrageous shots. I figured he was just another solid Swedish player. I was right about Andre’s potential and wrong about Mats. Seven months later Wilander won the French Open. I had to make a few additions to the “Wilander” section of my black book.

>挺有意思的。我小黑本子里早年的一些观察，到现在还很适用。有些已经不适用了。我第一次在曼谷见到马茨·维兰德是在1981年。当时他正在打一场比赛，我观察了一会儿，没看出什么特别之处——没有大威力的发球，没有强大的力量，也没有什么离谱的击球。我当时觉得他不过是个普通的瑞典好手。我当初判断安德烈的潜力是对的，而对维兰德的判断却是错的。七个月后，维兰德赢得了法国公开赛。我不得不在我小黑本的“维兰德”部分做些补充。

I saw that Stefan Edberg generally preferred to go crosscourt on his forehand volley. I wrote down that his one-handed backhand was one of the best in the business (Lendl’s was the other). But I also noted that his forehand was not a weapon—extremely weak for a top player. His grip was bad and the whole stroke was not as impressive as everything else in his arsenal. Believe me, I made a note of that because there was very little I could write down about him that was negative.

>我注意到斯蒂芬·埃德伯格在正手 volley 时通常更倾向于向斜前方击球。我记下他的单手反手是当今最好的之一（伦德尔的也是其中之一）。不过我还注意到，他的正手并不是一件武器——对于一名顶级球员来说，他的正手非常弱。他的握拍方式不佳，整个击球动作也远不如他其他技术手段那样引人注目。相信我，我特意做了这个笔记，因为关于他，我几乎没有什么可以写负面内容的。

I kept jotting down little things I noticed. Then when I had a match coming up against a player I’d check my notebook and see what was there. It was sort of like scouting reports in baseball; helping the pitcher know a batter’s tendencies and letting the batter know what a pitcher likes to do in certain situations. It gave me a good idea of what to expect and helped get me thinking about what I had to do to keep that player from playing the shots he was most comfortable with.

>我一直在记下一些注意到的小细节。然后，当我即将和一名球员对战时，我就会翻看笔记本，看看上面写了些什么。这有点像棒球里的 scouting report（球探报告）；它帮助投手了解击球手的习惯，也让击球手知道投手在某些情况下喜欢怎么做。这让我对即将面对的对手有了一个清晰的预期，也促使我去思考该怎么做才能阻止对方发挥他最舒服的击球技巧。

>[!note]- 译者注：
scouting report 在棒球中指对对手的详细分析报告，此处用中文“球探报告”既保留原意，又符合中文体育语境。

## 6 Be a Good Scout 做一个优秀的侦察兵

Do your own scouting. When you’re at your club or the public courts watching a match with players you expect to face in the future, pay [[null|]]attention. Where do they make mistakes? What shots do they like to hit? Do they pound the overhead, but miss the volley? Do they hit a great angled crosscourt forehand, but miss it down the line? Are they fast getting to net, but slow changing directions?

>做好自己的情报工作。当你在俱乐部或者公共球场观看比赛时，如果对手是你未来可能遇到的球员，一定要留心观察。他们常在哪里出错？他们喜欢打什么样的球？他们是喜欢大力扣杀过顶球，却在小球上失误？还是他们擅长打出漂亮的斜线正手球，却在直线球上频频失手？他们虽然跑动迅速，抢网积极，但变向时却显得迟缓？

Do they have a great first serve and no second serve? Are they consistent with their backhand except when it’s attacked? Do they come to the net on short balls? Can they make a good approach shot, and does it have topspin or slice? What do they do when they need a forehand passing shot, crosscourt, or down the line? A backhand passing shot? How bad is their backhand? And what happens under pressure?

>他们的发球一发是否出色，二发是否薄弱？他们的反手是否只有在被攻击时才不稳？他们是否在短球时会上网？他们能否打出高质量的上网击球，这种击球是上旋还是下旋？当他们需要打出正手补位球、斜线球或直线球时，他们会怎么做？反手补位球呢？他们的反手有多差？而在压力之下，他们又会如何应对？

Look for patterns in their play. How do they win points? How do they lose points? What is their style of play?

>观察他们的打法中是否有规律。他们是如何赢分的？又是如何失分的？他们的打法风格是什么？

Watch a set and keep track. When they lose or win a point make a note of it. Remember what they do at game point or set point. You’ll see a pattern of their weaknesses and strengths. It’s part of what you’ll use before the match in your preparation and during the match in deciding how to play.

>观察一盘比赛，并做好记录。当他们赢得或失去一分时，记下他们的表现。记住他们在局点或盘点时的应对方式。你将发现他们弱点和优势的模式。这是你在赛前准备和比赛中决定如何应对时会用到的一部分内容。

## 7 Know Thyself (And Thy Tennis Game)

>7 认识你自己（以及你的网球打法）

Do to yourself what you are doing to others. Learn about your game. You may think you know your strengths and weaknesses, but you’re probably not as bad (or as good) as you think. Take notes after a match or have a friend watch one of your sets and keep track of “who’s doing what to whom.”

>对你自己做的事，也像你对别人所做的那样。了解自己的比赛。你可能以为自己清楚自己的优势和劣势，但你很可能并不像自己以为的那么差（或那么好）。比赛后做些笔记，或者让朋友观看你的一盘比赛，记录下“谁在对谁做些什么”。

>[!note]- 译者注：
此处“who’s doing what to whom”是一种比喻，意指在比赛中双方的互动和影响，翻译时采用直译并加注释以确保意思明确。

Take a lesson from your club pro. But not the kind of lesson you usually take. Sit down for coffee and ask him or her to tell you what they’d tell another player about how to beat you. How are you vulnerable? What are your strong points? Have them analyze your game (and your opponent’s, while you’re at it). And pay them for their time—it’s as valuable as hitting balls.

>向你的球馆教练取经吧，但不是你平时那种课程。坐下来喝杯咖啡，请求他或她告诉你，如果他们要告诉另一个球员如何击败你，他们会怎么说。你有哪些弱点？你的强项又是什么？让他们分析你的球风（顺便也分析一下你的对手）。并为他们的时间付钱——这和挥拍一样有价值。

>[!note]- 译者注：
此处“hitting balls”指练习击球，暗示教练的指导与实际练习一样重要。

When you play a match have a friend keep track of your game. Find out just how many double faults, unforced errors, and winners you hit. [[null|]]Believe me, it’s vitally important to a thinking player. In fact, a company in Florida just started manufacturing a pocket computer that allows you to do just that. The Tennis Analyst gives you information about why you won or lost. If you’re too busy to write it down this will keep track of it for you. It’s recognition of the importance of knowing who’s doing what to whom.

>当你进行一场比赛时，找个朋友帮你记录你的表现。弄清楚你一共发了多少个双发失误、有多少非受迫性失误，以及有多少制胜分。

>[!note]- 译者注：
此处“double faults”指双发失误，“unforced errors”指非受迫性失误，“winners”指制胜分。 相信我，这对一个善于思考的球员来说至关重要。事实上，佛罗里达州一家公司刚刚推出了一款便携式电脑，它能帮你完成这些记录工作。这款“网球分析师”（The Tennis Analyst）能告诉你你是如何赢或输的。如果你太忙而没时间记下来，它也能帮你记录。这是对了解“谁在对谁做些什么”这一重要性的认可。

## 8 “A Fool Is Someone Who Doesn’t Know What They Don’t Know.”—Anon.

>8 “一个蠢人是那些不知道自己不知道什么的人。”——匿名者

I believe the most effective athletes are the ones who know their weaknesses. Of course they know what they can do. But, just as important, they know what they can’t do.

>我认为最有效的运动员是那些清楚自己弱点的人。当然，他们知道自己能做什么。但同样重要的是，他们也清楚自己不能做什么。

The Snake was a beautiful example of that. He knew his limitations and worked around them. He knew he couldn’t really throw long so he worked the fourteen- or eighteen-yard pass to perfection. He knew he had slow feet so when he dropped back and got trapped he took the punishment instead of risking a stupid mistake running around.

>“蛇”就是这样一个绝佳例子。他清楚自己的局限，并想办法克服。他知道自己的投掷距离有限，因此他把十四码或十八码的传球练得炉火纯青。他也明白自己脚下慢，所以当他在回防时被包夹、陷入困境时，他宁愿承受惩罚，也不愿冒着犯下愚蠢失误的风险去盲目跑动。

He’d look for his spots and if they weren’t there he was willing to take the beating. He wouldn’t risk mistakes trying something he knew he couldn’t do. Stabler was a very intelligent athlete. He maximized his strengths.

>他会寻找自己的得分点，如果找不到，他也愿意承受失败。他不会冒险尝试自己明明做不到的事情。斯塔伯是个非常聪明的运动员，他善于发挥自己的优势。
