>我开始加快节奏，以应对他的减速策略。我略微加快了一点速度。这是一种错误的应对方式。这正是他想要的，而且确实奏效了。

>[!note]- 译者注：
此处“wrong response”意指“错误的应对方式”，强调反制策略的失误，而非字面意义上的“错误”。

[[null|]]Lendl breaks me right back. We’re on serve and I’m mad as hell. I had slipped off my focus. I had failed to follow my rules. Ivan’s distractions had kept me from doing it.

I had started to play from an emotional stance. I was reacting in the wrong way to what he was doing. Instead of telling myself that I was was winning and that patience would get me a victory I was worrying about whether he’d steal the match from me.

>我一开始就是带着情绪在打球。我对他的动作反应错了。我没有告诉自己我正在赢，而且只要保持耐心就能赢得比赛，反而一直在担心他会不会偷走这场比赛的胜利。

## 2 Turtle Stew at Its Finest

>2 龟汤的巅峰滋味 /no_think

Lendl has gotten the break back and is serving at 2–1 in the third. I am furious and determined to get the break back again, and immediately.

>伦德尔已经拿到了破发点，现在三局比分是2-1，他正在发球。我非常愤怒，决心立刻再次抢回破发点。

He gradually edges up to the line to serve. I get ready, but he’s not quite set to go into his motion yet. He has some business to take care of first. He begins with his eyelash routine. You’ve seen that on television. He plucks an eyelash and looks at it. Then he plucks another one and looks at it. Then another one. How he can have any eyelashes left at this point in his career I don’t know. He’s been plucking and looking at them for fifteen years. On this night at Stratton Mountain he starts plucking again.

>他慢慢靠近发球线准备发球。我已准备就绪，但他还没完全进入发球动作。他还有些事情要先处理。他开始做起了挑睫毛的例行公事。你一定在电视上见过这个场景。他一根一根地拔下睫毛，然后仔细端详。接着又拔一根，再仔细看看。再一根。我实在搞不懂，他在这个职业生涯的阶段怎么还能剩下睫毛。他拔了十五年睫毛，还一直盯着看。就在这个夜晚，他再次开始挑睫毛。

Next, he deliberates over which ball to serve with. He looks at one. Then he takes a long look at the other one. Then back to the first one. Then he starts rolling them around in his hand like dice. Again he looks at them individually. Finally he decides on a ball. I’m still in the ready position.

>接下来，他开始考虑要发哪个球。他先看了一下第一个球，然后仔细端详第二个球，又回到第一个球前仔细观察。接着，他把两个球在手中像骰子一样来回滚动。他又分别仔细看了看它们。最后，他做出了决定。我仍然保持着准备姿势。

Time to serve? Ivan starts bouncing the ball he’s finally chosen. Once. Twice. Three times he bounces it. Four times. Wait a minute. Must be some sweat in his eyes. He’s wiping off the sweat and running the sweatband over his eyebrows. I’m still in the ready position. He bounces the ball again. Here we go. Once. Twice. Three times. Hold everything! The grip must be slippery.

>该发球了？伊万开始跳动他最终选定的球。一次。两次。三次。四次。等等，难道他的眼睛里有汗？他擦拭着汗水，把护眉带滑过眉毛。我仍然保持准备姿势。他又开始跳动球。开始发球了。一次。两次。三次。暂停一切！球拍的握持一定变得滑了。

Ivan goes to his pocket for a handful of sawdust. Rubs it on the grip. I’m still in the ready position. He spins the racket a few times. Taps his shoe. Bounces the ball one more time. Stares over at me. I’m still there.

>伊万从口袋里掏出一把木屑，抹在球拍手柄上。我仍然保持准备姿势。他转了转球拍，敲了敲鞋。又把球弹了一下，然后盯着我这边看。我还在原地。

He serves. Ace! Lendl leads 15–love. I’m still in the ready position.

>他发球。Ace！伦德尔以15-0领先。我仍然保持着准备姿势。

Ivan goes through the whole act again on the second point. I’ve been [[null|]]in the ready position so long my legs could cramp. First the eyelash routine. Then rubbing the sweat off. Bouncing the balls. Putting on the sawdust. Bouncing the ball. Finally he serves. Fault! Into the net.

>伊万又在第二分重复了一遍这套动作。我已经在准备姿势中站了太久，腿都开始抽筋了。先是眨眼的仪式，然后擦掉汗水，接着拍球，撒上木屑，再拍球。最后他发球了。失误！球飞进了网里。

Ivan walks away from the baseline. Straightens the strings on his racket. Wipes off the sweat. Checks the balls. The whole thing all over again before his second serve. Maybe even longer.

>伊万离开底线，调整球拍弦线，擦去汗水，检查球。在第二次发球前，他又重复了一遍这一切，也许还要更久一些。

Here’s a fact. Rules or not, Lendl has been timed at fifty seconds between his first serve and his second serve when it’s suited him. This is Turtle Time tennis and it’s lethal. I’m starting to lose it. Ivan slows his way through the game to hold. The match is dead even.

>这是一个事实。无论有没有规则，当利嫩德处于优势时，他从第一发球到第二发球之间的时间总是被记录为50秒。这叫做“乌龟式网球”，极具杀伤力。我开始有点受不了了。伊万慢悠悠地推进着比赛，成功保发。比分胶着，势均力敌。

Ivan worked his plan to perfection. From 2–0 in the third I didn’t win another game. He won the match going away, six games in a row to win the match 6–2, 3–6, 6–2.

>伊凡完美地执行了他的计划。第三盘他以2-0领先后，我再也没有赢过一局。他带着领先优势赢得比赛，连续赢下六局，最终以6-2、3-6、6-2的比分拿下整场比赛。

The next time you watch him slow things down, study his strategy. Think about what it means if you’re on the other side of the net waiting. Waiting. You know he’s playing games out there and those games can beat you. You end up beating yourself.

>下次你看到他放慢节奏时，仔细研究他的策略。想想如果你站在网的另一边等待，这意味着什么。等待。你清楚他正在那里玩着游戏，而这些游戏可能会击败你。最终，你是在打败自己。

That night in the quarters at the Volvo International at Stratton Mountain Lendl managed to break down my concentration, rhythm, and timing. If I’d been smarter he wouldn’t have been able to do it.

>那晚在沃尔沃国际赛的八强赛中，伦德尔在我身上成功瓦解了我的专注力、节奏和时机。如果我当时更聪明一些，他就不至于做到这一点。

He kept up Turtle Time until he was up a break and then returned to his usual pace. I had been psyched out by one of the finest psychologists in the game. I had forgotten the rules about countering this tactic. If I’d remembered them I could have won.

>他一直维持着乌龟节奏，直到他领先一分，然后才恢复到平时的速度。我被这位比赛中最出色的心理教练之一给击溃了。我忘了如何应对这种战术。如果我记得这些规则，我本可以赢的。

## 3 How to Beat Slow Play Tactics

>3 如何击败慢拍战术

I’ve already mentioned that gamesmanship or psyching is designed to upset your concentration, to disrupt what you’re doing. You’ll rarely see a smart player who’s ahead do anything that could change the pattern of play. We want things to stay just the way they are. It’s when someone falls behind that they’ll try to get under the other player’s skin. The goal is to divert attention, to get the opponent thinking about the wrong things.

>我之前已经提到过，所谓的“战术心理战”或“心理战术”就是为了打乱你的专注力，干扰你正在进行的比赛节奏。你很少会看到那些领先的一方会采取任何可能改变比赛节奏的行动。我们希望比赛能按照现有的节奏继续下去。真正会采取心理战术的是那些落后的一方，他们试图激怒对手，让对手分心，把注意力转移到错误的事情上。

>[!note]- 这里“心理战术”指的是在比赛中通过心理手段干扰对手，使其失去专注力和节奏感。

The slow play tactic takes a variety of forms—all of the Lendl routine, [[null|]]plus tying shoelaces, walking over to get the farthest ball to serve with, or doing just about anything to stall before playing. One weekend player I’ve seen has a unique method of slowing things down. When he’s behind and serving he’ll toss the ball two or three times before he actually hits it. It’s like he’s practicing his toss. Every point. Not exactly illegal, but a major annoyance and one I hope Lendl doesn’t hear about.

>慢打战术有很多种形式——所有伦德尔（Lendl）的常规动作，[[null|]]再加上系鞋带、走到另一边去捡最远的球来发球，或者在真正开始打球之前做几乎任何事情来拖延时间。我曾经见过一位业余球员有独特的慢打方式。当他落后并且需要发球时，他会把球抛上天两三次，才真正击球。这就像他在练习抛球动作。每一分都这么做。这并不算违法，但确实非常烦人，我希望伦德尔不会听到这件事。

Remember, no smart player starts changing things when they’re ahead and rolling right along. But when your opponent is behind and starts playing little games to upset you, it’s good to have a plan to protect yourself. When it comes to slow play, here’s how to keep it from damaging your game.

>记住，真正聪明的球员不会在自己处于领先、形势一片大好的时候就开始改变策略。但当对手落后时，却开始玩一些小手段来扰乱你，这时你就需要一个计划来保护自己。谈到慢节奏的比赛，这里有一些方法，可以帮助你避免它损害自己的整体表现。

## 4 Fight Slow Play, Slowly

>4 慢打慢玩，慢慢来 /no_think

Turtles know you have to wait for them to start play. It’s tough to do, but you have to be patient. When you notice what looks like a slowdown in the action, be flexible. Don’t press. Don’t force it and get impatient. There’s no way you can make someone play faster if they’re intentionally (or even unintentionally) slowing down the match tempo.

>乌龟知道你得等它们开始比赛。这很不容易，但你必须保持耐心。当你察觉到比赛似乎出现了节奏放缓的情况时，要灵活应对。不要急于求成，不要强迫，也不要变得焦躁。如果你的对手有意或无意地放慢了比赛节奏，你根本无法让他们加快速度。

Let the Turtle crawl through their whole range of business. Wait. Wait. And wait. When they’ve finally gotten around to beginning play, just before they serve, it’s your turn to slow things down. Hold up your hand and step away from the line. Tie your shoe. Step away from the line and adjust your strings or wipe off the sweat. Let the Turtle wait for you. Then when it’s your turn to serve, give them a taste of their own slow medicine. Bounce the ball eight or ten times. Do your own version of Turtle Time. They’ll usually get the idea.

>让乌龟慢慢爬完它的整个过程。等一等。再等一等。当他们终于开始发球前，就是你放慢节奏的时候了。举起你的手，退后一步，远离底线。系一下鞋带，退后一步，调整球拍弦或是擦掉汗水。让乌龟为你等待。等到你发球的时候，就给他们一点他们自己那慢吞吞的“药方”。把球弹跳八次或十次，做你自己的“乌龟时间”。他们通常会明白你的意思。

I know what you’re thinking. All this game playing is going to disrupt your thinking and rhythm. You’ll still come out behind. Not necessarily. You are now taking the initiative. You’re not just being passive. You’re not standing there being worked over. You’re doing something to defend yourself. It can be a big psychological boost.

>我知道你心里在想什么。这种打球方式会打乱你的思路和节奏，你还是会落在对手后面。不一定如此。你现在主动出击了。你不再被动地接受对方的进攻，你不是站在那里任人宰割。你正在采取行动来保护自己。这可以带来很大的心理优势。

>[!note]- 译者注：
此处“psychological boost”意为心理上的提振，可译为“心理优势”或“心理提振”，根据上下文选择更贴切的表达。

I have a little rule I follow. Before a point I don’t like to stand at the baseline waiting for my opponent to arrive and get ready. It creates [[null|]]tension and makes me rush into the point once they do get set. If the other player is not ready to proceed when I am I take a few steps away from the line and bounce the ball several times if I’m serving. If I’m receiving I’ll back away and straighten my strings. Then as they approach their line I’ll do the same. But I don’t just stand there and wait. Neither should you. Whether they’re making you wait for them intentionally or unintentionally doesn’t matter. You’re still just standing there waiting, and that doesn’t help your game.

>我有一个小规则，我总是遵守。在一分开始前，我不喜欢站在底线等着对手过来准备。这样会制造紧张情绪，一旦对方准备好，我就会仓促上阵。如果对方还没准备好，而我已经准备好了，我会后退几步，如果是我发球，我会把球反复弹几下；如果是我接发球，我会后退几步并整理一下球拍线。当对方靠近底线时，我也会这样做。但我不会只是站在那里等待。你们也不应该这样。无论他们是有意还是无意地让你等待，这都不重要。你还是站在那里等着，这对你打球没有任何帮助。

Lendl is one of the best at maintaining a good pace when he’s leading in a match. When he gets ahead he’s methodical and merciless. But when he’s behind, or it’s close in the third, fourth, or fifth set, it’s like watching water evaporate when he goes into Turtle Time. He’ll drive you bananas, and in the process get you totally out of your game. And officials through the years have cut him more slack, as they have with McEnroe, than they do for most other players.

>伦德尔在领先时是最擅长保持良好节奏的选手之一。一旦取得优势，他就会有条不紊、毫不留情地施压。但当他落后，或者在第三、第四、第五盘比分胶着时，就会进入“乌龟时间”——就像看着水蒸发一样，他变得缓慢而难以捉摸。他会让你抓狂，同时彻底打乱你的节奏。多年来，裁判们对他的宽容程度，就像对麦肯罗一样，远高于其他大多数球员。

>[!note]- “乌龟时间”是比喻伦德尔在比分胶着时节奏变慢、心态变得保守的阶段，类似于乌龟缓慢爬行的状态。

I’ve found that it helps a great deal if I can do something myself when I’m being worked over by a psych job. Instead of just standing there absorbing the routine my opponent is putting on, I want to respond. How? By doing to him what he’s doing to me. With Lendl that time I let him get inside my head and destroy my composure. He acted. I reacted.

>我发现，当我被对手的心理战攻击时，如果我能主动采取一些行动，会对我有很大帮助。我不想只是站在那里被动接受对手施加的那些套路，而是想要做出回应。我该怎么做呢？就是用他对我做的事来应对他。那次对阵伦德尔时，我让他钻进我的心里，破坏我的冷静。他发动攻击，我则做出反应。

Against Lendl it didn’t work just to stand there and do nothing. When he took fifty seconds to get ready (twenty-five seconds is allowed) for his second serve, I should have asked the umpire to keep him to time. If that didn’t work, the next time he went through the whole preserve routine I should have stepped away from the line when he was all done with it and toweled off (or tied my shoes, straightened my strings, plucked my eyelash, anything). I should have slowed the match down and forced him to stand around.

>面对伦德尔时，仅仅站在那儿什么也不做是行不通的。当他为第二发球准备花了足足五十秒（允许的时间是二十五秒），我本该请求裁判让他守时。如果这不管用，下一次他再做那些繁琐的准备动作时，我应该在他做完之后离开底线，擦擦汗（或者系鞋带、整理球线、拔掉睫毛，总之做点什么）。我应该放慢比赛节奏，迫使他站在那儿无所事事。

>[!note]- 译者注：
此处“preserve routine”原意是“保存程序”或“例行公事”，但在网球语境中指的是球员在发球前的固定准备动作，因此译为“繁琐的准备动作”更贴切。

But here’s why Lendl is a master. He started working on me before I recognized the tactics. And before I knew it was acting out of anger and frustration. I was ready to scream, he tied me up so bad.

>但这就是为什么伦德尔是个大师。他在我意识到这些战术之前就开始对我施压了。在我甚至没意识到自己是在出于愤怒和挫败感而行动时，他已经把我压制得喘不过气来。我几乎要大声喊出来了，他把我绑得那么紧。

Is that why I lost? No. But it was part of the reason. Match dynamics are volatile, and Lendl had taken control of them at a crucial moment. I lost because I didn’t respond correctly.

>这就是我输掉比赛的原因吗？不完全是。但这也是其中一部分原因。比赛局势瞬息万变，而伦德尔在关键时刻掌控了局面。我输掉比赛，是因为我没有做出正确的回应。

>[!note]- 译者注：
此处“didn’t respond correctly”意指在心理或战术层面未能做出恰当反应，而非单纯的技术失误。

[[null|]]And just so you don’t get the wrong impression, at last count Ivan Lendl “owned” me. He’s mentally and physically very tough. Most of the times he’s beaten me it’s been because he’s so doggone good. However, my point is that on those occasions where I’ve had a chance to win he knew how to use another aspect of the competition: gamesmanship. I’m not complaining. It comes with the territory.

Ivan understands the psychological aspects of the game very well. Here’s a small example. Early in my career when he didn’t view me as a threat to him I was occasionally invited to his estate in Connecticut to be his hitting partner. We’d work out on his court (the one he has resurfaced each year to exactly match the court surfaces at the U.S. Open). When I began to start playing him tougher in tournaments, taking him to three sets occasionally, the invitations stopped.

>伊万非常了解这项运动的心理层面。举个例子吧。在我职业生涯早期，当他还不把我视为威胁时，我偶尔会被邀请到他在康涅狄格州的庄园，做他的击球搭档。我们会在他的球场上训练（那个球场每年都会重新铺设，以完全匹配美网的球场表面）。当我开始在比赛中对他采取更激烈的打法，偶尔能将他拖入三盘大战时，这些邀请就停止了。

Ivan knew (and so did I) that the sessions were helping my confidence in playing him out on tour. He was losing his aura because I was getting familiar with his game and forgetting any sense of awe his image might once have created. When he recognized that it was happening he found a new hitting partner. He’s smart, both on and off the court.

>伊万知道（我也知道），这些训练正在帮助我提升在巡回赛中对阵他的信心。他正在失去那种令人敬畏的光环，因为我对他打法越来越熟悉，也忘了他过去那种形象所营造的敬畏感。当他意识到这一点时，他就换了一个新的击球搭档。他既聪明，场内也聪明，场外同样聪明。

## 5 It Can Happen to You, Too: Windmill and Dr. J

>5 你也会遇到这种情况：风车与“J博士”

You’re thinking that that kind of stuff (slow play) never happens where you play. Great. And I hope it never does. But it can. I’ve saved the greatest “slow-down-the-match” gambit I know of for last. In case you think Turtle Time only goes on at the professional level, you’re about to see that it can occur at every level of tennis, including tennis clubs and public courts. If something like this ever happens to you, just admit you’ve been outfoxed and go home sadder but wiser.

>你可能觉得，像这种慢悠悠打比赛（慢节奏）的事情，根本不会在你打球的地方发生。很好，我也希望它永远不会发生。但事实上，它可能发生。我打算把我知道的最厉害的“拖慢比赛”策略留到最后讲。如果你以为“乌龟时间”只会在职业比赛中出现，那接下来你就要见识到，它其实可以在网球的每一个层次上发生，包括网球场俱乐部和公共球场。如果有一天你遭遇了这种情况，就承认你被对手耍了，回家时虽然更沮丧，但也会更明智。

Again, it’s at the San Francisco Tennis Club. Dale Crase and Julius Colbert (their real names) are playing the semifinals of the club’s B tournament. They call their own lines and enforce their own rules (which is what led to the problem). Let me describe their styles of play.

>再次回到旧金山网球俱乐部。戴尔·克雷和朱利乌斯·科尔伯特（他们的真实姓名）正在参加俱乐部B级赛事的半决赛。他们自己判罚球线，也自己制定规则（这正是引发问题的原因）。让我描述一下他们的打法风格。

Crase is a born Retriever whose nickname is Windmill because of his [[null|]]strange serve. He stands with his serving arm straight down at his side holding the racket. Then he tosses the ball up, and without bending his elbow he swirls his arm around to hit it. He never bends his elbow. It looks like a windmill, hence the nickname.

>克雷斯天生就是个“ retriever”（指在比赛中积极抢网、主动进攻的球员），他的绰号叫“风车”，因为他的发球方式非常奇特。他站在发球位置时，手臂完全伸直垂在身体一侧，握着球拍。接着他将球抛向空中，手臂不弯曲，直接以手腕为轴，像风车一样旋转手臂击球。他从不弯曲肘部。这种发球动作看起来就像一台风车，因此得名“风车”。

Colbert (nicknamed Dr. J) is a good athlete who played minor league baseball before an injury stopped his rise to the big leagues. He plays full throttle, all or nothing. Goes for everything and makes spectacular shots occasionally. Ocassionally. But let’s get to the match.

>科伯特（昵称“J博士”）是一位优秀的运动员，曾效力于小联盟棒球，但一次伤病阻断了他进军大联盟的路。他打球全情投入，要么全取，要么全失。他总是全力以赴，偶尔还会打出令人惊叹的精彩击球。偶尔如此。不过，我们还是回到比赛本身。

Windmill wins the first set easily 6–2, and is ahead in the second 4–1. Looks like a quick win and a trip to the finals. But Dr. J starts to get it going. He makes a few of those spectacular shots and gets his energy level and attitude way up. Suddenly he’s won six of the next seven games to win the set 7–5! Sets are even at 1–all. The momentum has changed dramatically, and Windmill knows it.

>温米尔以6比2轻松拿下首盘，并在第二盘以4比1领先，看起来即将赢得比赛并晋级决赛。但杰博士开始发力了。他打出几记精彩的制胜球，士气和斗志瞬间高涨。突然之间，他接下来的七局中赢下六局，以7比5逆转拿下第二盘！双方战成1比1平。局势发生了巨大的转变，温米尔也清楚地意识到了这一点。

Now it’s the changeover. (Club protocol was to break out new balls and make a changeover before the third set in tournaments.) They sit down for the break. Windmill asks for a little favor. Would it be okay to run up to his locker and get a dry shirt? Dr. J has no problem with that. They’re buddies and who’s going to tell a friend they can’t put on a dry shirt. Windmill leaves. It takes Dr. J a little time to sense that something is going on. Four minutes go by. Seven minutes. Thirteen minutes. Dr. J is starting to boil. He can’t believe what’s happening. Eighteen minutes and still no Windmill.

>现在是换边休息时间。（俱乐部的规定是在第三盘开始前更换新球并进行换边休息。）他们坐下来休息。温米尔向杰医生提出一个小请求：能不能让他跑回更衣室拿一件干爽的衬衫？杰医生对此毫无异议。他们关系很好，谁会跟朋友说不能穿干衬衫呢？温米尔离开了。但杰医生花了点时间才察觉到有什么不对劲。四分钟过去了，七分钟，十三分钟……杰医生开始坐不住了，他简直不敢相信发生了什么。十八分钟过去了，温米尔还是没回来。

Finally, after twenty-two minutes, here comes Windmill, looking like he’s just had a good night’s sleep, a massage, and a cool drink. And it was almost true. He had gotten a dry shirt. He had put it on. But not before he’d taken a shower, shaved, relieved himself, and eaten a candy bar.

>终于，在二十分钟之后，温米尔出现了，看起来就像刚刚睡了个好觉，做了按摩，还喝了杯凉饮。这几乎是真的。他确实换上了干爽的衬衫，已经穿上了。不过在穿上之前，他洗了澡、刮了胡子、解决了生理需求，还吃了一根能量棒。

Dr. J was seething. His buddy had “gamed” him. What does he say when Windmill gets back to the court? Nothing. Ego gets into it. He refuses to declare a default because he’d rather take it out on Windmill on the court, really pound on him, nail him 6–0 in the third. You can guess the rest. The score was 6–0 in the third. Only Windmill had the 6. Dr. J had the 0. He couldn’t hit a ball within two feet of the court. He was out of his skull and lost it. He didn’t know how to deal with slow play and delay tactics. And he didn’t know how to deal with anger.

>贾博士怒火中烧。他的朋友“耍了”他。当温米尔回到球场时，他会说什么呢？什么也不会说。自尊心作祟，他拒绝宣布对方“弃权”，他宁愿在球场上好好教训温米尔，狠狠地打他，以6比0赢得第三盘。结局可想而知。第三盘的比分是6比0，只有温米尔拿到了这6分，贾博士则一球未得。他连离球场两英尺内的球都打不中，彻底失去了理智，彻底崩溃了。他不知道如何应对慢节奏的打法和拖延战术，也不知道如何控制自己的愤怒。

[[null|]]Maybe Dr. J should have disqualified Windmill (although he gave him permission to leave). Maybe he should have complained or left the court himself. Maybe he should have gone home for lunch. Instead, he got his lunch handed to him. He got psyched out, and he paid for it. Just like I paid for it against Lendl. If it ever happens to you, be ready. Don’t let a Turtle break down your concentration and your game.



# [[null|]][[null|]]14

>[[null|]][[null|]]14

# Agassi: Breaking the Speed Limit

>阿加西：突破速度极限

Andre Agassi moves faster between points than some players do during points. He’s rushing all the time. He’s got so much energy he could make a hummingbird nervous. But he lives on caffeine and sugar, so I guess you’d expect him to be a little on the quick side.

>安德烈·阿加西在得分之间移动的速度比一些球员在得分时还要快。他总是匆匆忙忙。他拥有如此充沛的精力，甚至能让蜂鸟都感到紧张。但他靠咖啡因和糖分维持生命，所以我想你大概会预料到他有点急躁。

I like Andre and think he’s great for the game. Some players resent his Las Vegas flash and the showboating, but it brings people through the gate. Plus, Andre doesn’t intentionally screw around with you out on the court. Unintentionally, he does, because Andre moves you through a match faster than anybody in the business. That can destroy your game.

>我喜欢安德烈，认为他非常适合这项运动。有些球员对他在拉斯维加斯的张扬作风和爱耍花招感到不满，但这些行为确实能吸引观众入场。此外，安德烈在球场上并不会故意刁难你。他并非有意为之，但事实上，安德烈能在比赛中比任何人都更快地推进节奏，这往往会破坏你的比赛状态。

>[!note]- 译者注：
此处“screw around with you”意为“故意刁难你”或“戏弄你”，根据语境选择更符合中文表达习惯的译法。

Rushing into points is bad. Rushing into points means you’re rushing through points. As we saw with Lendl, rushing creates both mental and physical mistakes. It throws off your rhythm in serving. It creates all kinds of timing problems. It just takes you out of your game totally. Unless you’re Andre Agassi. Andre likes rushing.

>仓促进入得分是不好的。仓促进入得分意味着你在得分过程中草率行事。正如我们从伦德尔身上看到的那样，仓促会导致心理和身体上的失误。它会打乱你的发球节奏，制造各种时间协调问题。它会让你完全脱离自己的比赛状态。除非你是阿加西。阿加西喜欢仓促行事。

The quick play artist doesn’t necessarily catch you by surprise during a match (e.g. by serving quick). Instead, what throws you off is the general tempo at which they do things between and during points. You never really get comfortable, set, and focused. They move you into the point immediately. Andre gets it going so fast when he’s serving that you feel like you could miss something if you blink. He doesn’t walk. He trots. He could be the poster child for the caffeine industry.

>这种打法快节奏的选手并不一定是在比赛中让你措手不及（例如通过快速发球）。真正让你感到不安的是他们在得分之间和得分过程中所保持的整体节奏。你很难真正放松、稳定和专注，他们总是立刻就把你带入到一个得分点中。安德烈发球时动作如此之快，快得让你觉得只要眨一下眼，就可能错过什么。他不是走，而是小跑。他简直就是咖啡因产业的代言人。

>[!note]- 这里用“咖啡因产业的代言人”来比喻他精力充沛、永不停歇的状态，类似于中文中“咖啡因上瘾者”或“精力充沛的代表”的表达。

Fast play is common at clubs and public courts. You’ll often run into players who are moving along just enough faster than you to throw you off. They serve immediately when you get to the line. They don’t sit down on the changeovers. One club player used to spin the racket for [[null|]]the serve while he and his opponent were still in the locker room! He’d have the other player rushing before they even got to the court. It does happen. And when it does it requires a little management.

>快速的比赛在俱乐部和公共球场上很常见。你经常会遇到一些球员，他们只比你快那么一点点，足以让你措手不及。你刚跑到球网边，他们就立刻发球。他们不会在换边时停下来休息。有位俱乐部球员甚至曾在更衣室里就开始旋转球拍准备发球，他和对手还在更衣室里，他就让对方球员提前开始紧张准备。这种情况确实会发生。当它发生时，就需要一点心理上的应对和管理。

## 1 Know Your Speed Limit

>1 知道自己的速度极限

It’s easier to deal with a player who’s rushing you along—a Rabbit—than with a Turtle. The main thing you have to be aware of is that it’s happening. Usually you let yourself get caught up in the quicker pace without realizing it. You’re never quite set, there isn’t time for composing yourself or analyzing what’s going on in front of you. And those are two crucial elements of my game. I want constantly to be thinking about what’s happening in the match. If you start playing faster than you normally do, you get mentally sloppy and physically careless.

>对付一个急于求成的对手——“兔子”——比对付一个节奏缓慢的对手——“乌龟”——要容易得多。你最重要的是要意识到这种情况正在发生。通常你都会在不知不觉中被对方更快的节奏带跑。你总是处于一种未完全准备好的状态，没有时间整理自己的状态或分析场上的情况。而这正是我打法中的两个关键要素。我希望在比赛中始终保持对局势的思考。如果你开始比平时打得更快，你的心理状态就会变得松散，身体动作也会变得粗心大意。

So, what do you do? Simple. Know your speed limit. That means knowing the tempo you are comfortable playing at. Don’t let your opponent exceed it. Be aware of when it happens. Don’t be a good guy and just go along with it. Slow it down! When your opponent jumps up to the service line before you’re set, hold up your hand and walk away. If he doesn’t sit down for a break on the changeovers, be sure you do. Don’t be afraid to towel off, adjust strings, or tie your shoes. The quick play artist will run you off the court if you let him.

>所以，你该怎么做呢？很简单。了解自己的“速度限制”。也就是说，知道自己最舒服的比赛节奏。不要让对手超过这个节奏。要时刻留意他是否已经突破了这个界限。不要做“好心人”，一味地顺着他。要放慢节奏！当对手在你还没准备好时就提前冲到发球线前，你就举起手来，走开。如果他在换边时没有休息，你一定要休息。不要害怕擦汗、调弦或系鞋带。如果让那个节奏快的球员按自己的节奏来打，他很快就会把你逼出场地。

In 1991 I played Andre in a semi-final match, and was fortunate enough to beat him in straight sets. Going into that match I was very intent on playing at my tempo: D.R.M. He wasn’t. Lots of times he’d quickly skate back to the service line and want to get it going before I was ready. I made very sure I was set, made sure we didn’t start rushing through points and games. I’d towel off, tie my shoe, check my strings. I checked my strings a lot that match. With Andre you’ve got to do that or he’ll roll you right away. (In the finals of the ’93 Volvo/San Francisco he got me back, winning in three sets and playing great tennis.)

>1991年，我与阿加西在半决赛中对阵，幸运地以直落两盘击败了他。进入那场比赛时，我非常专注于按照自己的节奏来打：D.R.M。（即“慢慢来”）而阿加西则不是这样。他经常迅速滑回发球线，迫不及待地想开始进攻，而我还未准备就绪。我非常确保自己已经准备好，确保我们不急于进行每一个得分和每一分。我会擦擦汗，系好鞋带，检查球拍的弦线。这场比赛中，我反复检查了无数次球拍的弦线。对付阿加西，你必须这么做，否则他马上就会把你打垮。（在1993年沃尔沃/旧金山公开赛决赛中，他成功复仇，以三盘战胜我，打出了非常精彩的比赛。）

You can do the same thing with the Rabbits you come up against. Protect your tempo. Guard your rhythm. When you’re on the other side [[null|]]of the net from a Rabbit, be very sure you don’t let them dictate how fast the match moves along.

>你也可以用同样的方法应对那些你遇到的“兔子”对手。保护你的节奏，守住你的步调。当你在网的另一边面对一个“兔子”时，一定要确保自己不让对方掌控比赛的节奏和速度。

## 2 Playing Possum

>第二章 撒谎装死

There’s another little situation or gambit that comes up occasionally that’s worth mentioning as long as we’re talking about gamesmanship. It’s called “playing possum.” It’s when a player will fake or exaggerate an injury. It can have a very negative effect on the possum’s victim.

>还有一个小情况或策略，偶尔也会出现，值得在我们讨论比赛策略时提一下。它被称为“装死”（playing possum）。当一名球员假装或夸大自己受伤时，就会使用这种策略。这种行为对“装死”的受害者可能会产生非常负面的影响。

You know how it works at the club level. The possum’s opponent (you) are in a Catch-22 situation. The match really doesn’t count if you win because your opponent is injured, right? If you lose, you’ve lost to somebody who’s playing hurt. Very embarrassing. It’s a no-win situation.

>你明白在俱乐部级别比赛是怎么回事吗？那只负鼠（你的对手）正陷入一个两难境地。如果你赢了，这场比赛其实根本不算数，因为你的对手受伤了，对吧？如果你输了，那你就是输给了一个带伤作战的人，非常丢脸。这简直就是个无解的困局。

It almost always takes you out of your game. You don’t try as hard. You don’t concentrate as well. You play sloppy. And you can lose. That’s when your opponent will say as you leave the court, “You know my leg didn’t bother me as much as I thought it would. Good match and better luck next time.” You’ve just been had by a possum.

>这几乎总是会把你从比赛中拉出来。你不再那么努力，注意力也不如从前集中，打得马马虎虎，甚至可能输掉比赛。这时你的对手会在你离开球场时说：“我知道我的腿没我想象的那么难受。刚才的比赛打得好，下次好运。”你刚刚被一只松鼠骗了。

>[!note]- “被松鼠骗了”是英语中“被某人骗了”的俚语表达，此处意为“被对手骗了”，译为“被一只松鼠骗了”既保留了原意，又符合中文表达习惯。

## 3 Save Your Sympathy

>3 態度要堅決

Remember this: Whether an opponent’s injury is real, exaggerated, or fake, it gets no sympathy or attention from you until after the match. If your opponent is healthy enough to walk out on the court and continue play, then he’s healthy enough to deserve your best effort. Ignore anything he tells you about his aches and pains. Assume it’s just for effect. Because whether the injury is real or not, it can have a negative impact on you. It can take you out of your concentration.

>记住这一点：无论对手的伤势是真实、夸大还是假的，比赛结束后你才可能对他的伤情表示同情或关注。如果对手健康到足以走出场外继续比赛，那他就是足够健康，值得你全力以赴。忽略他对你说的任何关于疼痛或不适的话。把它们当作是表演效果。因为无论伤势是否真实，它都可能对你产生负面影响，让你分心。

Case in point: I played Jimmy Connors in an A.T.P. semifinal match early in 1993. He actually came limping out on the court. I’m serious. He was noticeably favoring his right foot because of an ongoing problem with bone spurs. Now, Jimmy is a great manipulator and [[null|]]showman, so I’m trying to ignore what’s going on—no sympathy for this devil.

>典型案例就是：1993年初，我与吉米·康纳斯在ATP半决赛中交手。他当时甚至一瘸一拐地走到球场上，我可不是在开玩笑。他的右脚明显有伤，因为骨刺问题一直困扰着他。现在，吉米是个非常擅长操控局面的高手，也是个出色的表演者，所以我努力不去关注他现在的状况——我对这个魔鬼可没有同情心。

But during the first set, it gets so bad he’s actually pulling up on some shots because of the pain. This is no act. I cruise in the first set 6–1, and it only takes about ten minutes. I figure he’ll default during the changeover. He doesn’t.

>但在第一盘比赛中，他的伤势已经严重到实在无法继续，甚至在击球时都不得不因疼痛而拉住手臂。这不是演戏。我轻松地以6比0拿下第一盘，只用了大约十分钟。我本以为他会在盘间休息时退赛，但他没有。

I’m trying to figure out what’s going on. There’s no way he can finish the match—he probably can’t even finish the set. This is awkward. The crowd can see he’s struggling, and they’re cheering his valiant efforts. Suddenly I’m a villain beating on a legend who’s in pain. So what happens? Jimmy starts beating me. He goes up 4–1 and then retires. I know Connors was thinking, “Gotcha, Gilbert!” And he had.

>我试着弄清楚到底发生了什么。他根本不可能赢得这场比赛，甚至可能连这一盘都赢不了。这太尴尬了。观众们看得出来他正在挣扎，却还在为他的努力鼓掌喝彩。突然之间，我变成了一个欺负传奇人物的坏人，而他正忍受着痛苦。那接下来会发生什么呢？吉米开始击败我，他先以4比1领先，然后选择退赛。我知道康纳斯心里想的是：“你上当了，吉尔伯特！”他确实做到了。

Save the sympathy.

>别指望同情。



# [[null|]][[null|]]15

>[[null|]][[null|]]15

# How to Handle Hooking

>如何应对“挂拍”

>[!note]- 译注：“挂拍”是网球术语，指球员在击球时拍头停留在击球点上方，导致击球动作不完整，通常用于防守或过渡击球。

Occasionally in matches you’ll run into the player who cheats on line calls (hooking). Also, you have to deal with the player who unintentionally makes a mistake on a call. Both situations are tough to deal with. I’ve seen both sides of the coin. I’ve given bad calls and I’ve gotten them.

>有时在比赛中，你可能会遇到故意假报线位的球员（hooking）。你也必须应对那些无意中做出错误判罚的球员。这两种情况都很难处理。我经历过这两种情况，我既做过糟糕的判罚，也吃过别人糟糕判罚的亏。

As a junior player I had a terrible reputation for line calls. It was probably deserved. I wanted to win so much that I couldn’t give the other player the benefit of the doubt. Then I corrected the problem and probably ended up getting as many bad calls as I gave. It was never anything to be proud of.

>作为一名青少年球员，我曾经因为经常抱怨边线裁判的判罚而臭名昭著。这大概也是实至名归。我当时渴望胜利到了一种程度，以至于无法给对手任何善意的假设。后来我纠正了这个问题，结果反而可能因为自己判罚失误而得到更多不利的判罚。这从来都不值得骄傲。

>[!note]- 译者注：
此处“line calls”指的是球员对边线裁判判罚的抱怨，翻译时保留了原意并使表达更符合中文习惯。

What do you do when it happens in your match, especially during a tournament? Here are some suggestions.

>当你在比赛中遇到这种情况，尤其是在巡回赛中，你会怎么做？以下是一些建议。

Don’t argue about the marginal call. You may be wrong. But don’t let a blatantly bad call go by without action. You don’t have to be a jerk, but let your opponent know you aren’t going to let them force you to hit to a smaller court than the ones marked by the white lines.

>不要争论那些细微的判罚。你可能错了，但不要让一个明显错误的判罚就这样溜过去而不采取行动。你不必表现得像个混蛋，但要让对手明白，你不会让他们强迫你去打一个比白线标记的球场还要小的场地。

>[!note]- 译注：这里的“smaller court”指的是球员在比赛中因误判而被迫缩小击球范围，是一种心理战术上的表达。

Start easy. Ask if they’re sure of the call. Give it a longer look. Be controlled. Give your opponent a chance to be generous. If you start out accusing them of bad calls, they’ll just get defensive and probably give you more of the same. An early notice that you expect fair calls will often sharpen up the opponent’s eyes. In fact, after you’ve nudged someone on a bad call, you’ll almost always see that for a while afterward they’ll call balls good that are out. Guilty conscience probably. So, initially, be diplomatic.

>从简单的地方开始。询问他们是否确定这个判罚。仔细观察一下。保持冷静。给对手一个机会，让他们表现得大度一些。如果你一开始就在指责他们判罚不公，他们只会变得防御性更强，很可能还会继续给你同样的回应。事先表明你期待公平判罚，往往能提高对手的注意力。事实上，当你提醒对方某个判罚不公后，接下来的一段时间里，他们往往会把一些出界球判为有效。这大概是因为内心有愧吧。所以一开始，要讲究方式方法。

>[!note]- 译者注：
此处“be diplomatic”意为“讲究方式方法”或“委婉表达”，以避免直接冲突，符合中文表达习惯。

If diplomacy doesn’t work, you know what most club players will do? They respond in kind, giving bad calls themselves. This isn’t the way [[null|]]to go. Among other things, it takes you out of your concentration. You get caught up in the issue of good or bad line calls and forget about everything else. Believe me, it is rare when line calls make the difference in a match, so don’t let them disrupt your mental management.

>如果外交手段不起作用，你猜大多数俱乐部球员会怎么做？他们会以同样的方式回应，自己也做出错误的判罚。这可不是个好办法。首先，这会把你从专注中拉出来。你就会陷入对线裁是否正确的好坏争论中，而忘记其他一切。相信我，线裁的判罚在比赛中真正起决定性作用的情况其实非常少见，所以不要让它们干扰你的心理管理。

Hooking back doesn’t work. Screaming doesn’t work. (I’ve been screamed at and done my own share of screaming. It doesn’t work.) But you can say that you thought the call was wrong. Hey, don’t be afraid to smile when you say it. Give your opponent a chance to be nice.

>“叫分”没用，大吼也没用。（我被吼过，也吼过别人，没用。）但你可以表示你认为裁判的判罚错了。嘿，说这话的时候别怕笑，给对手一个好脸色。

If it continues, address the problem directly. Most tournaments have a person available to come in and assume responsibility for the line calls. Have them do it so you can concentrate on your game.

>如果情况持续下去，就直接面对问题。大多数比赛都会有专人负责判线工作，可以请他接手，这样你就能专注于自己的比赛了。

Ultimately, the best thing you can do with someone who is a chronic hooker is to cross them off your list of opponents. When you do find yourself in a situation where you can’t choose your opponent (like a tournament), stand up for your rights. Try what I suggested. It usually helps.

>最终，面对一个总是喜欢“钩子”（即不断设局或制造麻烦）的人，你最好的选择就是把他们从你的对手名单上划掉。当你确实无法选择对手（比如在比赛中），那就站出来维护自己的权利。尝试我建议的方法，通常会有效。

The most exciting part of tennis is when you are fully engaged physically and mentally. Psych jobs are occasionally part of what goes on. It’s important to recognize when you’re being psyched and to know what you can do about it because what affects you mentally has an effect on you physically.

>网球最令人兴奋的部分，是你在身体和心理上都全神贯注的时候。心理战术有时也会成为比赛的一部分。重要的是要能识别自己是否被激发出心理状态，并且知道在这种情况下自己该怎么做，因为影响你心理状态的事情，也会影响到你的身体表现。

>[!note]- 译者注：
这里的“psyched”指的是被激发、被激励的心理状态，类似于“被点燃”或“进入状态”的意思。

When I was coming up and playing junior tennis events, the psyching was brutal. It was good conditioning for what was ahead in the pros.

>当我刚开始打青少年网球比赛时，心理战简直残酷至极。这为我日后转入职业赛场做了极好的心理准备。

You’ll find a lot of satisfaction in being able to defend yourself when the other player (who just might be a friend) starts to reach into a bag of tricks to disrupt your game. Even if what they’re doing is unintentional, you should be willing and able to deal with it effectively.

>当你发现对方球员（说不定还是个朋友）开始动用一些小手段来干扰你的比赛时，你能够有效应对，这会带给你很大的满足感。即使对方的行为是无心的，你也应该愿意并且有能力妥善处理。

>[!note]- 译者注：
此处“bag of tricks”意为“各种手段或策略”，在中文中用“小手段”表达更符合语境。

I knew a high school player who would measure the height of the net before every match. It was a great little routine he went through, and it put the opponent in a reactive or passive role. The guy measuring the net was taking control right away. He was saying that he was the more conscientious player, that he was the one who made the extra effort. He was the one determining if the net was okay. The opponent would stand around waiting for this guy to make his ruling. Great little bit of gamesmanship.

>我认识一位高中球员，每次比赛前他都会先量一下网的高度。这是一套非常棒的小仪式，能让对手陷入被动或反应性的状态。那个量网的人一开始就掌握了主动权。他在暗示自己是个更认真负责的球员，是那个付出额外努力的人。他决定网是否符合标准，而对手只能站着等待他的裁决。真是一个巧妙的小手段。

[[null|]]I played a match once when I was a kid and my opponent said during a changeover, “Gilbert, you’re too competitive.” It threw me off back then because I thought I was doing something wrong. I backed off a little. It was a good ploy.

Remember this. Most of the matches you play are strictly for fun. The best thing to do when someone starts pulling objectionable stuff is to scratch them off your list of players. I mention “dirty tricks” so you get an idea of what it’s like out there during a tournament. Maybe it’ll help you when you run into a version of it in your own matches, especially in tournaments where you can’t choose who your opponents are. Then you may want to fight fire with fire.

>记住这一点。你打的大多数比赛其实都是为了好玩。当有人开始使一些令人反感的手段时，最好的办法就是把他们从你的对手名单上划掉。我提到“肮脏的手段”，只是让你对比赛现场的氛围有个概念。也许当你在自己的比赛中遇到类似情况时，这会对你有所帮助，特别是在那些你无法选择对手的比赛中。那时你也许就想以牙还牙。



# [[null|]][[null|]]16

>[[null|]][[null|]]16

# A Million-Dollar Match: War With Wheaton

>一场百万美元的赛事：与怀特森的较量

“Bradley was different. He wasn’t a barn burner in high school. When he mentioned college we were shocked. We thought he’d eventually get a job, maybe $3.35 an hour, and work his way up.”

>“布拉德利不一样。他在高中时并不是那种能让人眼前一亮的球员。当他提到要上大学时，我们感到震惊。我们认为他最终可能会找份工作，也许每小时挣3.35美元，然后慢慢往上爬。”

—Mrs. Elaine Gilbert, Brad’s mother

>——布拉德的母亲艾莉恩·吉尔伯特女士

The International Tennis Federation Grand Slam Cup in Munich is one of those events that shows how big tennis has gotten in terms of money. The winner gets $2 million. But here’s the amazing part. The runner-up makes $1 million! That’s a real heart-starter. It’s about what John Newcombe made in his entire career.

>慕尼黑国际网球联合会大满贯杯赛是那种能体现网球在金钱方面发展规模的赛事之一。冠军能获得100万美元！这可真是让人热血沸腾。这奖金数额相当于约翰·纽康比整个职业生涯的收入总和。

>[!note]- 译者注：
此处“heart-starter”意为“令人激动、激发斗志的事物”，译为“热血沸腾”既保留原意，又符合中文表达习惯。

I was invited to the 1990 Grand Slam Cup when Andre Agassi withdrew because of a torn rib cartilage. I worked my way up to the semifinals, where I was scheduled to face David Wheaton, a player I’d beaten earlier in a 15–13 fifth-set marathon that had moved me into the quarterfinals at Wimbledon. This confrontation had the added pressure of being worth more money than either of us had ever played for in our lives. The winner could retire; a victory was worth a million dollars and a chance at two million.

>1990年，安德烈·阿加西因肋软骨撕裂而退出了温网大师赛，我因此受邀参加。我一路闯进了半决赛，对手是大卫·惠顿，我之前曾在一场耗时第五盘、比分15比13的漫长比赛中击败过他，那次胜利让我成功晋级温网四分之一决赛。这场比赛的附加压力在于，它带来的奖金是我们职业生涯中从未获得过的。胜者可以提前退役，一场胜利意味着一百万美元，还有机会赢得两百万美元。

In many ways it was a culminating point in my career, as I was playing against some of the greatest players in the game for the biggest purse ever offered in a tournament. Kim and my two-year-old son Zach came along with me for moral support.

>在很多方面，这场比赛是我职业生涯的一个重要节点，因为我在对阵这项运动中一些最伟大的选手，争夺着一项赛事有史以来最高金额的奖金。金和我两岁的儿子扎克也一起来为我加油打气。

## 1 [[null|]]Pre-Match Mental Preparation

>1 比赛前心理准备

In mentally preparing for the match at the Munich Hilton I ran through a checklist on Wheaton’s game: Huge first and second serve. Maybe the best second serve in the business. At Wimbledon he had aced me twenty-three times. Loves pace. Doesn’t handle junk well. His speed is not spectacular and his footwork is occasionally off. Questionable forehand. Comes up late on his swing a lot. Makes a lot of mistakes on his forehand return of serve. (I’ve played him where he’s missed twenty forehand service returns.) When he gets tight he tends to overhit his forehand returns—when he starts doing that, the rest of his game suffers a little.

>在慕尼黑希尔顿酒店心理准备比赛时，我回顾了韦顿的比赛特点：大角度的第一和第二发球，也许全场比赛中第二发球最好的选手。在温布尔登，他曾二十次用ACE球击败我。他喜欢快节奏，对质量不高的球处理得不好。他的移动速度并不特别快，步伐有时也不太稳定。他的正手击球有些问题，挥拍动作常常滞后，接发球正手处理时经常出错。（我曾和他交手时，他错失了二十次正手接发球。）当他紧张时，往往会过度发力打出正手接发球，一旦他开始这样，他的整体表现就会有所下降。

I also knew Wheaton could take the choke. At Wimbledon he had a two-set lead and couldn’t convert two match points when he had a chance.

>我还知道怀顿（Wheaton）能承受压力。在温布尔登赛场上，他曾一度领先两盘，却在关键时刻错失两次赛点。

## 2 The All-American Player?

>2 全美最佳选手？

That was the picture I had of his tennis, at least of his strokes and playing tendencies. But it was the other side of his game that I really thought about, the mental or emotional part. Wheaton’s a very “in your face” kind of player, very emotional and confrontational. Plus, at that time in his career, he was wrapping himself in the flag (literally) by wearing a red, white, and blue headband or a shirt with stars and stripes on it.

>这是我对他网球打法的印象，至少是对他的击球方式和比赛风格的印象。但真正让我深思的是他比赛的另一面，也就是心理或情绪层面。怀特森是个非常“咄咄逼人”的球员，情绪丰富，喜欢正面交锋。而且在那个时期，他的职业生涯正处于一个阶段，他正通过佩戴一条红、白、蓝三色的头带，或穿着带有星条图案的球衣，来“把国旗披在身上”（字面意思）。

That irritated me because he was obviously attempting to create an image for himself as being the American player, like he represented American tennis. I had played for the U.S.A. in the Olympics and earned a bronze metal. Several times I had been invited to be a member of the Davis Cup team and had been thrilled at representing my country. I didn’t consider myself the American player and neither did great young guys like Chang, Sampras, Agassi, or Courier. This wearing the flag stuff by Wheaton seemed like a gimmick. It was baloney.

>这让我很不爽，因为他显然在刻意塑造自己作为美国球员的形象，仿佛他代表了美国网球。我曾代表美国参加奥运会并获得过铜牌，也多次受邀加入戴维斯杯队伍，为国效力让我感到非常激动。但我从不觉得自己是“美国球员”，像张德培、塞拉、阿加西和库尔特这样的优秀年轻球员也都不这么认为。怀特森这种挂国旗的行为看起来像是个花招，简直就是在骗人。

[[null|]]I also hadn’t forgotten the tough match we played at Wimbledon. I knew Wheaton didn’t roll over when he got behind. He fought to the finish. With that much money on the table I wanted to be ready for anything. As it turned out, the match called for everything I could muster physically, mentally, and emotionally.

John McEnroe and Jimmy Connors were willing and able to turn a tennis match into a tennis madhouse where they controlled the chaos. Lendl could strangle you in his own slow, powerful way. However, what I learned by going through those trials was going to serve me well in the most difficult and lucrative match of my life.

>约翰·麦肯罗和吉米·康纳斯愿意并且有能力将一场网球比赛变成一片网球疯人院，他们在其中掌控着混乱。伦德尔则可以用他那缓慢而强大的方式将你掐死。然而，通过经历这些考验，我学到的东西将对我人生中最艰难也最赚钱的一场比赛大有裨益。

Over 14,000 fans had packed into Munich’s Olympiahalle and they were obviously excited about the tennis ahead. I could hear their rumble from inside the locker room. They wanted to see some serious tennis. What they almost saw was some serious boxing. But that was still ahead.

>超过14,000名球迷涌入了慕尼黑奥林匹克大厅，他们显然对即将上演的网球比赛充满期待。我在更衣室里就能听见他们此起彼伏的喧闹声。他们想要看到一场真正的网球比赛。而几乎就要看到的，却是一场真正的拳击比赛。不过，那还要稍后才能发生。

An hour before the match I was trying to stay calm by playing with Zach in the player’s lounge. They have a pinball machine set up there and we were fooling around with it just to keep busy. I’d pull the lever back and he’d laugh as the ball lit up the lights and rang the buzzers. At one point I looked down and he was trying to eat my shoelaces. He obviously wasn’t nervous about anything. That helped me control my own emotions.

>比赛前一个小时，我正在球员休息室里和扎克玩，试图保持冷静。他们在那里设了一台弹珠机，我们只是随便玩玩，打发时间。我拉一下拉杆，他就会笑，球会点亮灯，发出蜂鸣声。有一刻我低头一看，发现他正试图吃我的鞋带。他显然对什么都毫不紧张，这让我也控制住了自己的情绪。

>[!note]- 译者注：
此处“吃鞋带”是俚语，意指做些无伤大雅的小动作，以缓解紧张情绪，故译为“试图吃我的鞋带”以保留原意。

Our time together ended too soon. I headed to the locker room and Kim and Zach went to their seats in the Olympiahalle. Time to earn a living.

>我们的相处时间太短了。我走进更衣室，而金和扎克则回到了奥林匹亚大厅的座位上。该赚点生活费了。

## 3 War With Wheaton

>3 与怀顿的战争

Grand Slam Cup matches are best of five sets (they want you to work for that money). When we headed out onto the court, I was as tight as I have ever been. The lights seemed brighter and the crowd noise seemed sharper. Everything seemed faster because of the tension and pressure. In the pre-match warm-up period I was talking to my feet and trying to breathe deeply, fighting off the nerves and establishing a little bit [[null|]]of rhythm. It wasn’t easy. These were circumstances I had never played under before.

>大满贯杯赛采用五盘三胜制（他们希望你为这笔钱付出努力）。当我们踏上球场时，我的紧张程度达到了前所未有的高度。灯光似乎更加明亮，观众的喧闹声也仿佛更加刺耳。一切事物都因紧张和压力而显得更快。在赛前热身阶段，我一直在跟自己的脚说话，努力深呼吸，试图驱散紧张情绪，建立一点节奏感。这并不容易。这是我以前从未经历过的情况。

## 4 “Bitte Meine Herren, Spielen Sie Tennis” (“Gentlemen, Play Tennis”)

>4 “请诸位，打网球吧”（“Gentlemen, Play Tennis”）

Wheaton and I started out in typical style, scraping and digging hard for points and games. I won the first set. He got the second.

>我们一开始就是按部就班地苦战，为每一分、每一局拼尽全力。我赢下了第一盘，他赢下了第二盘。

The third gets tough. His serve has clicked in and is huge. Twice it almost hits me it’s coming in so fast. I haven’t broken him since the first game of the second set and I don’t break him in the third. He’s holding easily and I’m struggling each time not to get broken. We head into a tiebreaker, which goes down to the wire—six points all. It’s my serve, a Set-up Point. Win it and I’m looking at set point and a possible strangle-hold on the match. I’m two points away from a 2–1 lead in the match.

>第三盘变得异常艰难。他的发球已经找到了感觉，威力十足。两次我都几乎被他直接击中，球来得太快了。我从第二盘第一局开始就没能破了他的发球局，第三盘也未能成功。他轻松保发，而我每次接发都挣扎着不被破发。我们进入抢七，比分战至六平。现在轮到我发球，是赛点局。赢下这一分，我就将看到盘点，甚至可能牢牢掌控整场比赛。我距离以2–1领先只差两分。

I decide to go at his forehand when I get a chance, remembering that it’s vulnerable to pressure (and this is a million dollars’ worth of pressure). I make a strong approach shot angled wide to his forehand. He moves to it and gives me a weak forehand crosscourt. I’m at the net and poke the volley to the ad court sideline, but I go for more than I have to.

>我抓住机会向他的正手进攻，记得正手在压力下容易出问题（而这可是百万美元的压强）。我打出一个角度宽的强攻正手球。他向那边移动，回给我一个质量不高的正手直线球。我跑到网前，用小球击球到发球线外的右侧边线，但我贪多，做过了头。

I’ve got the whole side of the court open and I put the ball out! At least, the linesman calls it out. I slam my racket into the net. “Ahhhhhgh!” But before I can even protest the call, the chair umpire, Stephen Winyard from Briton, overrules: “Correction. Ball was good. Point Mr. Gilbert.”

>我这边整个半场都敞开了，我直接把球打出了界！至少，边线裁判判定是出界。我一把 racket 打向网子，发出一声长长的“啊啊啊——”。但在我还没来得及抗议这个判罚之前，主裁史蒂芬·温亚德（来自英国）就驳回了这个判罚：“纠正一下，球是好的。这个分归吉尔伯特先生。”

Now all hell breaks loose. Wheaton is screaming bloody murder at Winyard. And he had a right to be hysterical. It’s bad to overrule calls when they’re close. And especially at crucial moments. Was my shot in? I thought so, but it was a tough call either way. Very close calls aren’t what overrules are intended to correct. It’s the obvious mistake they’re supposed to deal with. Was I glad he made the overrule? Very glad. However, I knew what Wheaton felt. I had been in his position with Connors in Chicago back in ’85.

>现在，情况彻底失控了。怀顿对着温纳德大喊大叫，简直像是要撕裂嗓子。他确实有理由情绪激动。在比分接近时驳回裁判的判罚是件很不好的事，尤其是在关键分上。我的球是不是进去了？我原本这么想，但无论怎么判都很难说。这种非常接近的判罚，其实并不是“驳回判罚”这个机制原本要纠正的。它本来是为了解决那些明显错误的判罚。我非常高兴他做出了驳回判罚的决定。然而，我知道怀顿的感受。我曾经也处在过类似的位置，那是在1985年，我在芝加哥对阵康纳斯的时候。

>[!note]- 译者注：
此处“in his position”指的是在比赛中遇到类似争议判罚的情况，而非字面意义上的“处于他的位置”。

## 5 [[null|]]Family Feud

>5 家族纷争

Wheaton can see that million-dollar check slipping away. He scrambles over to the chair and screams that the call can’t be overruled, that it was out, that the linesman had a better look at it, that the umpire is destroying the match! He is vicious mad. He’s jumping up and down like he’s on a pogo stick. I don’t blame him.

>怀特森能看出来那张价值百万美元的支票正从他手中溜走。他慌忙冲向椅子，大喊大叫，说这个判罚不能被推翻，球已经出界了，线审看得更清楚，裁判正在毁掉这场比赛！他怒火中烧，像踩着跳跳杆一样上下跳动。我完全理解他的愤怒。

But that’s not the end of it. Wheaton’s brother now gets up behind the umpire and starts barking obscenities at the top of his lungs. He’s screaming so loud you can hear him all over the arena as he runs from one side of the court to the other. Wheaton’s completely lost it and is acting like an idiot. The brother is jumping back and forth. And the crowd is whistling and stomping and hollering. Another madhouse. But I’d been there before. Connors and McEnroe are world-class when it comes to creating this kind of situation. The overrule stood.

>但这还远未结束。韦顿的弟弟现在跳到裁判身后，开始对着裁判大声咒骂，嗓门大得整个球场都能听见。他一边在球场两侧来回奔跑，一边吼得震天响。韦顿彻底失控了，像个傻瓜一样胡闹。他的弟弟来回跳动，而观众们也在大声嘘叫、跺脚、喊叫，场面又乱成一团。不过我以前也经历过这种情况。康纳斯和麦肯罗在制造这种场面方面可是世界级的高手。这个判罚最终还是维持了。

It’s Wheaton’s serve at 6–7. This is just a huge point—win it and I’m in great shape, up two sets to one. I am totally focused and ready. Wheaton makes his toss and serves. Ace! The ball is by me in a blur. Unbelievable. He’s like McEnroe. The dispute seemed to give him energy and focus, to improve his game. Now it’s 7–7 in the tiebreak. Another Set-up Point, this time for both of us. Big, big, big. Wheaton gets ready to serve again. He bounces the ball a couple of times and looks over at me. He makes his toss and swings. He faults. I like this.

>现在比分是6-7，轮到Wheaton发球。这分至关重要——赢了我就占据绝对优势，以两盘领先。我全神贯注，准备充分。Wheaton开始抛球，发球——Ace！球以闪电般的速度飞过我的身前。难以置信。他就像麦肯罗一样，争执似乎给了他能量和专注力，提升了他的表现。现在抢七比分是7-7，又是一个赛点，这次双方都面临同样的机会。非常、非常、非常关键。Wheaton再次准备发球，他反复地击球，然后看向我。他抛球、挥拍，结果发球出界。我喜欢他这种状态。

>[!note]- 这里“like this”可理解为“我喜欢他这种状态”，也可译为“我喜欢他这种表现”，根据上下文选择更自然的表达。

Again he looks over at me and bounces the ball two or three times. Quickly he serves again. It’s another fault. Double fault? Choke? Maybe he’s not like McEnroe. I lead 8–7 and get ready to serve. I roll one in at about 80 miles an hour. Wheaton tries to crush it, over-swings, and nets it. “Set, Mr. Gilbert.” I win 7–6 (9–7) and lead two sets to one. But the trouble isn’t over.

>他又一次朝我看來，並把球彈了兩三次。他迅速再次發球，又是一記出界。是雙誤嗎？是不是緊張了？也許他不像麥肯羅那樣。我以8比7領先，準備發球。我發出一記約80英里每小時的球。韋托努力大力擊球，卻過度揮拍，把球打進網內。『發球局，吉爾伯特先生。』我以7比6（9比7）拿下這盤，總比分領先兩盤一盤。但麻煩還沒有結束。

On the changeover Wheaton’s brother starts cursing at me from directly behind my back while I’m sitting down. He’s yelling over and over, “I’m going to kick your ass, Gilbert!” This is the brother, now. He’s not even playing the match, but I guess he’s figuring some of that money could be his. I complain to Winyard that the brother is disrupting the match and should be told to shut up; that he’s totally out of [[null|]]control. The umpire nods his head in agreement and cautions him to be quiet and stay off the court.

>换边时，我正坐在场边，我哥哥就从我背后直接冲我咒骂起来。他不停地大喊：“我一定会让你好看，吉尔伯特！”现在他可真成了个哥哥。他连比赛都没在场上，但我想他大概是觉得这笔钱说不定能分他一份。我向温亚德抱怨说，他哥哥正在干扰比赛，应该让他安静下来；说他完全失去了控制。裁判点头表示同意，警告他要安静，不要上场。

## 6 Nose-to-Nose With Wheaton

>6 鼻对鼻面对小麦顿

Now, as I pass Wheaton on the way back out onto the court, he has some choice words for me, all unprintable. He’s incensed that I criticized his brother and he threatens me. I answer back with my own unprintables. It was like one of those schoolyard fights where everybody’s calling everybody names. The crowd can see we’re ragging on each other, so they start making even more noise, whistling and hooting. Wheaton bumps me as I bump into him. We are nose-to-nose and yelling in each other’s face. It is a zoo.

>现在，当我从球场上往回走，经过威托恩（Wheaton）时，他对我大加指责，言语不堪入耳。他气愤地因为我批评了他的兄弟而威胁我，我也不甘示弱，回敬了他一串不堪入耳的话。我们就像在学校里打群架一样，互相辱骂。围观的人看到我们这样互相嘲讽，也开始起哄，吹口哨、喊叫。威托恩撞了我一下，我也撞了他一下，我们面对面地瞪着眼睛，大声喊叫。简直就像个动物园。

In back I can hear the brother still honking at me. My wife is sitting near him. I’m hoping she doesn’t go after him with her purse. It sounded like a boxing match after a knockdown. There was blood in the air. Bruno Rebeuh, another umpire, races toward us. He grabs Wheaton by the arm and pushes me back at the same time. He’s chattering “Stop this! Stop this. Gentleman, please settle down!”

>我后面还能听见兄弟在对我按喇叭。我妻子坐在他旁边。我希望她别用包追着他。那声音听起来就像拳击比赛被打倒后的一幕，空气中弥漫着血腥味。另一个裁判布罗诺·雷贝乌冲了过来，他一把抓住怀顿的胳膊，同时把我推回原位。他一边拉着我，一边喊道：“别这样！别这样！先生，请冷静一下！”

Wheaton said later, “I wouldn’t have been disappointed if Gilbert had thrown the first punch.” I may have been upset, but I wasn’t about to get booted out of a million-dollar match just for that. Slowly order is restored and the crowd quiets down for the start of the fourth set, but not before Winyard gives us both warnings for unsportsmanlike conduct.

>怀特森后来说道：“如果吉尔伯特先动手，我也许会感到失望。”我确实有点恼火，但绝不会因为这点就退出一场百万美元级别的比赛。渐渐地，秩序恢复了，观众也安静下来，准备进入第四盘的较量——不过在那之前，温亚德已经对我们两人各罚了一次不正当行为的警告。

I felt good because I had learned my lesson well. I didn’t let the confrontation or the obscenities from Wheaton or his brother rattle me going into the set. In fact, I was so calm I got careless, so relieved to get past that crisis and be ahead in the match that I came out of my game plan.

>我感觉很好，因为我已经很好地吸取了教训。在进入这一盘比赛之前，我不让韦顿和他的兄弟的对峙和粗言秽语影响到我。事实上，我冷静得有些大意，过于放松地渡过了那个危机，领先了比赛，以至于我偏离了原本的战术安排。

I’m up two sets to one, and instead of staying with my usual tactic of squeezing a lead (steady tennis, not risky tennis), I start getting a little ambitious—rushing some shots, going for winners too soon—just a little too aggressive. I felt like I had the match won. But it’s dangerous to get comfortable with a lead, to start feeling as if the match is in hand. Remember, when I go ahead I want to become even more alert. I want [[null|]]to be even more concerned about the opponent’s comeback power. I failed to do it here. I forgot my basic philosophy and became a happy camper. And just like that I lose the set 6–2. I haven’t broken Wheaton’s serve in nearly three sets.

>我以两盘领先，本该继续采用我一贯的策略——稳扎稳打，不冒进。但这次我却有点心浮气躁，开始打一些冒险球，过早地追求制胜分，显得有点过于激进。我感觉这场比赛已经赢定了。但拥有领先优势时，切不可掉以轻心，不能以为比赛已经掌控在手。记住，当我领先时，我应该更加警觉，更加关注对手可能的反扑力量。可这次我却没有做到，我忘了自己最基本的信念，变得自满起来。结果，就像这样，我以6–2输掉了这一盘。我几乎整整三盘都没能破掉Wheaton的发球局。

We go to the fifth. One set for $1 million. If I stop to think about the money riding on it I doubt if I can breathe. But I’m so mad at myself for letting down in the fourth set my focus sharpens up a lot. Now our mind-sets are reversed. It was a classic case of the Wounded Bear (me) and the Happy Camper (Wheaton). I wanted to make something happen right away. He may have been relieved to have evened things up.

>我们来到第五盘。一盘决胜负，奖金高达一百万美元。如果我停下来想一想这场比赛所牵涉的金钱，我怀疑自己是否还能呼吸。但我对自己在第四盘的表现感到非常愤怒，这种自责让我注意力一下子集中起来。现在我们的心理状态完全颠倒了。这是一场典型的“受伤的熊”（我）与“开心的旅行者”（怀顿）之间的较量。我想立刻扭转局势。而他可能只是松了一口气，终于把比分扳平了。

Before the start of the fifth set I take a long time to get ready. I want to stop his momentum. I don’t want him to kick things off strong in this final set. There’s no changeover so I walk back and pick up a towel. After I towel off I head over to a ball boy and hand it back. I go back over my game plan. What am I trying to do? What am I trying to keep him from doing?

>第五盘开始前，我花了很多时间来准备。我想阻止他的势头。我不希望他在决胜盘一开始就强势开局。因为没有换边，我走回去拿起一条毛巾。擦完身之后，我走到球童那里把毛巾还给他。然后我重新回顾自己的战术安排。我到底想做到什么？我想阻止他做些什么？

I tell myself that I’ve let him hold serve too easily by missing too many returns. I want to get my service return in play, take a little off of it and make him hit some shots. I remind myself again to work on his forehand, try to break it down and hope he starts missing it. I decide to hit almost everything to that side. If he’s solid there, I lose. But if he gets shaky, if he starts to overhit it, I’ll win.

>我告诉自己，我刚才让他轻松地保住了发球局，因为我接发球失误太多。我想让他的发球回到场上，稍微降低一点他的击球质量，迫使他打出一些球。我又提醒自己，要继续针对他的正手进行攻击，试图打乱他的节奏，希望他开始出现失误。我决定几乎把所有球都打到他那个方向。如果他在那边防守得很稳，我就输了；但如果他那边开始不稳，开始出现大力击球的失误，我就能赢。

Wheaton serves to start the fifth. He’s strong, but not as forceful as in the previous set. I stay in the game just scratching and clawing, nubbing a shot here, massaging one there. I’m staying in points just a little longer by trying to do less with my service return. Wheaton is having to hit an additional shot or two on each point. He’s missing a volley occasionally.

>第五局开球由韦顿发球。他力量不错，但不如上一局那么强势。我努力维持局面，不断拼抢，这边勉强接住一球，那边又勉强救回一拍。我通过减少接发球时的主动进攻，让自己在每个回合中多坚持片刻。韦顿不得不在每个回合多打一到两拍，他偶尔也会错过一些截击球。

That first game of the fifth set goes deuce and several times. Finally I bust him for the break. I lead in the fifth 1–0. My serve. My strategy has been successful. He’s having to work harder for points. I’m not giving him any donations. We make the changeover.

>第五盘第一局进入平分，反复几次之后，我终于拿下这一局。我以5-0领先，发球局。我的策略奏效了。他必须更努力地争取每一分，我完全没有给他任何轻松的机会。我们完成了换边。

Again, I focus hard on my game plan. I’ve finally gotten a break. Now I want to squeeze him—no freebees, no donations—make him earn it the hard way. I stand up and head out to serve.

>再次，我全神贯注地执行我的战术计划。我终于等到了一个机会。现在我想要逼迫他——不给任何轻松得分的机会，不给他任何“白送”的分数，让他用艰苦的方式去赢得每一分。我站起身，走向发球位置。

[[null|]]I immediately start hitting anything I can to his forehand. I keep massaging the ball with nothing on it, and he has trouble with it. I hold and so does Wheaton. I hold again and so does Wheaton. I lead 3–2. As we get deeper and deeper into the final set Wheaton knows that he has to make something happen. Otherwise I’ll just grind it out to the finish.

And as he tries to make something happen he starts overhitting a few shots and making mistakes on his forehand side. And, true to form, he starts to make some errors on other shots as well. I don’t break him again, but, more important, I don’t get broken either. I keep holding serve until it’s 5–4 ad in for the match and at least a million dollars in prize money.

>他试图有所作为，却开始频频出现失误，正手一侧的击球频频出错。更糟的是，他果然又在其他击球上也犯了错误。我没有再次破掉他的发球局，但更重要的是，我也没有被他破掉。我成功地保住了发球局，比分来到5-4局点，比赛也进入了决胜局，至少还有百万美元的奖金在等着我。

I want to tell you that I was so focused on that match point that I felt no fear or nervousness. I would tell you that, except it would be a lie. I was nervous as hell. I looked across the net at Wheaton and saw a guy ready to fight for his life. He was bouncing up and down and shifting from foot to foot. It didn’t look like he was going to retire because of fatigue.

>我想告诉你们，我当时全神贯注于那个赛点，感觉不到一丝恐惧或紧张。我确实会这么说，不过这其实是个谎言。我当时紧张得要命。我望向对面网前的怀特森，看到的是一副准备殊死一搏的架势。他不停地上下跳动，双脚不停地换来换去，看起来他绝不是因为疲劳而要退赛。

I remembered my basic strategy. Just get the serve in and get it to his forehand. I bounced the ball twice. Took another look across the net and one deep breath. Then a beautiful toss and a great motion. Whap! My serve goes two feet into the net. Wheaton now is really getting excited. He’s hopping up and down. Moving back and forth. He sensed I was in trouble.

>我记得了自己的基本策略：只要把发球发到对方的正手位就可以了。我跳了两次球，又朝网对面看了一眼，深吸了一口气。接着是一个漂亮的抛球和一个优美的挥拍动作。啪！我的发球擦网而过，落在网前两英尺的位置。怀特现在真的激动起来了，他上下跳动，来回走动，明显感觉到我陷入了困境。

Second serve. Win the point and I’m guaranteed a check for $1 million! Again I bounce the ball twice. Look over the net and toss the ball in the air. Not a great toss. Not a great serve. But it does crawl over the net and gravity does its job. And it goes to his forehand. Wheaton looks ready for it, but he misfires. Swings late and tries to overhit the ball. He plows it wide. I was never happier to see a ball sail out in my life. He had taken the collar. I had beaten him (and his brother) 6–4 in the fifth.

>第二发球。赢下这一分，我就能拿到一百万美元的奖金！我又一次将球弹跳两次，望向网线，将球抛向空中。抛得并不好，发球也不够出色。但球还是艰难地越过了网线，重力也完成了它的任务。球落在了他的正手上。怀顿看起来准备接球，但他却失误了。他挥拍较晚，试图大力击球，结果把球打出了界。我这辈子从未如此高兴地看到球飞出界外。他失去了主动权。我在第五盘以6比4击败了他（以及他的兄弟）。

The match had started at 8:30 Saturday night, December 15. It was now 12:47 Sunday morning—over four hours of the most intense tennis I had ever played. I had just won a million bucks (minus a $5,000 fine both Wheaton and I had to pay for the altercation).

>比赛于12月15日周六晚上8:30开始，现在已是周日清晨12:47——整整四小时，是我职业生涯中最激烈的一场比赛。我刚刚赢下了百万美元（不过我和怀顿都因为冲突被罚款了5000美元）。

[[null|]]We shook hands. Barely. He was too tired to be mad. I just wanted to get out of there. I did some interviews, iced down, got a massage to loosen up my legs and took a shower. I finally got back to the hotel at 4:30 a.m. In less than ten hours I was scheduled to start the finals against my friend Pete Sampras. As it turned out, I had nothing left.

It was okay. I had played over four hours of unbelievably hard tennis against a very tough opponent. I got five hours of sleep and started getting ready for the finals. Even though I had beaten Pete in our two previous matches he cruised me in straight sets. I gave it everything I had. There just wasn’t much left after the struggle with Wheaton. And Pete played super tennis.

>这没什么大不了的。我刚刚和一个非常强硬的对手打了四个多小时的网球，打得极其激烈。我睡了五个小时，然后开始准备决赛。尽管我之前两次和皮特交手都赢了，但他这次却直落两盘把我打垮了。我尽了全力，但和韦顿的激战之后，我的体能已经所剩无几了。而皮特则打出了超级水准的网球。

I looked over at Kim during the award ceremonies. I could see tears in her eyes. The last twelve hours had been like nothing we’d ever been through before. The intensity had been almost overwhelming. I smiled at her and she waved back. Zach was sitting on her lap chewing on a tennis ball. Then I congratulated Pete and was handed a check for $1 million.

>我在颁奖典礼上望向金。我能看见她眼中有泪光。过去十二个小时对我们来说就像从未经历过的经历，紧张程度几乎让人难以承受。我朝她微笑，她也朝我挥手。扎克坐在她腿上，正咬着一个网球。然后我祝贺彼特，随即收到了一张一百万美元的支票。

## 7 The Long and Winding Road

>7 长长的蜿蜒之路

On the flight back to the United States following the tournament in Munich I thought of another flight and another tournament eight years earlier. That time I was on a plane heading back home after winning my first tournament as a pro. I’d been a professional for six months and all of a sudden I’d won this little event in Taipei. I beat a guy named Craig Wittus. It surprised everyone, including me. And to make it even sweeter they paid me in cash; no check, no money order, no wire transfer. Fifteen thousand dollars in cash!

>从慕尼黑比赛结束后飞回美国的航班上，我回想起八年前另一次飞行和另一次比赛。那时我刚成为一名职业选手六个月，突然就在台北赢得了自己的第一个职业赛事。我击败了一位名叫克雷格·维图斯的选手。这件事让所有人，包括我自己都感到震惊。更让人欣喜的是，他们用现金支付奖金，而不是支票、汇票或电汇。整整一万五千美元现金！

I remembered flying back home from Taipei after that first win. My tennis rackets were in the overhead and $15,000 in cash was packed in the equipment bag on my lap. This may sound strange, but I actually got a bigger thrill out of that than the million bucks I earned in Munich. Nobody, but nobody, thought I had a chance out on the tour. I didn’t have the strokes. I didn’t have the natural ability. “Who you [[null|]]kidding, Brad? Get a real job.” I knew my days were numbered if I didn’t get results fairly soon.

>我记得第一次胜利后飞回台北时的情景。我的球拍放在头顶的行李舱里，而装着15000美元现金的装备袋则放在我的腿上。这听起来可能有点奇怪，但我从那次胜利中得到的兴奋感，甚至超过了我在慕尼黑赚到的那百万美元。当时，没人，没人，认为我能在巡回赛中有所作为。我没有好的击球技术，也没有天生的天赋。“布拉德，你开什么玩笑？找个正经工作吧。”我知道，如果我不在短时间内取得成绩，我的职业生涯恐怕就要结束了。

So the flight back from Taipei was special. I’ll never forget the feeling I had heading back to California holding that bag of money, sitting there in the darkness with a cold can of beer and looking down at the Pacific. I realized for the first time that maybe they were wrong. Maybe I could make it on the tour. Maybe this club player, this guy with the “ugly” strokes, had a life in front of him as a professional tennis player. Just before I fell asleep I remember thinking, “I hope this doesn’t turn out to be a dream.” It wasn’t.

>从台北飞回的那趟航班特别不一样。我永远都不会忘记，拎着那袋钱回到加州时的心情，坐在黑暗中，手里握着一罐冰啤酒，望着太平洋的那一刻。我第一次意识到，也许他们说得不对。也许我真的能在巡回赛中站稳脚跟。也许这个俱乐部选手，这个“丑陋”的球技家伙，真的能成为一名职业网球选手。就在我快要入睡时，我记得自己还在想：“我希望这不会只是一场梦。” 结果，它不是。



# [[null|]][[null|]]17

>[[null|]][[null|]]17

# Tournament Tough All the Time

>赛事中的残酷始终如一

## 1 How to Make It Part of Your Game

>1 把它融入你的比赛

When I was a kid just getting into tennis I loved to play matches. If I couldn’t play a match, Dad was always available to hit with me and help me work on my game. If he couldn’t, then my sister Dana (who later played on the women’s tour) or my brother Barry (who also played professionally) would hit with me. I loved it so much that if I couldn’t find a partner I’d go hit against a wall and pretend I was playing a match. I spent thousands of hours in Piedmont, California, with a racket, a tennis ball, and a wall.

>我小时候刚开始打网球的时候，特别喜欢比赛。如果找不到对手，我爸爸总会抽空陪我打球，帮助我提高技术。如果他没空，我的姐姐达娜（后来在女子巡回赛中打球）或者哥哥巴里（他也曾职业打球）就会来陪我。我热爱这项运动到了这种程度，如果找不到搭档，我就会对着墙击球，假装自己在和对手比赛。我在加州皮德蒙特度过了成千上万个小时，拿着球拍、网球和一面墙，一遍遍地练习。

Gradually I developed my tennis skills and began to enter an occasional tournament around San Francisco. Every once in a while a local event in the Bay Area would loom on the horizon and I’d start practicing harder and paying more attention—making sure my equipment was all set and that I got a good night’s sleep. My mother made sure I was eating right. For a tournament I paid a lot more attention to what I was doing before and during the match or matches. I’m sure you do the same thing.

>我逐渐提高了自己的网球水平，开始偶尔参加旧金山附近的比赛。每隔一段时间，湾区的某个本地赛事就会出现在我的视野中，我便会更加努力地训练，更加专注地准备——确保装备齐全，保证有充足的睡眠。我的母亲也确保我吃得健康。对于比赛，我会在赛前和比赛中投入更多注意力。我想你肯定也是一样。

In those early days I’d play thirty or forty “fun” matches (and hit against the wall for hours and hours) for every tournament that I entered. Over the years that ratio gradually changed. One day I woke up and realized (as a pro) that I didn’t ever play a match just for “fun” anymore. Virtually every single match I played was a tournament match. I no longer had one set of rules that I used for “fun” or social matches and another set of rules for preparing for a tournament. I had to be [[null|]]tournament tough all the time. I had to pay attention all the time because a great deal of money was at stake.

>在刚开始的时候，我每参加一场比赛，就会打三十到四十场“娱乐”性质的比赛（甚至连续好几个小时对着墙击球）。但随着时间推移，这个比例逐渐发生了变化。有一天我醒来时，突然意识到（作为一名职业选手）我再也没打过纯粹为了“娱乐”而进行的比赛了。几乎我所打的每一场比赛，都等同于一场正式的赛事。我不再有针对“娱乐”或社交比赛的一套规则，也不再有为准备赛事而制定的另一套规则。我必须时刻保持赛事所需的强度和专注。因为这场比赛往往牵涉到大量的金钱利益。

My point is this. Many of the ideas and tips in this book require some effort to absorb and apply. But if you genuinely want to improve your game, proper integration of these ideas will give you dramatic results: you will win more often. And, you’ll have a lot of fun bringing the mental part of tennis into your game.

>我的意思是，这本书中很多想法和建议都需要你付出一些努力去理解和应用。但如果你真心想提高自己的球技，将这些理念正确地融入你的训练和比赛中，将会带来显著的效果：你赢球的次数会大大增加。同时，你也会在将网球的“心理层面”融入自己的比赛过程中，享受到很多乐趣。

## 2 Desire, Dedication, Diligence

>2 渴望、奉献、勤奋

Approach it as you would approach a tournament. Apply yourself to the task and make a conscientious effort to be a smarter and more aware player. Work at it. Most of what I discuss in Winning Ugly involves mental alertness: recognizing opportunity, analyzing options, capitalizing on the opportunity with the best option. Recognize. Analyze. Capitalize.

>把它当作参加比赛一样来对待。全身心投入这项任务，认真努力地让自己成为一个更聪明、更敏锐的球员。要不断练习。我在《赢得很丑》中讨论的大部分内容都与心理警觉性有关：识别机会，分析选项，选择最佳方案来把握机会。识别。分析。把握。

You might compare my approach to Tim Gallwey’s in his book The Inner Game of Tennis. He focuses on the right brain, or intuitive process. I focus on the left brain process. The left side of your brain is the administrator, checking data and establishing priorities. In my approach to the game, that’s crucial to winning.

>你可能会将我的方法与蒂姆·加尔韦在其著作《网球内心的博弈》中所提出的方法进行比较。他关注的是右脑，也就是直觉的过程。而我则关注左脑的过程。你大脑的左半部分是管理者，它负责检查数据并确定优先事项。在我的网球理念中，这一点对于赢得比赛至关重要。

## 3 Mental Management

>3 心理管理

In addition to the physical game of tennis there is a mental game going on that can have a profound impact on the outcome of the match. But you have to be able to recognize and take advantage of opportunities, to know what’s going on before and during a match. You’ll win if you play good left-brain tennis.

>除了网球这项身体对抗的运动之外，还存在着一个心理层面的较量，它对比赛的结果可能产生深远的影响。但你必须能够识别并利用机会，在比赛前和比赛中清楚地了解局势。如果你打的是左脑型网球，你就会赢。

You must understand that playing tennis is not just either a right brain or left brain activity. It uses both sides in an incredible combination of spontaneous and creative mental and physical responses, plus a cold analytical approach that involves observation and calculation.

>你必须明白，打网球并不是单纯的右脑或左脑活动。它需要大脑两半球以一种不可思议的方式结合运作：一方面是一种自发而富有创造力的身心反应，另一方面则是一种冷静的分析方式，包括观察和计算。

Acquiring these new mental habits and incorporating them into [[null|]]your game requires as much effort as developing new physical habits in tennis. You have to want to do it or it won’t happen. You have to apply the same approach you would use before a tournament—diligence, desire, dedication.

>培养这些新的心理习惯，并将其融入你的比赛中，所需要的付出不亚于在网球中培养新的身体习惯。你必须真正想要去做，否则这一切都不会发生。你必须像准备参加赛事一样，以同样的态度去投入——勤奋、渴望、专注。

## 4 The Early Edge

>4 早期优势

As you get ready for a match, make sure you are mentally and physically prepared. It’s an opportunity waiting to be capitalized on, so give yourself the early advantage. Usually that opportunity is ignored, wasted. But for the smart player it is there, waiting to be used.

>比赛前，确保你既在心理上又在身体上做好准备。这是一次等待被把握的机会，因此要给自己一个先发优势。通常，这种机会被忽视、浪费了。但对聪明的球员来说，它就在那里，等待被利用。

>[!note]- 译者注：
这里的“capitalized on”翻译为“把握”更符合中文表达习惯，同时保留了原文中强调机会的重要性。

1\. Pre-Match Mental Preparation: Think about your opponent and develop your game plan before you get to the court.

>1\. 比赛前心理准备：在踏上球场之前，先想想你的对手，并制定好你的战术计划。

2\. Tools of the Trade: Your equipment can save matches for you. It can also cost you matches. I can usually tell by looking at what a club player brings in their equipment bag how serious they are about winning.

>2\. 工具与装备：你的装备可以帮你赢得比赛，也可能让你输掉比赛。通常，我只需看看一名业余选手的装备袋里装了些什么，就能判断他们赢得比赛的认真程度。

3\. Stretch for Success: Warm up the muscles and then stretch them for better performance and less risk of injury.

>3. 拉伸带来成功：先热身，再拉伸肌肉，以提高表现并降低受伤风险。

4\. Get the Early Lead: Begin your match intelligently with the goal of grabbing a quick break and establishing early dominance.

>4\. 抢先取得领先：以迅速取得破发并建立早期优势为目标，聪明地开始你的比赛。

## 5 Playing Smart

>5 智取对手

Always believe that most of the time there is a way for you to win. You just have to find it.

>始终相信，大多数时候总有一条路可以让你获胜。你只需要找到它。

1\. The Key to Victory: During a match accumulate information about how you’re winning and losing points. Ask “Who’s doing what to whom?”

>1\. 胜利的关键：在比赛中积累关于你如何赢分、如何失分的信息。要问自己：“是谁在对谁做什么？”

2\. Destroy Your Opponent’s Game Plan: Know what to do when your opponent is winning points by attacking you in various ways.

>2\. 破坏对手的战术：了解当对手通过各种方式攻击你从而赢得分数时，你该怎么做。

[[null|]]3\. The “Hidden” Ad Points: Recognize the overlooked dynamic moments in the match and know what to do with them. But mainly, be aware of them when they occur.

4\. The Player’s Pit Stop: When the wheels come off a particular stroke, recognize it and make an effort to correct it before it’s too late.

>4\. 球员的暂停时刻：当某一击球技术出现失误时，要立刻察觉并努力纠正，以免为时已晚。

5\. Learn From the Legends: Watch the top players and apply tactics that work for them to your own game.

>5\. 向传奇学习：观看顶尖选手的比赛，将他们的成功战术应用到自己的比赛中。

## 6 Mind Games, Psyching, and Gamesmanship

>6 心理博弈、心理战与竞技策略

Be aware of the personal dynamics of the match and recognize what the other player is doing to affect your game—gamesmanship, tempo, or anything else that can crack your concentration and take your mind off the goal. Protect yourself.

>注意比赛中的个人互动，并识别对方球员正在采取哪些行动影响你的表现——比如心理战术、节奏变化，或者其他可能干扰你专注力、让你分心的手段。保护好自己。

>[!note]- 译者注：
此处“gamesmanship”译为“心理战术”更符合中文语境，意指通过心理策略干扰对手。

The two most common errors that recreational tennis players make are these:

>业余网球选手最常见的两个错误是：

1\. They don’t think about what they’re doing.

>1\. 他们根本没想自己在做什么。

2\. They do it too fast.

>2\. 他们打得太快了。

>[!note]- 译者注：
此处“too fast”并非单纯指击球速度，而是指球员在比赛中过于急躁，缺乏耐心，导致技术动作变形，影响发挥。

Aerobic tennis is the result, players who rush around swatting the ball, oblivious to almost everything except keeping track of the score. There’s nothing wrong with that—after all, it’s good for your health—but if winning is important to you, then incorporating what’s in this book will be worthwhile.

>“有氧网球”是一种结果，那些球员在场上慌忙跑动，挥拍击球，几乎对一切视而不见，只顾着盯住比分。这并没有什么不对——毕竟，这对健康是有好处的。但如果你非常重视胜利，那么将本书中的内容融入你的训练中，将会是值得的。

## 7 Gilbert’s Golden Rules

>7 吉尔伯特的黄金法则

## 8 1\. Have a Plan

>8 1\. 有计划

A player who has a plan is a thinking player. Even a bad plan is better than no plan at all. A bad plan will be recognized and changed by a [[null|]]thinking player. The result will be a better plan. Better plans win matches.

>一个有计划的球员才是真正会思考的球员。即使是一个糟糕的计划，也比完全没有计划要好。一个会思考的球员会识别出糟糕的计划并加以修正，最终形成更好的计划。更好的计划才能赢得比赛。

This applies to every aspect of your tennis. Have a plan for your mental preparation, stretching, warm-up, and the start of your match. Have a game plan during the match and a routine for assessing your effectiveness during the competition. Know what you’re doing out on the court.

>这适用于你网球的方方面面。为你的心理准备、热身、拉伸和比赛开始制定计划。比赛中要有战术安排，比赛中也要有一套评估自己表现的例行程序。知道你在场上在做什么。

## 9 2\. Don’t Rush

>9 2\. 不要急于求成

Know what is the best pace or match tempo for you and stick with it. Don’t let yourself get rushed into or through points. Be thoughtful, deliberate, observant, and analytical—incorporate left brain thinking into your tennis. You can’t control your pace when you’re playing aerobic tennis.

>了解最适合你的比赛节奏，并坚持这种节奏。不要让自己在比赛中被催促或仓促地完成每一个得分点。要细心、有条理、观察入微、分析到位——把左脑的思维方式融入你的网球中。当你进行有氧型网球比赛时，你无法控制自己的节奏。

Follow these rules and the other ideas I’ve suggested and not only will you win more often, but you’ll have a great time figuring out how to do it. Your opponents will start saying, “Hey, your strokes don’t look any different. How come you’re beating me more often?” Smile and say, “Just my day, I guess.”

>遵循这些规则以及我提出的其他建议，你不仅会赢得更多比赛，还能在摸索如何做到的过程中享受乐趣。你的对手会开始问你：“嘿，你的击球动作看起来没什么不同，为什么你赢我的次数越来越多？”你可以微笑着说：“也许是今天我状态好一点吧。”



# [[null|]][[null|]]18

>[[null|]][[null|]]18

# The Road to Number One

>通往世界第一之路

Coauthor’s Note: Much has happened since I first presented Brad Gilbert with an idea for a book called Winning Ugly: Mental Warfare in Tennis. Among other things it has become one of the best-selling tennis books of the 1990s, receiving critical acclaim from the Los Angeles Times, Tennis Week, the Indianapolis News, Inside Tennis, the Atlanta Journal, Los Angeles Daily News, Tennis, the San Francisco Examiner, and other publications.

>合著者序：在我第一次向布拉德·吉尔伯特提出撰写一本名为《以丑取胜：网球中的心理战》的书的想法后，很多事情都发生了。这本书最终成为1990年代最畅销的网球书籍之一，获得了《洛杉矶时报》、《网球周报》、《印第安纳波利斯新闻》、《网球内幕》、《亚特兰大宪章报》、《洛杉矶每日新闻》、《网球》、《旧金山观察家报》以及其他多家媒体的好评。

Mary Carillo, John McEnroe, Barry MacKay, Fred Stolle, Cliff Drysdale, and other network tennis commentators began mentioning our new book in their television broadcasts: “Boy, that’s a page right out of Winning Ugly!”

>玛丽·卡里洛、约翰·麦肯罗、巴里·麦克凯、弗雷德·斯托勒、克里夫·德雷塞尔以及其他电视网球评论员开始在他们的电视节目中提到我们的新书：“天哪，这简直是从《赢得难看》里直接抄来的一页！”

Coaches at high schools and colleges around the nation made Winning Ugly required reading for many of their players. In fact, head coaches at Texas A and M, the University of Washington, Stanford, the University of California-Berkeley, the University of Florida, and elsewhere gave their entire teams copies of Winning Ugly to study. The N.C.A.A. Division I Coach of the Decade, Dick Gould, called Winning Ugly “superb!”

>全国各高中和大学的教练们都将《赢得难看》列为许多球员的必读书籍。事实上，德克萨斯A&M大学、华盛顿大学、斯坦福大学、加州大学伯克利分校、佛罗里达大学等校的主教练甚至将整本《赢得难看》分发给球队成员，供他们研读。N.C.A.A.一级联赛“十年最佳教练”迪克·高德（Dick Gould）称《赢得难看》是“非常出色的作品”！

Michael Chang was even spotted buying a copy of Winning Ugly. It’s being serialized in Japan by that country’s leading tennis publication, Tennis Classic, and will be published soon in Europe. Hey, even Tony Bennett likes it.

>迈克尔·张甚至被拍到购买了一本《以丑取胜》。这本书目前正在日本由该国领先的网球杂志《网球经典》连载，并将在不久后在欧洲出版。嘿，就连托尼·贝内特也喜欢这本书。

But, along the way something else happened that brought Winning Ugly more attention than all of the above; namely, Andre Agassi asked Brad Gilbert to become his coach. Twelve and a half months later Agassi had [[null|]]risen from number thirty-two in the world to number one and had won two Grand Slam titles including the U.S. Open.

>但在这条路上，发生了一件比上述所有事情都更引人注目的事：安德烈·阿加西请布拉德·吉尔伯特担任他的教练。12个半月后，阿加西从世界排名第32位跃升至第1位，并赢得了包括美国公开赛在内的两项大满贯冠军。

Suddenly such “un-tennis” publications as The New Yorker, Esquire, The New York Times, Men’s Journal, and Newsweek were mentioning Winning Ugly in feature stories about Agassi’s meteoric rise to the top and the role Brad Gilbert had played in it. Recently, Brad was voted Coach of the Year by the United States Tennis Registry.

>突然间，《纽约客》、《Esquire》、《纽约时报》、《男士杂志》和《新闻周刊》等原本不以网球为题材的媒体，也开始在关于阿加西迅速崛起至巅峰以及布拉德·吉尔伯特在他成功过程中所扮演角色的专题报道中提及《丑胜》。最近，布拉德还被美国网球协会评为年度最佳教练。

In separate interviews I asked each of them to evaluate the other and reflect and comment on what is one of the most extraordinary tennis relationships in the history of the game.

>我在分别的采访中，请他们各自评估对方，并反思和评论一下，这可能是网球历史上最非凡的球员关系之一。

What follows is the number one Coach and the number one Player talking about losing, winning, and how they got to the top.

>接下来是排名第一的教练和排名第一的球员，他们谈论输赢，以及他们如何登上巅峰。

“C’mon, Andre. Stop middle! Stop middle!” I’m watching Michael Chang bounce the ball during this important semifinal match in the ’96 Sybase Open and know what’s about to happen. Steve Jamison, my coauthor, leans over and gives me a look. “Stop middle what?” he asks.

>“安德烈，快停下！别再打中间球了！”我正在观看迈克尔·张在1996年Sybase公开赛半决赛中发球，我知道接下来会发生什么。我的合著者史蒂夫·贾米森弯下腰，朝我瞥了一眼，问道：“停下中间球是什么意思？”

Boom! Chang unloads an ace up the middle to take a 4–2 lead in the third set of a match he should have lost in two. Jamison nods his head as the crowd roars. Andre is steamed. “Middle! Middle!” I’m thinking, “We’ve got a big problem. You don’t let Chang back into a match like this or you lose.”

>“砰！”昌打出一记正中间的发球 ace，以 4–2 的比分领先，这局他本该在两盘之内就输掉比赛。贾米森点头认可，观众席上爆发出欢呼声。安德烈则气得跳脚。“中间！中间！”我心里想着，“我们这次真的遇到大麻烦了。在这种情况下，绝不能让昌重新回到比赛中，否则你就输了。”

## 1 Coaching vs. Playing

>1 教练与打球

One thing I’ve learned since Andre and I got together as player and coach is that it’s a lot tougher playing than coaching. Sitting on the sidelines it’s fairly easy to spot a match and see what’s happening; who’s doing what to whom. It’s totally something else to be in the ring fighting for your life. It’s easy to get distracted, and Andre had gotten distracted; by the crowd, by mistakes caused maybe by impatience, by Chang. Even his do-rag started bothering him.

>自从我和安德烈成为球员和教练的搭档以来，我学到的一件事是，打球比当教练要难得多。坐在场边，观察比赛、看清楚局势、看谁在针对谁做些什么，这相对而言要轻松得多。但真正置身于赛场、为自己的生命而战，那就完全是另一回事了。很容易分心，而安德烈就分心了——被观众、可能因急躁而犯下的错误，以及张（Chang）所干扰。甚至他的头巾也开始让他感到困扰。

>[!note]- “do-rag” 是一种戴在头上、通常用于遮阳或吸汗的头巾，此处翻译为“头巾”以符合中文表达习惯。

[[null|]]Sitting in the comfort of the friend’s box I could see Michael was causing some major bleeding serving Andre up the center and he was doing it on almost every important pressure point. Reason? Chang is short, maybe 5’8”, and it’s tougher for him to hit the wide serve because the net’s higher at the sides. It’s simple physics or geometry. Whatever it is, it’s easier for him to do damage serving up the middle on both deuce and ad courts. A guy like Sampras doesn’t have that problem because he’s taller and has those long arms. Pete may stand 6 feet tall but he serves 7 feet tall and loves to swing you wide. And he can do it under pressure.

Even with Chang’s new long racket he has more trouble going wide, especially under pressure. So he was picking up lots of points, big points, by pounding up the middle. At one stage he served middle nine straight times; five for clean winners or weak returns. Over and over. When he got ahead in a game he might try wide, but otherwise no. Middle.

>即使使用了张的新型长拍，他仍然在打出宽球方面遇到更多困难，尤其是在压力之下。因此，他经常通过打中间球来赢得很多分，关键的分数。有一段时间，他连续发了九个中间球；其中五个是干净利落地得分，或者对手回球较弱。反复如此。当他在一个局中取得领先时，也许会尝试打宽球，但除此之外，他几乎从不这么做——中间球。

## 2 World’s Number One at Stake

>世界排名第一的头衔悬而未决

This Saturday night match near San Francisco is important because if Andre wins he advances to the finals Sunday against Pete Sampras. The winner of that match then moves to number one in the world rankings. It would be the first time in the history of tennis two players, neither of whom are ranked number one, would fight for control of the top spot. (ED. NOTE: Thomas Muster had been number one very briefly in February of ’96, but by losing in the first round of a tournament elsewhere he was about to lose his top ranking. The winner of a Sunday night final-round Agassi/Sampras match would be elevated to a world ranking of number one.)

>这周六晚在旧金山附近举行的比赛非常重要，因为如果阿加西获胜，他将在周日对阵皮特·桑普拉斯进入决赛。那场比赛的胜者将获得世界第一的排名。这将是网球历史上第一次，两位非世界第一的选手争夺世界第一的位置。(编者注：托马斯·穆斯特曾在1996年2月短暂地获得过世界第一的排名，但随后在另一场比赛的第一轮失利，即将失去世界第一的排名。如果周日夜晚阿加西与桑普拉斯的决赛中胜出，胜者将获得世界第一的排名。)

A win here would start up ’96 real good; a great return to form for Andre, who had ripped a chest muscle during Davis Cup play and had been out of action for three months until the ’96 Australian Open. There Chang beat him in straights.

>在这里赢下这一场比赛，将为1996年开个好头；对安德烈来说，这将是他状态的强势回归，因为他曾在戴维斯杯比赛中撕裂了胸肌，整整三个月未能参赛，直到1996年澳大利亚公开赛才复出。在那场比赛中，他直落两盘败给了对手。

## 3 [[null|]]Off to a Quick Start

>3 稍作热身便迅速开局

The night started out fine for Andre. He was hitting hard, very focused and methodical, and took the first set 6–2. No sweat. The second set got even better because Chang started making some very rare unforced errors as he tried to make something happen. Chang donating points is like snow in Las Vegas. Doesn’t happen much. So, Andre is up a set and it’s 4–2 in the second. Eight points from a ticket to the finals. But then it began.

>安德烈的夜晚一开始非常顺利。他击球有力，专注而有条不紊，以6比2拿下首盘。一切都很平静。第二盘更是锦上添花，因为张开始频频出现一些极为罕见的非受迫性失误，试图制造一些局面。张送分就像雪落在拉斯维加斯，很少见。所以，安德烈以一盘领先，第二盘以4比2占据优势，距离决赛门票仅差八分。但就在这时，一切开始发生了变化。

I didn’t notice at first, but very quietly a shift in the match takes place. Chang settles down and stops donating points, which I expected. But at the same time Andre begins to hurry it up just a little; rushing to finish the point off too quickly. Missing his first serve, giving Chang a look at the second. He may have been just a little eager to close out the match, but whatever it is he’s changed his play pattern. Very subtle, but definitely a change.

>起初我没注意到，但比赛悄然发生了变化。张逐渐冷静下来，不再轻易送分，这正是我预料到的。然而与此同时，阿加西却开始稍微加快节奏，急于结束每个回合，动作显得有些仓促。他发球出界，给了张第二次发球的机会。也许他只是略微急切地想要结束比赛，但无论如何，他的打法已经发生了变化。变化非常细微，但确实存在。

Now Andre is serving at 4–2, up a set; a huge moment in the match. I call it the Super Set-Up Game because this game can really leverage Andre’s position, putting him just 4 points away from victory. If he holds serve he’s got Chang right in the crosshairs. Losing gets Chang back on serve and could completely change the dynamics of the match. Chang gets a second suck of the gas. So I’m thinking, “Hold, Andre. Get that first serve in.”

>现在阿涅伊（Andre）以4-2领先，占据发球局，这是比赛中的一个关键时刻。我称之为“超级发球局”，因为这一局能够充分利用阿涅伊的优势，让他仅差4分就能赢得整场比赛。如果他成功保发，张（Chang）就将处于被重点针对的位置。而如果阿涅伊失分，张就将重新获得发球权，这完全可能改变比赛的局势。张将再度掌握主动权。因此我在心里想着：“阿涅伊，稳住，发球要稳住。”

No such luck. Chang breaks back. Uh-oh. On serve at 3–4, second set. But, a break is never a break until you hold. Maybe breaking back will defocus Michael a little, make him a little overeager. Maybe not. Chang doesn’t defocus twice in one match. Andre keeps trying for the quick kill; hasty, trying to finish it off maybe one or two shots early. Maybe three. And it continues that way, with Chang holding to go 4–4.

>没那么巧。张破发了。哦不，第二盘抢七，张在发球局，3-4。但，破发并不等于胜利，直到你保发为止。也许破发能稍微让迈克尔分心，让他有点急于求成。也许不会。张可不会在一场球赛中两次失误。安德烈继续尝试速胜，仓促出击，也许想用一两个球就结束比赛。也许三个。就这样持续下去，张保发，比分来到4-4。

I think Andre’s also a little agitated because the crowd is cheering Chang’s comeback. They want a third set, and it doesn’t matter who you are, the crowd will turn on you if it means they get more tennis. Andre throws off his do-rag when he looks up at the huge overhead monitor and notices that it’s on crooked. That gets the crowd going again [[null|]]and over 10,000 fans start whooping it up. He doesn’t look happy. I’m thinking he’s got to get some patience.

>我认为安德烈可能也有些焦躁，因为观众在为张的反扑欢呼。他们希望进入第三盘，无论你是谁，只要能多看一会儿网球，观众就会对你翻脸。安德烈抬头看向巨大的顶棚屏幕时，发现屏幕歪了，他随手摘下头巾。这一幕再次点燃了观众的热情，超过一万名球迷开始欢呼雀跃。他看起来并不开心。我想他现在最需要的就是一点耐心。

>[!note]- 译者注：
此处“get some patience”意为“需要一点耐心”，根据中文表达习惯，译为“需要一点耐心”更为自然。

## 4 The Perfect Point

>4 完美一拍

What I mean by that is Andre ideally plays a point with Michael that goes between six and nine shots. In other words, against a Retriever like Chang who gets to everything, Andre is best off if he hits the ball no fewer than six times and no more than nine times as he constructs the point trying to build an advantage or set up an opening.
