>我的意思是，安德烈理想情况下应该和迈克尔打出一个来回在六到九拍之间的回合。换句话说，面对像张这样的“全垒打型”选手（Retriever，指能接到几乎所有来球的对手），安德烈最好是在构建优势或创造机会的过程中，至少击球六次，最多击球九次。

More than nine shots and you’re in a war of attrition and Michael looks good. Fewer than six hits and Andre is probably forced to go for the gigundo shot because the point hasn’t really opened up right yet. I’m speaking generally now.

>超过九个击球回合，比赛就演变成持久战，而迈克尔在这种情况下表现更出色。而如果击球次数少于六次，安德烈大概率会被迫选择打出那个高难度的“吉格努多”球，因为此时局面尚未真正打开。我现在是在一般意义上说的。

Obviously, there are exceptions, but over the course of a match this is what Andre’s looking for against Chang: construct an opening with between six and nine shots.

>显然，也有例外情况，但在一场比赛中，安德烈针对张的策略通常是：构建一个由六到九个击球组成的开局。

Andre is letting Chang off the hook by trying to finish off points too soon. He’s serving now at 5–6, second set; still missing that first serve and letting Chang look at too many seconds where he can take charge of the point. Chang breaks at love.

>安德烈急于结束得分，让张松轻易摆脱困境。他现在在第二盘5-6局，发球时仍然频频失误，让张松有太多时间掌控局面。张松随即以“love”（零分）破发。

He’s on cruise control. The match is even, but only in the score. Michael has all the momentum and he stays tough to go up 4–2 in the third. This one is slipping away and it’s going to hurt. No way Andre should let Chang win this match.

>他现在完全处于自动驾驶状态。比分看似势均力敌，但实际上迈克尔掌控了比赛的节奏，他稳扎稳打，第三盘以4比2领先。这场比赛正在从他手中溜走，这会让他非常痛苦。绝不可能让安德烈眼睁睁看着张赢得比赛。

Suddenly Chang ’s just 8 points from the finals. But even though Michael’s tenacious, let me tell you this about Andre Agassi: he is solid. I like his mental attitude these days. A few years ago he might have figured this wasn’t his day and gone out quietly. Now Andre keeps looking for a way to dig it out right up until the end. He may not win it, but he’ll fight you right down to the wire.

>突然之间，张只差8分就能进入决赛。但即使迈克尔表现得非常顽强，让我告诉你关于安德烈·阿加西的一些事：他非常稳定。我挺欣赏他现在的心理状态。几年前，他可能早就觉得今天不是自己的日子，然后悄悄地退出比赛。但现在，阿加西会一直寻找翻盘的机会，直到最后一刻。他可能不会赢，但他会一直跟你拼到最后一刻。

Chang has won six of the last nine games and is getting close to having a stranglehold on this thing. But Andre starts to settle down. I can see he’s gotten back into his rhythm and is focused. He looks over in our [[null|]]direction and I can see his eyes look calm, focused. Actually zoned is a better description.

>张正浩最近九局赢了六局，正逐渐掌握比赛的主动权。但安德烈开始冷静下来了。我看得出来，他已经重新找回了节奏，专注地投入比赛。他朝我们这边看了一眼，我注意到他的眼神平静而集中。其实用“全神贯注”来形容更贴切。

Andre wins 16 of the next 21 points and four straight games. He just really started racking the ball and dictating the points; waiting for the good opening. And he stops middle; makes the adjustment and starts anticipating middle for service returns that are huge. Just really sweet.

>安德烈接下来的21分中赢了16分，连得四局。他开始真正掌控球路，主导每一分，不再等待对方的主动进攻。他及时调整战术，开始预判对手的发球接发，尤其是那些威力巨大的回球。真是漂亮的一局。

Sweeter than the next night when Pete shows up in his own zone. His first serve of the match is clocked at 126 mph. That’s what you call coming out of the locker room ready to play. Pete said later it was the best he had ever played against Andre, and the win let him take over the number one spot at 12:01 a.m. February 19. But stay tuned, folks.

>比他第二天晚上出现在自己半场时还要甜。他这场比赛的第一发球时速达到了126英里。这可真是从更衣室出来就准备好比赛了。后来皮特说，这场比赛是他对阵阿加西打得最好的一仗，胜利也让他在19年2月19日凌晨12点01分登上了世界第一的宝座。但请各位继续关注。

## 5 Rock and Roll Tennis

>5 摇滚式网球

Andre was the wild child of tennis when he first came up ten years ago; almost like a rock star or something with the long hair, earrings, throwing his shirt into the crowd. A great showman.

>安德烈十年前初登赛场时，还像个不羁的野孩子；他长发飘飘，戴着耳环，把衬衫扔向观众席，活像一位摇滚明星。他是个天生的表演者。

None of that stuff mattered much to me. What got my attention was something that was really crazy as far as I was concerned. Namely, Andre wasn’t using his talent right. Anybody could see he had awesome abilities, but what I saw was a guy who didn’t capitalize on those abilities.

>我那些事情根本没怎么放在心上。真正引起我注意的是另一件事，对我来说那简直疯狂。具体来说，就是安德烈没有好好利用他的天赋。任何人都能看出来他拥有非凡的能力，但在我眼里，他却是个没有充分发挥自己潜能的人。

He sort of got away with it because he had so much talent that he was able to win matches in spite of himself. He also lost a lot of them he should have won.

>他之所以能侥幸过关，是因为他天赋异禀，即使自己表现不佳也能赢得比赛。不过，他也输掉了不少本该赢的比赛。

>[!note]- 该句通过对比强调天赋与实际表现之间的差距，翻译时保留了这种对比关系，使中文表达更自然。

Because he wasn’t thinking out on the court he was never consistent long term. Players he should have beat would beat him. Matches he should have won he lost. Now and then he’d put it together and reach perfection, like when he beat Ivanisevic in the ’92 Wimbledon finals to win his first Slam.

>因为他不在场上专注思考，所以从长远来看他从未保持稳定。本该击败的对手却击败了他，本该赢的比赛却输了。不过偶尔他能集中精力，达到完美状态，比如他在1992年温布尔登决赛中击败伊万塞维奇，赢得首个大满贯冠军的时候。

He got up to number three in the world at one point, but watching him play was almost painful for me at times because of this huge talent he was careless about. I’d see him make tremendous shots and then [[null|]]follow them up with a little drop shot from back of the baseline, almost like he was playing an exhibition and working to entertain the crowd.

>他曾一度跻身世界排名前三，但有时看他在场上比赛对我来说几乎是一种煎熬，因为他拥有如此惊人的天赋却毫不在意。我常常看到他打出一些惊人的击球，接着却从底线后方轻飘飘地打出一个小小的下旋球，仿佛他是在进行一场展览，刻意为了取悦观众而表演。

So over the years I had kept track of his game just like I kept track of anybody else on the tour. I knew what he was doing and had a good idea of what he should be doing if he wanted to win more. But I never in a million years thought about coaching him or anybody else.

>多年来，我就像记录巡回赛上其他任何选手一样，一直在关注他的比赛。我知道他在做什么，也大致明白如果他想赢得更多比赛，应该怎么做。但说实话，我这辈子都不曾想过要指导他，或者指导任何人。

## 6 How I Became Andre’s Coach

>6 我是如何成为阿涅的教练的

During the ’94 Lipton Championships in Key Biscayne, Andre and I were having dinner at a little Italian restaurant on Fisher Island. Since winning Wimbledon Andre had dropped to number thirty-two in the world because of a variety of things including a wrist injury that required surgery and a layoff. But the wrist wasn’t the real issue.

>1994年利普顿锦标赛期间，我和阿加西在凯布西奈岛上一家小意大利餐厅共进晚餐。自从赢得温网以来，阿加西的世界排名已经跌至第32位，原因包括手腕伤势需要手术治疗以及一段休赛期。但真正的问题并非手腕伤。

Somewhere between the spaghetti and the spumoni that night Andre asked me if I had any ideas that might improve his game. I thought he was just shooting the breeze, so I started telling him how he wasn’t playing to his potential; how he could use more strategizing during matches; how he was playing outside the court, back on his heels behind the baseline. He used to be inside the baseline when he hit groundstrokes and I told him that. He is the greatest player in the history of tennis for taking the ball on the rise, so it made no sense whatsoever to be standing behind the baseline and letting the ball drop.

>那天晚上，就在意大利面和甜点之间，安德烈问我有没有什么想法可以提高他的球技。我当时以为他只是随便聊聊，于是开始告诉他，他现在并没有发挥出自己的潜力；他在比赛中缺乏策略性；他打的是在底线外的球，总是退到底线后方去接球。以前他接底线球时都是站在底线内侧，我之前也这么告诉过他。他是网球历史上最伟大的球员之一，因为他擅长在球还在上升时就击球，所以站在底线后方让球落地，这简直毫无意义。

I also told him it seemed to me he was inconsistent from match to match; sometimes ready to go, but other times almost like he hadn’t thought about the match or his opponent. I told him he’d play loose against a player until he got himself in trouble and then try to dig himself out by going for huge winners from the baseline.

>我还告诉他，我觉得他在每场比赛之间表现都不太稳定；有时候准备得很充分，但其他时候又好像根本没有想过这场比赛或对手。我告诉他，他会在面对某个球员时打得比较随意，直到自己陷入困境，然后才试图通过底线大力抽击来扭转局势。

But really, I don’t think I told Andre anything he didn’t already know or feel. He’s smart, so what I was saying made sense with what he was thinking. It really happened that casually. He says, “Let’s work together when we can. Keep it loose, hit at tournaments we’re both playing and talk on the phone.” I don’t think the word “coach” was ever used and we didn’t even have a contract.

>但说实话，我觉得我并没有告诉安德烈什么他不知道或者没有感觉的事情。他很聪明，所以我所说的话和他当时的想法是契合的。那一切真的发生得那么随意。他说：“我们有时间的时候一起合作吧，保持轻松，参加我们都在打的赛事，然后电话联系。” 我觉得“教练”这个词从未被用过，我们甚至都没有签合同。

[[null|]]At first I kept playing my own schedule of tournaments and just let things progress like we had discussed over dinner; hitting at tournaments, phone calls. I told Andre that I didn’t expect to see any real results in tournaments for about five months because of his layoff. It turns out that was a good guess. Nothing happened at first and Andre’s results at the Italian, French, Wimbledon, and elsewhere were generally not too good.

Then, almost exactly five months after we talked at Fisher Island over plates of pasta, he went to Toronto and won the ’94 Canadian Open. Next stop: the U.S. Open, where Andre would go in unseeded because of his low ranking (number nineteen worldwide).

>然后，几乎就在我们于菲舍岛共进意面谈话后的五个月，他前往多伦多，赢得了1994年的加拿大公开赛。下一站是美国公开赛，安德烈因世界排名仅第十九位而未被种子排名，以非种子选手的身份出战。

## 7 The Magic of Believing

>7 相信的力量

I felt very strongly that Andre could win it all in New York because his game had really clicked in during the tournament in Canada. He was just thinking so well out on the court and I told him that he could go all the way in New York. He must have thought I was kidding because he said, “If I win you gotta shave off your body hair like I did at Wimbledon last year, OK?” I had a bad feeling I was going to be shaving my chest in about two weeks.

>我非常确信安德烈能在纽约赢得一切，因为他在加拿大举行的比赛中状态已经完全找到了。他在球场上思考得非常出色，我告诉他，他完全可以在纽约一路杀到底。他一定觉得我在开玩笑，因为他说：“如果我赢了，你得像我去年在温布尔登那样，把全身的毛都剃掉，行吗？”我当时就觉得，大概两周后我就会在剃须膏和剃刀之间度过一个难熬的夜晚。

Nevertheless, Andre had a huge task in front of him as the tournament unfolded. He had to play Wayne Ferreira, Michael Chang, Thomas Muster, Todd Martin, and, in the finals, Michael Stich. Andre beat them all.

>然而，安德烈在赛事进行过程中仍面临着巨大的挑战。他必须接连对阵韦恩·费雷拉、迈克尔·张、托马斯·穆斯特、托德·马丁，最终在决赛中迎战迈克尔·施蒂希。安德烈一一击败了他们。

Nobody in the history of the U.S. Open had won the way he had done it: the hard way, beating five seeded players en route to the championship. Watching him gain momentum and confidence, seeing his performance and mental focus go higher and higher was stunning. His concentration just got white hot.

>在美国公开赛的历史上，没有人曾以他这样的方式夺冠：通过艰难的历程，击败五位种子选手，最终赢得冠军。看着他逐渐积累势头与信心，目睹他的表现和心理专注度不断提升，令人惊叹。他的专注力简直达到了白热化的状态。

After that he started calling me “the Believer.” More important than that, I saw that Andre was a believer.

>之后他开始叫我“信徒”。更重要的是，我意识到阿伦德也是个信徒。

Two days after he won his first U.S. Open title I made good on my promise and went on national television where my wife, Kim, got out the razor and shaved off my chest hair. I pay my bets.

>在我赢得个人首个美国公开赛冠军两天后，我兑现了承诺，登上国家电视台。我的妻子金拿出剃须刀，为我剃掉了胸毛。我兑现了赌注。

## 8 [[null|]]Long-Term Thinking

>8 长期思维

But I wanted us both thinking beyond one tournament or title. I never felt the goal for Andre was to win another Grand Slam, but to win Grand Slam titles. That’s why the only thing I said to him when he came over to the friend’s box after beating Michael Stich in the ’94 U.S. Open final was, “Australia’s next.” Nike gave a party for everybody later that night at Il Vagabondo restaurant in Manhattan and I repeated that message again. Six months later he beat Pete to win the ’95 Australian Open.

>但我希望我们两个人的思考不止停留在一场比赛或一座冠军奖杯上。我从未觉得安德烈的目标是赢得另一座大满贯，而是赢得大满贯冠军头衔。因此，当他在1994年美国公开赛决赛击败米哈伊尔·施蒂希后，走向朋友席位时，我唯一对他说的话就是：“澳大利亚的下一站。” 那晚，耐克公司在曼哈顿的Il Vagabondo餐厅为所有人举办了派对，我又一次重复了这句话。六个月后，他击败皮特，赢得了1995年澳大利亚公开赛的冠军。

On April 10, 1995, Andre hit number one and stayed there for thirty weeks. Pete grabbed it back, Thomas Muster had it for a few days, and then Pete reclaimed it that Sunday night at the Sybase tournament. I think Pete and Andre are going to be fighting for the top spot for a while.

>1995年4月10日，阿伦德（Andre）首次登顶世界第一，并连续保持了30周。彼得（Pete）随后夺回了头衔，托马斯·穆斯特（Thomas Muster）短暂占据榜首几天后，彼得又在当周日晚上的施巴赛（Sybase tournament）中重新夺回了世界第一的位置。我认为，彼得和阿伦德之间争夺世界第一的较量还会持续一段时间。



# [[null|]][[null|]]19

>[[null|]][[null|]]19

# Andre Agassi on “Winning Ugly”

>安德烈·阿加西谈“以丑取胜”

“Winning Ugly” to me is being able to figure out how to win even when you’re not at your best; when things aren’t going your way and you’ve got to do more than just hit the ball hard. That’s one of the big things I’ve learned from Brad—how to win ugly when it’s necessary.

>对我而言，“赢得难看”意味着即使你不在最佳状态，也能想出办法赢；即使事情不顺，你也必须做比仅仅大力击球更多的事。这是我从布拉德那里学到的最重要的东西之一——在必要时如何“赢 得难看”。

Brad Gilbert is a great talker. In fact, he’s always talking. When I first met him on tour it really kind of bothered me because he talked so much; always saying something or trying to strike up a conversation with somebody. I’m wondering “Geez, how come this guy’s in everybody’s business? He’s talking all the time! ” But that’s Beej. He’s got an opinion on everybody and everything.

>布拉德·吉尔伯特是个爱说话的人。事实上，他总是喋喋不休。我第一次在巡回赛上见到他时，真的有点烦，因为他说话太多，总是在说这说那，或者试图和别人搭话。我当时心想：“天啊，这家伙怎么什么人都知道？他整天都在说个不停！”但这就是比奇（Beej）。他对每个人和每件事都有自己的看法。

Then we played Davis Cup together in Germany back in ’89 and I started to get to know him a little better. Plus, I met his wife, Kim, who’s a great person, and I’m thinking, “If she thinks he’s OK, maybe he is.” We had dinner together a few times over the next couple of months and I learned that even though Beej may talk a lot, when it comes to sports he usually knows what he’s talking about. Especially tennis.

>然后我们在1989年一起在德国参加了戴维斯杯，我开始更了解他一些。此外，我还见到了他的妻子金（Kim），她是个非常棒的人，我想：“如果她觉得他没问题，那他大概真的没问题。” 接下来的几个月里，我们几次一起吃晚饭，我了解到虽然比奇（Beej）说话很多，但谈到体育时，他通常都清楚自己在说什么。尤其是网球方面。

So, that’s how we got to know each other at first; hanging around before and after some Davis Cup matches and practice sessions, at other tournaments, a pizza once in a while. It was very casual, but gradually I got to know him better and we became friends. I’m glad we did.

>所以，我们最初是怎么认识的呢？就是在一些戴维斯杯比赛前后以及训练期间四处晃悠，参加其他赛事时也偶尔碰面，有时还会一起吃个披萨。那时的关系非常随意，但渐渐地，我对他有了更深的了解，我们成了朋友。我很庆幸我们成为了朋友。

## 1 [[null|]]Appreciating Brad’s “Ugly” Game

>1 欣赏布拉德的“难看”打法

What Beej has done for my game since we got together is unmistakable and nobody else could have done it because nobody knows the game better. There’s no question in my mind about that. He has a tremendous understanding of the whole deal; strategy, tactics, match dynamics, and how to apply them to beat players like Pete, Michael Chang, Boris, Courier, and others.

>自我们在一起以来，比奇（Beej）对我比赛的提升是显而易见的，而且没有人能做得更好，因为没人比他更了解这项运动。这一点在我心中毫无疑问。他对整个比赛的理解非常深刻，包括战略、战术、比赛节奏以及如何将这些运用到实际中，击败像皮特（Pete）、迈克尔·张（Michael Chang）、博里斯（Boris）、库尔 (Courier) 这样的选手。

He’s very scientific when it comes to analyzing opponents. Brad puts them under a microscope and can just see really clearly where their flaws are and where they’re strong; especially where the cracks in their game appear during big-point pressure, where it counts.

>他在分析对手时非常科学。布拉德会像显微镜一样细致地观察对手，能非常清楚地看到他们的弱点和优势，尤其是在关键分的压力下，对手技术上的漏洞往往会暴露无遗。

I first ran into that ability of his when I played him in tournaments. It was always a nerve-racking kind of experience, because he wouldn’t let you play your game.

>我第一次体会到他这种能力，是在参加比赛时与他交手的时候。那总是种令人紧张的经历，因为他从不让你打自己的球风。

In preparation for him you could never feel as comfortable as you could with other guys, because you knew somehow Brad would take you out of your rhythm; that he would jerk you around out there. You ended up playing his game instead of making him play your game.

>为了准备迎战他，你永远无法像和其他选手比赛时那样感到自在，因为你隐约知道布拉德会打乱你的节奏，他会把你搞得晕头转向。你最终是在打他的比赛，而不是让他来打你的比赛。

>[!note]- 译者注：
原文中“jerk you around”意为“让对手感到困惑、打乱其节奏”，此处采用意译，使中文表达更自然。

It was almost like taking a tennis lesson, because he was always coming up with surprises. You never knew when he was going to attack or when he wasn’t. You never knew when he was going to pound the ball or when he was going to massage it. What you did know was that he would never let you settle into a groove and get comfortable hitting your good stuff.

>这简直就像上一节网球课，因为他总是出其不意地制造惊喜。你永远不知道他什么时候会发动进攻，什么时候又会按兵不动；你永远猜不透他什么时候会大力轰球，什么时候又会轻柔地抚摸球。唯一可以确定的是，他绝不会让你进入舒服的节奏，打起你擅长的球来得心应手。

Playing him was very frustrating because he had this tremendous ability to exploit a player’s game; to just pick at a weakness until he would start to break you down. And in addition to having a great mind for tennis he also had heart. He just never gave up on a match.

>和他打球非常令人沮丧，因为他拥有非凡的能力，能够发现并利用对手技术上的漏洞，不断骚扰你的弱点，直到把你彻底击溃。除了拥有卓越的网球头脑，他还有颗坚韧的心。他从不轻易放弃一场比赛。

>[!note]- 译者注：
此处“heart”既指“斗志”也暗含“毅力”，在中文中用“坚韧的心”来传达其双重含义。

And if you were a rookie, forget it. Rookies were breakfast for Brad. I know because the first time I ever played him as a rookie I went on the court thinking “easy match” and came off the court thinking I’d just had a bad dream. It was ugly. If you were a rookie, you weren’t going to beat him.

>如果你是个菜鸟，那就算了吧。菜鸟在布拉德眼里就跟早餐一样简单。我之所以这么清楚，是因为我第一次以菜鸟身份跟他打球时，一上场就想着“这是一场轻松的比赛”，可下场后却觉得我刚刚做了一场噩梦。那场比赛糟透了。如果你是菜鸟，你根本不可能打败他。

By January of ’94 I had fallen from a world ranking of number three [[null|]]to number thirty-two. My days with Nick Bolletieri were behind me. I had spent some time with Pancho Segura. I thought maybe Brad and I should have a little talk.

>到了1994年1月，我的世界排名已经从第三位跌到了第三十二位。和尼克·波莱蒂里（Nick Bolletieri）共处的日子已经过去。我曾和潘乔·塞格拉（Pancho Segura）待了一段时间。我想，也许我和布拉德（Brad）应该好好谈一谈。

## 2 A Winning Formula

>2 一剂制胜良方

In Florida during the Lipton Championships in March of ’94 I asked him if he had any ideas about my game; what I could do to pick it up. You can guess he wasn’t at a loss for words. Right away he started telling me what I should be doing to win more. Everything he said was right. Mainly what he said was, “Think.”

>1994年3月，在佛罗里达举行的利普顿锦标赛期间，我问他有没有关于我打法的想法，我该怎么做才能进步。你可以猜到，他并不缺乏话语。他立刻开始告诉我该做些什么才能赢得更多比赛。他所说的一切都是对的。主要他说的是：“要思考。”

He told me, “If you play Sampras the same way you play Chang, you’ll lose. If you play Becker like you play Courier, you’ll lose. Each player is different. Your strategy should be different for each of them.” He’s right, and applying what he said has turned my game completely around.

>他告诉我：“如果你用对付张德培的方式去对付桑普拉斯，你就会输；如果你用对付库尔库奇的方式去对付贝克尔，你也会输。每个球员都不一样，你应对他们的策略也应该不同。”他说得对，按照他的话去做，我的球风完全改变了。

## 3 “Work a Plan”

>3 “按计划行事”

Brad believes that to win consistently at any level you’ve got to “work a plan.” That means during a match you’ve got to have a clear strategy for what you want to do and then work hard to force it on your opponent. That sounds simple, but the key to its success is having the right strategy or plan. And in order to accomplish that you need to do three things correctly. Here’s some basic Brad:

>布拉德认为，要在任何级别持续获胜，你都必须“执行计划”。这意味着在比赛中，你必须对想要采取的策略有清晰的计划，并且努力将其强加于对手。听起来似乎很简单，但成功的关键在于制定出正确的策略或计划。而为了做到这一点，你需要正确完成三件事。下面是一些布拉德的基本原则：

1\. Know your strengths and weaknesses.

>1\. 了解自己的优势与劣势。

2\. Understand your opponent’s strengths and weaknesses.

>2\. 了解对手的优势与劣势。

3\. Spend time thinking about “How can I put my strengths on my opponent’s weaknesses?”

>3\. 花时间思考“我该如何将自身的长处施加在对手的短处之上？”

I wasn’t doing it. Brad couldn’t believe it when I told him that sometimes I’d decide where I was going to hit my serve during the ball toss. [[null|]]For Brad, that was unacceptable because he was the kind of player who would think about where he was going to hit his serve two weeks before the toss.

>我当时并没有这么做。当我告诉布拉德，有时我在抛球时就已经决定要把发球打到哪里时，他简直难以置信。[[null|]] 对布拉德来说，这不可接受，因为他是一个会在抛球前两周就开始思考要把发球打到哪里的球员。

## 4 Strategizing

>4 策略制定

One of the first things he told me was I had to go in for the kill when I opened up the court. He felt I loved to pound the groundstrokes so much that even after I opened up the court by swinging a guy out wide to get a weak return I’d stay back to hit another hard one. Brad said, “When you get the chance to end a point you’ve got to take it. Go in for the kill.”

>他告诉我的第一件事就是，我打开球场时必须积极进攻。他认为我太喜欢大力击打底线球，即使我通过大力抽球把对手打到边线，迫使对方回球较弱，我也会退回到后场，再次大力击球。布拉德说：“当你有机会结束一分时，一定要抓住机会。积极进攻，一举击溃对手。”

He also got me thinking about my serve. Instead of just spinning it in, I started using it to help set up the point. In the ’94 U.S. Open final against Michael Stich, Brad and I discussed the need to mix up my serves so he wouldn’t be able to get comfortable, go on the offensive, and take the net. It worked. Straight sets for a Grand Slam title.

>他同时还让我重新思考我的发球。我不再只是把球发进去，而是开始利用发球来为整场比赛创造优势。在1994年美国公开赛决赛对阵迈克尔·施蒂希（Michael Stich）时，布拉德和我讨论了如何变换发球方式，让对手无法适应，无法进入进攻状态，无法上网抢攻。这个策略奏效了。我以直落三盘的战绩赢得了大满贯冠军。

## 5 Turning Weakness into Strength

>5 将弱点转化为力量

Now this may seem like fairly basic stuff, but it’s amazing how players limit their physical talents by not utilizing their mental talents. I know this was true for me. In fact, there’s no doubt that my mind used to be my weakness because I didn’t think I needed to use it. I thought I could just go out there and hit harder than the other guy.

>这听起来可能似乎很基础，但令人惊讶的是，许多球员正是由于没有充分发挥心理潜能，才限制了自己的身体天赋。我对此深有体会。事实上，我过去无疑就是被自己的思维所拖累，因为我根本没意识到自己需要动脑子。我总以为，只要走出去，把球打得比对方更用力就可以了。

Players knew that even when I was playing well, if they could just hang in there long enough I would probably give the match away. I really like the fact that I’ve proven to myself that you can take a weakness and turn it into a strength.

>球员们知道，即使我在比赛中表现不错，只要他们能坚持足够长的时间，我大概率就会输掉比赛。我真的很喜欢这一点，因为我证明了自己：你可以把一个弱点转化为一种力量。

I get the feeling now they know that even if I’m not playing great I’ll be on them so much that they may lose a little heart—like maybe it’s just not worth it.

>我现在感觉他们可能已经明白，即使我打得不好，我也会对他们施压，让他们有些动摇——比如，也许这样根本不值得。

>[!note]- 译者注：
此处“on them so much”意为“对他们施压”或“持续施加心理压力”，根据中文表达习惯进行意译。

I do feel that difference since Beej and I teamed up. Players give me [[null|]]that kind of respect and I can sense it. They know that I’m out there physically and mentally 100 percent until the last point.

>我确实感觉到和比吉合作后的变化。球员们对我表现出那种尊重，我能感受到。他们知道，我会在最后一分之前，百分之百地在身体和心理上全力以赴。

Another big thing is that Brad has played and beaten the same guys I’m facing out there. He knows from experience what they can or can’t do on the big points and he’s good at working a plan to match my game up against other guys.

>另一个重要因素是，布拉德曾和我面对的对手打过，并且击败过他们。他凭实战经验清楚这些人在关键分上能做什么、不能做什么，而且他擅长制定战术，让我能更好地应对其他对手。

## 6 Courier’s Cannon

>6 邮差的炮台

For example, with Jim Courier you know going in that his forehand is a cannon. But a lot of players fear it so much that they’ll try to get the ball to his backhand early in the point. I don’t do that now because I’ve learned that while Jim’s backhand is vulnerable, it’s a lot more vulnerable if he has to hit it on the move.

>例如，面对吉姆·科里尔时，你一开始就知道他的正手像一门大炮。但很多球员却因为太害怕他的正手，而试图在比赛中尽早将球打到他的反手位。我现在就不会这么做，因为我已经学到，虽然吉姆的反手本身就有弱点，但如果他必须在移动中击球，那他的反手就会更加脆弱。

So first I go to Jim’s strength on the forehand side, which opens up the court and exposes that running backhand. That’s when he’ll have a lot more trouble than if he sets up for it. You’ve somehow got to open up the court so you can make him hit that backhand while he’s moving. It’s almost a sure point if I can do that.

>首先，我先攻击吉姆正手一侧的优势位置，这样就能打开场地，暴露出他的跑动反手。这时他就会比他提前站位时更难应对。你必须想办法打开场地，让他在移动中击打反手。如果我能做到这一点，几乎就能赢得这一分。

Brad used to try the same thing as a player by swinging Jim wide off the deuce court with his serve. I do it on both the serve and with the groundstrokes early in the point.

>布雷德过去在比赛中也会这么做，他会用发球把吉姆打到反手位的二发区。我则在发球和接发球的早期阶段，无论正手还是反手都会这么做。

The plan I work is only slightly different from what everyone else is trying to do against Jim, but it uses my strength (groundstrokes) to set up his weakness, which is not the backhand, but the running backhand. And the reason it’s vulnerable is his grip. His hand is wrapped so far under the racket it looks almost like he’s swinging a baseball bat. It’s the kind of thing Brad likes to notice and exploit.

>我制定的战术与其他人针对吉姆所采取的策略略有不同，但它的核心是利用我的优势（底线击球）来针对他的弱点。他的弱点不是反手，而是“跑动反手”。之所以容易被攻破，是因为他的握拍方式。他的手紧紧地缠绕在球拍下方，看起来几乎像是在挥动棒球棒。这种细节正是布拉德喜欢注意到并加以利用的地方。

>[!note]- 译者注：
此处“running backhand”指跑动反手，是网球中的一种击球方式，球员在移动中击球，通常用于应对对手的正手击球。

## 7 Becker Is Too Stubborn

>7 贝克尔太固执了

My plan against Boris (Becker) is really almost no plan at all because of his stubbornness. Boris just plays me very straight up. He’s a very [[null|]]stubborn person and because of it stays at the baseline too much and tries to pound me. This plays right into my strength, groundstrokes.

>我对贝克尔（Boris Becker）的战术其实几乎没有任何计划，因为他太固执了。贝克尔打球非常直接，他是个非常固执的人，因此总是站在底线位置，试图用大力击球来压制我。这正好发挥了我的优势，那就是我的底线进攻。

It’s almost like Boris is so stubborn about what he believes he can do that it prevents him from accepting the fact that he can’t win doing it against me. It prevents him from trying something else. It’s very surprising. It’s kind of like Boris attacks my strength with his weakness. Against other guys you’ll see him mixing it up a lot. Against me he gets a little bit rattled. He waits a little too long for a short ball, and allows me to exploit his movement, which isn’t as good as Courier’s. His backhand becomes more of a weakness because of his lack of good foot speed.

>鲍里斯对自己能做什么有着近乎固执的信念，这种信念阻碍了他接受一个事实：他无法以这种方式战胜我。这还阻止了他尝试其他方法。这真的很令人惊讶。有点像鲍里斯用自身的弱点去攻击我的强项。面对其他选手时，你会看到他经常主动出击、变化多端。但面对我时，他就会有些慌乱。他对于短球的反应慢了一点，给了我机会利用他的移动能力不如库尔特（Courier）这一点。他的反手也因此变得更弱，因为他的移动速度不够快。

The other advantage I work is off his serve. I return serve well, which takes away Boris’s natural game.

>我另一个优势在于接发球。我的接发球技术不错，这能削弱鲍里斯的自然打法。

>[!note]- 译者注：
此处“natural game”意指对手在发球时通常会采用的、最擅长的打法，因此翻译为“自然打法”以传达原意。

Nevertheless, he is a very dangerous player. In the semis at Wimbledon in ’95 I was up 6–2, 4–1 and playing absolutely perfect tennis. In fact, Boris said later my play in the first set was the absolute best tennis he had ever faced anywhere.

>然而，他确实是一个非常危险的对手。1995年温布尔登半决赛中，我一度以6–2、4–1领先，而且打得非常完美。事实上，鲍里斯后来告诉我，我第一盘的表现是他职业生涯中见过的最出色的网球。

But at Wimbledon Boris is never dead, and he came back to life in the sixth game of the second set after a fantastic point that he managed to win. That single point convinced him that he could win the match. At the same time I had gone on cruise control and had mentally started thinking about the finals against Pete. Boris won in four sets, 2–6, 7–6, 6–4, 7–6. We met up again two months later at the U.S. Open with a better result for me.

>但在温布尔登，鲍里斯永远都不会死，他在第二盘第六局的一次精彩得分后重新振作起来。那一分让他确信自己能够赢得整场比赛。与此同时，我却已经进入自动驾驶模式，开始在脑海中想象与皮特在决赛中对决的场景。鲍里斯以四盘险胜，比分是2–6、7–6、6–4、7–6。两个月后，我们在美国公开赛再次相遇，这次我的成绩更好。

## 8 Pete Is Nearly Perfect

>8 彼特几乎完美

Pete (Sampras) is the most talented player on tour. He does everything well and just doesn’t have any really notable weaknesses. However, the one thing that maybe isn’t as strong as everything else is Pete’s return of serve. He likes to float that return back and then work himself into the point.

>皮特（萨姆普拉斯）是巡回赛中天赋最高的球员之一。他几乎样样都做得很好，几乎没有什么明显的弱点。然而，也许唯一不如其他方面突出的就是他的接发球。他喜欢把接发球轻轻打回去，然后慢慢把比分拉入延长赛。

>[!note]- 译者注：
此处“work himself into the point”意为通过耐心的击球将比赛拖入更长的回合，译为“慢慢把比分拉入延长赛”既保留原意，又符合中文表达习惯。

Brad emphasized that this is where you’ve got to hurt him quickly. I work on serving big into Pete to get the floater return or the shorter return and then try to hurt him right away. You can’t wait for him to [[null|]]get back in the point. He can work that transition game, going from defense to offense, better than anybody in the world. You think you’ve got him on the defense and all of a sudden he explodes on one ball and he’s on the offense.

>布拉德强调，这就是你要迅速给他施压的地方。我通常会用大角度发球打 Pete，迫使他回球不到位，要么是高球，要么是短球，然后立刻给他施压。你不能等他重新回到球拍前再出手。他擅长在比赛中转换攻防节奏，从防守转为进攻，全世界没有人比他更擅长了。你以为他已经进入防守状态，可突然之间，他就在一个球上发动反击，转眼就变成了进攻方。

So with Pete you’ve got to make him pay for every shorter ball that he gives you. In the past I wouldn’t be that aggressive in capitalizing on the shorter ball opportunity. Brad made a living out of looking for the slightest opening. Now he’s got me thinking more like that; looking to appreciate and exploit opportunities.

>所以面对皮特，你必须让他为每一次他给你发的短球付出代价。过去我不会那么积极地去抓住短球带来的机会。布拉德靠的就是寻找哪怕最细微的破绽来取胜。现在他让我开始用这种方式思考，更加注重发现并利用这些机会。

Of course, Pete’s serve is something else. Even if you’re leaning the right way he can beat you with it. You just have to hope you can guess right a lot or that he isn’t on it. And if his first serve is missing, I’ve got to jump on his second serve. Brad also gets me to look for Pete to serve the wide slice to my forehand in the deuce court under pressure. He likes to hit it and then come in behind it with an approach shot. Same on the ad court; under pressure Pete likes to hit up the middle and come in behind it.

>当然，皮特的发球另有一套。即使你站位正确，他也能用发球击败你。你唯一能做的，就是希望你猜对他的发球方向，或者他不在状态。如果他的第一发球出界，我必须立刻去接他的第二发球。布拉德还教我，当处于压力下时，要留意皮特向我的正手位大角度发球。他喜欢这样发球，然后用切入球跟进。同样的情况也适用于反手位：在压力之下，皮特倾向于把球打向中路，然后跟进切入。

The other element of Pete’s game that you’ve really got to respect is his running forehand both on the baseline and when he approaches behind it. It’s a major weapon, but you can’t respect it so much that you never go to it and just let him hit forehands from the backhand side. You’ll see him camp about three feet to the left of the center of the baseline. Brad keeps telling me, “Get Pete off his pitcher’s mound. Get him off that spot.” So I try to work a point where he’s not always hitting from his favorite spot on the court, which means going to his strength.

>皮特的比赛中，你真正需要敬重的另一个元素是他的跑动正手击球，无论是底线位置还是他靠近网前时。这是一种强大的武器，但你不能敬重到不敢使用，从而让他一直从反手位击正手球。你会看到他站在底线中心左侧大约三英尺的位置。布拉德总是对我说：“把皮特从他的投手丘上‘请’下来，让他离开那个位置。”因此，我总是试图在比赛中让他不总从他最喜欢的场地位置击球，也就是不总是使用他的强项。

Brad and I talk a lot about that mixture of exposing a player’s weakness or vulnerability by going at his strength. It’s a delicate balance sometimes, but it’s the key to cracking open a match. It works. I beat Pete in the finals of the Lipton Championships, Australian Open, and Canadian Open in ’95. But it doesn’t work all the time. He got me in the finals of the U.S. Open that same year. But as I’ve said before, when Pete’s at 100 percent of his game and I’m at 90 percent of my game, it feels like I’m at about 60 percent.

>布拉德和我经常讨论一种策略，那就是利用对手的弱点或漏洞，去攻击他的强项。这有时候需要非常微妙的平衡，但这是打开一场比赛的关键。这个方法是有效的。我在“利普顿锦标赛”、澳大利亚公开赛和加拿大公开赛的决赛中都击败了皮特，那是在1995年。但并不是每次都能奏效。同年美国公开赛的决赛中，皮特就击败了我。但正如我之前所说，当皮特发挥到100%的时候，而我只能发挥到90%的时候，我感觉就像是只发挥了60%的水平。

Basically, when we play he’s going to hold serve and I’m going to [[null|]]beat him from the ground. The question is, do I break him or does he beat me on the ground? The one who can do it usually wins.

>基本上，我们打球的时候，他会在发球局稳住，而我则会从底线一路追击把他打败。问题是，究竟是我突破他的防线，还是他在我底线前把我击败？通常来说，能做到这一点的人往往能赢得比赛。

## 9 Challenging Chang

>9 挑战张江

Of all the top ten players, if I’m playing my game I prefer to play Michael Chang over anybody else. That’s because I match up well against Chang. By that I mean I feel the only thing he does out on the court better than me is move. So, if I’m on top of my game he should have no say-so in the match. I just have more firepower than he does. It’s like two heavyweight boxers going toe to toe and one of them weighs 25 pounds more than the other one. I should win.

>在所有前十名的球员中，如果我打得出色，我更愿意和迈克尔·张对打，而不是其他任何人。这是因为我和张的对决非常匹配。我的意思是我感觉他在场上唯一比我强的地方就是移动能力。所以，如果我发挥得当，他就没有机会赢。我拥有的力量比他强。这就像两名重量级拳击手正面对面较量，其中一人比另一人重25磅。我应该赢。

What I do against Michael is attack his strength, which is speed. In fact, I look forward to his speed, because if he gets to one ball he’ll have to run to another one. I want him to get to the ball. My mentality is, “I can hit these balls side to side a lot longer than Michael can run side to side.” Basically, my strength is bigger than Michael’s. What I have to be is patient. Brad’s got me a lot better at being patient.

>我在对战迈克尔时，是攻击他的优势，也就是他的速度。事实上，我甚至期待他的速度，因为如果他接到一个球，就必须跑到另一个球的位置。我希望他能接到球。我的心态是：“我能在左右来回击球的时间比迈克尔能左右奔跑的时间长得多。”说白了，我的体力比他强。我需要做的就是保持耐心。布拉德让我在这方面进步了很多。

>[!note]- 译者注：
此处“Brad”可能指教练或队友，根据上下文可译为“布拉德”或“布拉德教练”，此处保留原名。

## 10 Overcoming My Own Mistakes

>10 克服自己的失误

In the ’95 Australian Open finals against Pete in the first set I was holding serve easily and had three chances to break him but didn’t. Then I was serving at 4–5 and the game and set suddenly just sort of slipped away. Pete hits a good shot. I make one error. Suddenly it’s love–30. Then 15–40. And then I double-fault to give away the set! A double fault to lose the first set of a Slam? It was unbelievable.

>1995年澳网决赛第一盘对阵皮特时，我轻松保住了发球局，并且有三次机会破发却都没能成功。之后我发球局在4-5的情况下，比赛和整盘突然就莫名其妙地失去了控制。皮特打出一个好球，我犯了一个错误，突然间比分变成0-30，接着是15-40，然后我双误，直接输掉这一盘！在大满贯赛事的第一盘就因为双误而失利？这简直难以置信。

Believe me, I was upset; really angered because I should have won the set at least twice and instead lost it with a double fault. I was very angry.

>相信我，我当时真的很生气，真的被激怒了，因为我本至少能赢下两盘，结果却因为双误而输掉了这一盘。我非常愤怒。

But here’s the difference. With Brad I’ve worked to turn that emotion, that anger, into a positive direction instead of negative. Instead of getting me down that anger can drive me to another level. I feel like I [[null|]]get a shot of adrenaline. Almost like I go into a full court press in basketball.

>但这里有个区别。在和布拉德合作的过程中，我努力将那种情绪、那种愤怒，转化为积极的力量，而不是消极的。与其让我情绪低落，这种愤怒反而能推动我达到另一个层次。我感觉就像打了一针肾上腺素。几乎就像在篮球比赛中进入全面施压状态。

Against Pete in that second set in the Australian finals I just came out very intense; going after every rebound, the full court press, on him all over the court. I was fighting for everything and all of a sudden it’s 6–1 and I’m back in the match. Not only back in the match. I won it in four.

>在澳大利亚决赛第二盘对阵皮特的时候，我一上来就非常投入，全力以赴地追每一个球，全场紧逼，把他逼得满场跑动。我为每一分拼尽全力，突然之间比分变成6比1，我重新回到了比赛中。不仅仅是我回到了比赛，我甚至以四盘赢得了整场比赛。

## 11 Winning Ugly

>11 赢得难看

One of the biggest things I’ve gotten from Brad is how to stay in a match when things aren’t going my way. He believes that 5 percent of the time your opponent is in the zone and you won’t win; 5 percent of the time you’re in the zone and can’t lose. But the other 90 percent of the time it’s up for grabs; there is a way to win. You’ve just got to figure out what it is. And to do that you’ve got to stay positive. You’ve got to believe. I’ve been doing that a lot better these days, thanks to Brad.

>从布拉德身上，我学到最大的一点，是如何在局面不利时依然保持专注，打好比赛。他认为，有5%的时间，对手处于最佳状态，你很难赢；5%的时间，你处于最佳状态，几乎不可能输。但剩下的90%的时间里，胜负还未确定，只要找到正确的方法，你依然有机会赢。你只需要弄清楚该怎么做。要做到这一点，你必须保持积极的心态，你必须相信自己。如今，我在这方面做得比以前好多了，这都要感谢布拉德。
